10148{_}581{_}Then all sat down to the woodland feast and talked among themselves of the merry jest that had been played upon the Sheriff, and of the adventures that had befallen each member of the band in his disguise.
10148{_}582{_}But when the feast was done, Robin Hood took Little John apart and said, "Truly am I vexed in my blood, for I heard the Sheriff say today, 'Thou shootest better than that coward knave Robin Hood, that dared not show his face here this day.'
10148{_}583{_}I would fain let him know who it was who won the golden arrow from out his hand, and also that I am no coward such as he takes me to be."
10148{_}584{_}Then Little John said, "Good master, take thou me and Will Stutely, and we will send yon fat Sheriff news of all this by a messenger such as he doth not expect."
10148{_}585{_}That day the Sheriff sat at meat in the great hall of his house at Nottingham Town.
10148{_}586{_}Long tables stood down the hall, at which sat men-at- arms and household servants and good stout villains,[Bond-servants.]
10148{_}587{_}in all fourscore and more.
10148{_}588{_}There they talked of the day's shooting as they ate their meat and quaffed their ale.
10148{_}589{_}The Sheriff sat at the head of the table upon a raised seat under a canopy, and beside him sat his dame.
10148{_}590{_}"By my troth," said he, "I did reckon full roundly that that knave Robin Hood would be at the game today.
10148{_}591{_}I did not think that he was such a coward.
10148{_}592{_}But who could that saucy knave be who answered me to my beard so bravely?
10148{_}593{_}I wonder that I did not have him beaten; but there was something about him that spoke of other things than rags and tatters."
10148{_}594{_}Then, even as he finished speaking, something fell rattling among the dishes on the table, while those that sat near started up wondering what it might be.
10148{_}595{_}After a while one of the men-at-arms gathered courage enough to pick it up and bring it to the Sheriff.
10148{_}596{_}Then everyone saw that it was a blunted gray goose shaft, with a fine scroll, about the thickness of a goose quill, tied near to its head.
10148{_}597{_}The Sheriff opened the scroll and glanced at it, while the veins upon his forehead swelled and his cheeks grew ruddy with rage as he read, for this was what he saw:       "_Now Heaven bless Thy Grace this day      Say all in sweet Sherwood      For thou didst give the prize away      To merry Robin Hood_."
10148{_}598{_}"Whence came this?"
10148{_}599{_}cried the Sheriff in a mighty voice.
10148{_}600{_}"Even through the window, Your Worship," quoth the man who had handed the shaft to him.
10148{_}601{_}Will Stutely Rescued by His Companions  NOW WHEN THE SHERIFF found that neither law nor guile could overcome Robin Hood, he was much perplexed, and said to himself, "Fool that I am!
10148{_}602{_}Had I not told our King of Robin Hood, I would not have gotten myself into such a coil; but now I must either take him captive or have wrath visited upon my head from his most gracious Majesty.
10148{_}603{_}I have tried law, and I have tried guile, and I have failed in both; so I will try what may be done with might."
10148{_}604{_}Thus communing within himself, he called his constables together and told them what was in his mind.
10148{_}605{_}"Now take ye each four men, all armed in proof," said he, "and get ye gone to the forest, at different points, and lie in wait for this same Robin Hood.
10148{_}606{_}But if any constable finds too many men against him, let him sound a horn, and then let each band within hearing come with all speed and join the party that calls them.
10148{_}607{_}Thus, I think, shall we take this green-clad knave.
10148{_}608{_}Furthermore, to him that first meeteth with Robin Hood shall one hundred pounds of silver money be given, if he be brought to me dead or alive; and to him that meeteth with any of his band shall twoscore pounds be given, if such be brought to me dead or alive.
10148{_}609{_}So, be ye bold and be ye crafty."
10148{_}610{_}So thus they went in threescore companies of five to Sherwood Forest, to take Robin Hood, each constable wishing that he might be the one to find the bold outlaw, or at least one of his band.
10148{_}611{_}For seven days and nights they hunted through the forest glades, but never saw so much as a single man in Lincoln green; for tidings of all this had been brought to Robin Hood by trusty Eadom o' the Blue Boar.
10148{_}612{_}When he first heard the news, Robin said, "If the Sheriff dare send force to meet force, woe will it be for him and many a better man besides, for blood will flow and there will be great trouble for all.
10148{_}613{_}But fain would I shun blood and battle, and fain would I not deal sorrow to womenfolk and wives because good stout yeomen lose their lives.
10148{_}614{_}Once I slew a man, and never do I wish to slay a man again, for it is bitter for the soul to think thereon.
10148{_}615{_}So now we will abide silently in Sherwood Forest, so that it may be well for all, but should we be forced to defend ourselves, or any of our band, then let each man draw bow and brand with might and main."
10148{_}616{_}At this speech many of the band shook their heads, and said to themselves, "Now the Sheriff will think that we are cowards, and folk will scoff throughout the countryside, saying that we fear to meet these men."
10148{_}617{_}But they said nothing aloud, swallowing their words and doing as Robin bade them.
10148{_}618{_}Thus they hid in the depths of Sherwood Forest for seven days and seven nights and never showed their faces abroad in all that time; but early in the morning of the eighth day Robin Hood called the band together and said, "Now who will go and find what the Sheriff's men are at by this time?
10148{_}619{_}For I know right well they will not bide forever within Sherwood shades."
10148{_}620{_}At this a great shout arose, and each man waved his bow aloft and cried that he might be the one to go.
10148{_}621{_}Then Robin Hood's heart was proud when he looked around on his stout, brave fellows, and he said, "Brave and true are ye all, my merry men, and a right stout band of good fellows are ye, but ye cannot all go, so I will choose one from among you, and it shall be good Will Stutely, for he is as sly as e'er an old dog fox in Sherwood Forest."
10148{_}622{_}Then Will Stutely leaped high aloft and laughed loudly, clapping his hands for pure joy that he should have been chosen from among them all.
10148{_}623{_}"Now thanks, good master," quoth he, "and if I bring not news of those knaves to thee, call me no more thy sly Will Stutely."
10148{_}624{_}Then he clad himself in a friar's gown, and underneath the robe he hung a good broadsword in such a place that he could easily lay hands upon it.
10148{_}625{_}Thus clad, he set forth upon his quest, until he came to the verge of the forest, and so to the highway.
10148{_}626{_}He saw two bands of the Sheriff's men, yet he turned neither to the right nor the left, but only drew his cowl the closer over his face, folding his hands as if in meditation.
10148{_}627{_}So at last he came to the Sign of the Blue Boar.
10148{_}628{_}"For," quoth he to himself, "our good friend Eadom will tell me all the news."
10148{_}629{_}At the Sign of the Blue Boar he found a band of the Sheriffs men drinking right lustily; so, without speaking to anyone, he sat down upon a distant bench, his staff in his hand, and his head bowed forward as though he were meditating.
10148{_}630{_}Thus he sat waiting until he might see the landlord apart, and Eadom did not know him, but thought him to be some poor tired friar, so he let him sit without saying a word to him or molesting him, though he liked not the cloth.
10148{_}631{_}"For," said he to himself, "it is a hard heart that kicks the lame dog from off the sill."
10148{_}632{_}As Stutely sat thus, there came a great house cat and rubbed against his knee, raising his robe a palm's-breadth high.
10148{_}633{_}Stutely pushed his robe quickly down again, but the constable who commanded the Sheriffs men saw what had passed, and saw also fair Lincoln green beneath the friar's robe.
10148{_}634{_}He said nothing at the time, but communed within himself in this wise: "Yon is no friar of orders gray, and also, I wot, no honest yeoman goeth about in priest's garb, nor doth a thief go so for nought.
10148{_}635{_}Now I think in good sooth that is one of Robin Hood's own men."
10148{_}636{_}So, presently, he said aloud, "O holy father, wilt thou not take a good pot of March beer to slake thy thirsty soul withal?"
10148{_}637{_}But Stutely shook his head silently, for he said to himself, "Maybe there be those here who know my voice."
10148{_}638{_}Then the constable said again, "Whither goest thou, holy friar, upon this hot summer's day?"
10148{_}639{_}"I go a pilgrim to Canterbury Town," answered Will Stutely, speaking gruffly, so that none might know his voice.
10148{_}640{_}Then the constable said, for the third time, "Now tell me, holy father, do pilgrims to Canterbury wear good Lincoln green beneath their robes?
10148{_}641{_}Ha!
10148{_}642{_}By my faith, I take thee to be some lusty thief, and perhaps one of Robin Hood's own band!
10148{_}643{_}Now, by Our Lady's grace, if thou movest hand or foot, I will run thee through the body with my sword!"
10148{_}644{_}Then he flashed forth his bright sword and leaped upon Will Stutely, thinking he would take him unaware; but Stutely had his own sword tightly held in his hand, beneath his robe, so he drew it forth before the constable came upon him.
10148{_}645{_}Then the stout constable struck a mighty blow; but he struck no more in all that fight, for Stutely, parrying the blow right deftly, smote the constable back again with all his might.
10148{_}646{_}Then he would have escaped, but could not, for the other, all dizzy with the wound and with the flowing blood, seized him by the knees with his arms even as he reeled and fell.
10148{_}647{_}Then the others rushed upon him, and Stutely struck again at another of the Sheriff's men, but the steel cap glanced the blow, and though the blade bit deep, it did not kill.
10148{_}648{_}Meanwhile, the constable, fainting as he was, drew Stutely downward, and the others, seeing the yeoman hampered so, rushed upon him again, and one smote him a blow upon the crown so that the blood ran down his face and blinded him.
10148{_}649{_}Then, staggering, he fell, and all sprang upon him, though he struggled so manfully that they could hardly hold him fast.
10148{_}650{_}Then they bound him with stout hempen cords so that he could not move either hand or foot, and thus they overcame him.
10148{_}651{_}Robin Hood stood under the greenwood tree, thinking of Will Stutely and how he might be faring, when suddenly he saw two of his stout yeomen come running down the forest path, and betwixt them ran buxom Maken of the Blue Boar.
10148{_}652{_}Then Robin's heart fell, for he knew they were the bearers of ill tidings.
10148{_}653{_}"Will Stutely hath been taken," cried they, when they had come to where he stood.
10148{_}654{_}"And is it thou that hast brought such doleful news?"
10148{_}655{_}said Robin to the lass.
10148{_}656{_}"Ay, marry, for I saw it all," cried she, panting as the hare pants when it has escaped the hounds, "and I fear he is wounded sore, for one smote him main shrewdly i' the crown.
10148{_}657{_}They have bound him and taken him to Nottingham Town, and ere I left the Blue Boar I heard that he should be hanged tomorrow day."
10148{_}658{_}"He shall not be hanged tomorrow day," cried Robin; "or, if he be, full many a one shall gnaw the sod, and many shall have cause to cry Alack-a- day!"
10148{_}659{_}Then he clapped his horn to his lips and blew three blasts right loudly, and presently his good yeomen came running through the greenwood until sevenscore bold blades were gathered around him.
10148{_}660{_}"Now hark you all!"
10148{_}661{_}cried Robin.
10148{_}662{_}"Our dear companion Will Stutely hath been taken by that vile Sheriff's men, therefore doth it behoove us to take bow and brand in hand to bring him off again; for I wot that we ought to risk life and limb for him, as he hath risked life and limb for us.
10148{_}663{_}Is it not so, my merry men all?"
10148{_}664{_}Then all cried, "Ay!"
10148{_}665{_}with a great voice.
10148{_}666{_}So the next day they all wended their way from Sherwood Forest, but by different paths, for it behooved them to be very crafty; so the band separated into parties of twos and threes, which were all to meet again in a tangled dell that lay near to Nottingham Town.
10148{_}667{_}Then, when they had all gathered together at the place of meeting, Robin spoke to them thus:  "Now we will lie here in ambush until we can get news, for it doth behoove us to be cunning and wary if we would bring our friend Will Stutely off from the Sheriff's clutches."
10148{_}668{_}So they lay hidden a long time, until the sun stood high in the sky.
10148{_}669{_}The day was warm and the dusty road was bare of travelers, except an aged palmer who walked slowly along the highroad that led close beside the gray castle wall of Nottingham Town.
10148{_}670{_}When Robin saw that no other wayfarer was within sight, he called young David of Doncaster, who was a shrewd man for his years, and said to him, "Now get thee forth, young David, and speak to yonder palmer that walks beside the town wall, for he hath come but now from Nottingham Town, and may tell thee news of good Stutely, perchance."
10148{_}671{_}So David strode forth, and when he came up to the pilgrim, he saluted him and said, "Good morrow, holy father, and canst thou tell me when Will Stutely will be hanged upon the gallows tree?
10148{_}672{_}I fain would not miss the sight, for I have come from afar to see so sturdy a rogue hanged."
10148{_}673{_}"Now, out upon thee, young man," cried the Palmer, "that thou shouldst speak so when a good stout man is to be hanged for nothing but guarding his own life!"
10148{_}674{_}And he struck his staff upon the ground in anger.
10148{_}675{_}"Alas, say I, that this thing should be!
10148{_}676{_}For even this day, toward evening, when the sun falleth low, he shall be hanged, fourscore rods from the great town gate of Nottingham, where three roads meet; for there the Sheriff sweareth he shall die as a warning to all outlaws in Nottinghamshire.
10148{_}677{_}But yet, I say again, Alas!
10148{_}678{_}For, though Robin Hood and his band may be outlaws, yet he taketh only from the rich and the strong and the dishonest man, while there is not a poor widow nor a peasant with many children, nigh to Sherwood, but has barley flour enough all the year long through him.
10148{_}679{_}It grieves my heart to see one as gallant as this Stutely die, for I have been a good Saxon yeoman in my day, ere I turned palmer, and well I know a stout hand and one that smiteth shrewdly at a cruel Norman or a proud abbot with fat moneybags.
10148{_}680{_}Had good Stutely's master but known how his man was compassed about with perils, perchance he might send succor to bring him out of the hand of his enemies.
10148{_}681{_}"Ay, marry, that is true," cried the young man.
10148{_}682{_}"If Robin and his men be nigh this place, I wot right well they will strive to bring him forth from his peril.
10148{_}683{_}But fare thee well, thou good old man, and believe me, if Will Stutely die, he shall be right well avenged."
10148{_}684{_}Then he turned and strode rapidly away; but the Palmer looked after him, muttering, "I wot that youth is no country hind that hath come to see a good man die.
10148{_}685{_}Well, well, perchance Robin Hood is not so far away but that there will be stout doings this day."
10148{_}686{_}So he went upon his way, muttering to himself.
10148{_}687{_}When David of Doncaster told Robin Hood what the Palmer had said to him, Robin called the band around him and spoke to them thus:  "Now let us get straightway into Nottingham Town and mix ourselves with the people there; but keep ye one another in sight, pressing as near the prisoner and his guards as ye can, when they come outside the walls.
10148{_}688{_}Strike no man without need, for I would fain avoid bloodshed, but if ye do strike, strike hard, and see that there be no need to strike again.
10148{_}689{_}Then keep all together until we come again to Sherwood, and let no man leave his fellows."
10148{_}690{_}The sun was low in the western sky when a bugle note sounded from the castle wall.
10148{_}691{_}Then all was bustle in Nottingham Town and crowds filled the streets, for all knew that the famous Will Stutely was to be hanged that day.
10148{_}692{_}Presently the castle gates opened wide and a great array of men-at-arms came forth with noise and clatter, the Sheriff, all clad in shining mail of linked chain, riding at their head.
10148{_}693{_}In the midst of all the guard, in a cart, with a halter about his neck, rode Will Stutely.
10148{_}694{_}His face was pale with his wound and with loss of blood, like the moon in broad daylight, and his fair hair was clotted in points upon his forehead, where the blood had hardened.
10148{_}695{_}When he came forth from the castle he looked up and he looked down, but though he saw some faces that showed pity and some that showed friendliness, he saw none that he knew.
10148{_}696{_}Then his heart sank within him like a plummet of lead, but nevertheless he spoke up boldly.
10148{_}697{_}"Give a sword into my hand, Sir Sheriff," said he, "and wounded man though I be, I will fight thee and all thy men till life and strength be gone."
10148{_}698{_}"Nay, thou naughty varlet," quoth the Sheriff, turning his head and looking right grimly upon Will Stutely, "thou shalt have no sword but shall die a mean death, as beseemeth a vile thief like thee."
10148{_}699{_}"Then do but untie my hands and I will fight thee and thy men with no weapon but only my naked fists.
10148{_}700{_}I crave no weapon, but let me not be meanly hanged this day."
10148{_}701{_}Then the Sheriff laughed aloud.
10148{_}702{_}"Why, how now," quoth he, "is thy proud stomach quailing?
10148{_}703{_}Shrive thyself, thou vile knave, for I mean that thou shalt hang this day, and that where three roads meet, so that all men shall see thee hang, for carrion crows and daws to peck at."
10148{_}704{_}"O thou dastard heart!"
10148{_}705{_}cried Will Stutely, gnashing his teeth at the Sheriff.
10148{_}706{_}"Thou coward hind!
10148{_}707{_}If ever my good master meet thee thou shalt pay dearly for this day's work!
10148{_}708{_}He doth scorn thee, and so do all brave hearts.
10148{_}709{_}Knowest thou not that thou and thy name are jests upon the lips of every brave yeoman?
10148{_}710{_}Such a one as thou art, thou wretched craven, will never be able to subdue bold Robin Hood."
10148{_}711{_}"Ha!"
10148{_}712{_}cried the Sheriff in a rage, "is it even so?
10148{_}713{_}Am I a jest with thy master, as thou callest him?
10148{_}714{_}Now I will make a jest of thee and a sorry jest withal, for I will quarter thee limb from limb, after thou art hanged."
10148{_}715{_}Then he spurred his horse forward and said no more to Stutely.
10148{_}716{_}At last they came to the great town gate, through which Stutely saw the fair country beyond, with hills and dales all clothed in verdure, and far away the dusky line of Sherwood's skirts.
10148{_}717{_}Then when he saw the slanting sunlight lying on field and fallow, shining redly here and there on cot and farmhouse, and when he heard the sweet birds singing their vespers, and the sheep bleating upon the hillside, and beheld the swallows flying in the bright air, there came a great fullness to his heart so that all things blurred to his sight through salt tears, and he bowed his head lest the folk should think him unmanly when they saw the tears in his eyes.
10148{_}718{_}Thus he kept his head bowed till they had passed through the gate and were outside the walls of the town.
10148{_}719{_}But when he looked up again he felt his heart leap within him and then stand still for pure joy, for he saw the face of one of his own dear companions of merry Sherwood; then glancing quickly around he saw well-known faces upon all sides of him, crowding closely upon the men-at-arms who were guarding him.
10148{_}720{_}Then of a sudden the blood sprang to his cheeks, for he saw for a moment his own good master in the press and, seeing him, knew that Robin Hood and all his band were there.
10148{_}721{_}Yet betwixt him and them was a line of men-at-arms.
10148{_}722{_}"Now, stand back!"
10148{_}723{_}cried the Sheriff in a mighty voice, for the crowd pressed around on all sides.
10148{_}724{_}"What mean ye, varlets, that ye push upon us so?
10148{_}725{_}Stand back, I say!"
10148{_}726{_}Then came a bustle and a noise, and one strove to push between the men- at-arms so as to reach the cart, and Stutely saw that it was Little John that made all that stir.
10148{_}727{_}"Now stand thou back!"
10148{_}728{_}cried one of the men-at-arms whom Little John pushed with his elbows.
10148{_}729{_}"Now stand thou back thine own self," quoth Little John, and straightway smote the man a buffet beside his head that felled him as a butcher fells an ox, and then he leaped to the cart where Stutely sat.
10148{_}730{_}"I pray thee take leave of thy friends ere thou diest, Will," quoth he, "or maybe I will die with thee if thou must die, for I could never have better company."
10148{_}731{_}Then with one stroke he cut the bonds that bound the other's arms and legs, and Stutely leaped straightway from the cart.
10148{_}732{_}"Now as I live," cried the Sheriff, "yon varlet I know right well is a sturdy rebel!
10148{_}733{_}Take him, I bid you all, and let him not go!"
10148{_}734{_}So saying, he spurred his horse upon Little John, and rising in his stirrups smote with might and main, but Little John ducked quickly underneath the horse's belly and the blow whistled harmlessly over his head.
10148{_}735{_}"Nay, good Sir Sheriff," cried he, leaping up again when the blow had passed, "I must e'en borrow thy most worshipful sword."
10148{_}736{_}Thereupon he twitched the weapon deftly from out the Sheriff's hand, "Here, Stutely," he cried, "the Sheriff hath lent thee his sword!
10148{_}737{_}Back to back with me, man, and defend thyself, for help is nigh!"
10148{_}738{_}"Down with them!"
10148{_}739{_}bellowed the Sheriff in a voice like an angry bull; and he spurred his horse upon the two who now stood back to back, forgetting in his rage that he had no weapon with which to defend himself.
10148{_}740{_}"Stand back, Sheriff!"
10148{_}741{_}cried Little John; and even as he spoke, a bugle horn sounded shrilly and a clothyard shaft whistled within an inch of the Sheriff's head.
10148{_}742{_}Then came a swaying hither and thither, and oaths, cries, and groans, and clashing of steel, and swords flashed in the setting sun, and a score of arrows whistled through the air.
10148{_}743{_}And some cried, "Help, help!"
10148{_}744{_}and some, "A rescue, a rescue!"
10148{_}745{_}"Treason!"
10148{_}746{_}cried the Sheriff in a loud voice.
10148{_}747{_}"Bear back!
10148{_}748{_}Bear back!
10148{_}749{_}Else we be all dead men!"
10148{_}750{_}Thereupon he reined his horse backward through the thickest of the crowd.
10148{_}751{_}Now Robin Hood and his band might have slain half of the Sheriff's men had they desired to do so, but they let them push out of the press and get them gone, only sending a bunch of arrows after them to hurry them in their flight.
10148{_}752{_}"Oh stay!"
10148{_}753{_}shouted Will Stutely after the Sheriff.
10148{_}754{_}"Thou wilt never catch bold Robin Hood if thou dost not stand to meet him face to face."
10148{_}755{_}But the Sheriff, bowing along his horse's back, made no answer but only spurred the faster.
10148{_}756{_}Then Will Stutely turned to Little John and looked him in the face till the tears ran down from his eyes and he wept aloud; and kissing his friend's cheeks, "O Little John!"
10148{_}757{_}quoth he, "mine own true friend, and he that I love better than man or woman in all the world beside!
10148{_}758{_}Little did I reckon to see thy face this day, or to meet thee this side Paradise."
10148{_}759{_}Little John could make no answer, but wept also.
10148{_}760{_}Then Robin Hood gathered his band together in a close rank, with Will Stutely in the midst, and thus they moved slowly away toward Sherwood, and were gone, as a storm cloud moves away from the spot where a tempest has swept the land.
10148{_}761{_}But they left ten of the Sheriff's men lying along the ground wounded--some more, some less--yet no one knew who smote them down.
10148{_}762{_}Thus the Sheriff of Nottingham tried thrice to take Robin Hood and failed each time; and the last time he was frightened, for he felt how near he had come to losing his life; so he said, "These men fear neither God nor man, nor king nor king's officers.
10148{_}763{_}I would sooner lose mine office than my life, so I will trouble them no more."
10148{_}764{_}So he kept close within his castle for many a day and dared not show his face outside of his own household, and all the time he was gloomy and would speak to no one, for he was ashamed of what had happened that day.
10148{_}765{_}Robin Hood Turns Butcher  NOW AFTER all these things had happened, and it became known to Robin Hood how the Sheriff had tried three times to make him captive, he said to himself, "If I have the chance, I will make our worshipful Sheriff pay right well for that which he hath done to me.
10148{_}766{_}Maybe I may bring him some time into Sherwood Forest and have him to a right merry feast with us."
10148{_}767{_}For when Robin Hood caught a baron or a squire, or a fat abbot or bishop, he brought them to the greenwood tree and feasted them before he lightened their purses.
10148{_}768{_}But in the meantime Robin Hood and his band lived quietly in Sherwood Forest, without showing their faces abroad, for Robin knew that it would not be wise for him to be seen in the neighborhood of Nottingham, those in authority being very wroth with him.
10148{_}769{_}But though they did not go abroad, they lived a merry life within the woodlands, spending the days in shooting at garlands hung upon a willow wand at the end of the glade, the leafy aisles ringing with merry jests and laughter: for whoever missed the garland was given a sound buffet, which, if delivered by Little John, never failed to topple over the unfortunate yeoman.
10148{_}770{_}Then they had bouts of wrestling and of cudgel play, so that every day they gained in skill and strength.
10148{_}771{_}Thus they dwelled for nearly a year, and in that time Robin Hood often turned over in his mind many means of making an even score with the Sheriff.
10148{_}772{_}At last he began to fret at his confinement; so one day he took up his stout cudgel and set forth to seek adventure, strolling blithely along until he came to the edge of Sherwood.
10148{_}773{_}There, as he rambled along the sunlit road, he met a lusty young butcher driving a fine mare and riding in a stout new cart, all hung about with meat.
10148{_}774{_}Merrily whistled the Butcher as he jogged along, for he was going to the market, and the day was fresh and sweet, making his heart blithe within him.
10148{_}775{_}"Good morrow to thee, jolly fellow," quoth Robin, "thou seemest happy this merry morn."
10148{_}776{_}"Ay, that am I," quoth the jolly Butcher, "and why should I not be so?
10148{_}777{_}Am I not hale in wind and limb?
10148{_}778{_}Have I not the bonniest lass in all Nottinghamshire?
10148{_}779{_}And lastly, am I not to be married to her on Thursday next in sweet Locksley Town?"
10148{_}780{_}"Ha," said Robin, "comest thou from Locksley Town?
10148{_}781{_}Well do I know that fair place for miles about, and well do I know each hedgerow and gentle pebbly stream, and even all the bright little fishes therein, for there I was born and bred.
10148{_}782{_}Now, where goest thou with thy meat, my fair friend?"
10148{_}783{_}"I go to the market at Nottingham Town to sell my beef and my mutton," answered the Butcher.
10148{_}784{_}"But who art thou that comest from Locksley Town?"
10148{_}785{_}"A yeoman am I, and men do call me Robin Hood."
10148{_}786{_}"Now, by Our Lady's grace," cried the Butcher, "well do I know thy name, and many a time have I heard thy deeds both sung and spoken of.
10148{_}787{_}But Heaven forbid that thou shouldst take aught of me!
10148{_}788{_}An honest man am I, and have wronged neither man nor maid; so trouble me not, good master, as I have never troubled thee."
10148{_}789{_}"Nay, Heaven forbid, indeed," quoth Robin, "that I should take from such as thee, jolly fellow!
10148{_}790{_}Not so much as one farthing would I take from thee, for I love a fair Saxon face like thine right well--more especially when it cometh from Locksley Town, and most especially when the man that owneth it is to marry a bonny lass on Thursday next.
10148{_}791{_}But come, tell me for what price thou wilt sell me all of thy meat and thy horse and cart."
10148{_}792{_}"At four marks do I value meat, cart, and mare," quoth the Butcher, "but if I do not sell all my meat I will not have four marks in value."
10148{_}793{_}Then Robin Hood plucked the purse from his girdle, and quoth he, "Here in this purse are six marks.
10148{_}794{_}Now, I would fain be a butcher for the day and sell my meat in Nottingham Town.
10148{_}795{_}Wilt thou close a bargain with me and take six marks for thine outfit?"
10148{_}796{_}"Now may the blessings of all the saints fall on thine honest head!"
10148{_}797{_}cried the Butcher right joyfully, as he leaped down from his cart and took the purse that Robin held out to him.
10148{_}798{_}"Nay," quoth Robin, laughing loudly, "many do like me and wish me well, but few call me honest.
10148{_}799{_}Now get thee gone back to thy lass, and give her a sweet kiss from me."
10148{_}800{_}So saying, he donned the Butcher's apron, and, climbing into the cart, he took the reins in his hand and drove off through the forest to Nottingham Town.
10148{_}801{_}When he came to Nottingham, he entered that part of the market where butchers stood, and took up his inn[Stand for selling] in the best place he could find.
10148{_}802{_}Next, he opened his stall and spread his meat upon the bench, then, taking his cleaver and steel and clattering them together, he trolled aloud in merry tones:   "Now come, ye lasses, and eke ye dames,      And buy your meat from me;  For three pennyworths of meat I sell      For the charge of one penny.
10148{_}803{_}"Lamb have I that hath fed upon nought      But the dainty dames pied,  And the violet sweet, and the daffodil      That grow fair streams beside.
10148{_}804{_}"And beef have I from the heathery words,      And mutton from dales all green,  And veal as white as a maiden's brow,      With its mother's milk, I ween.
10148{_}805{_}"Then come, ye lasses, and eke ye dames,      Come, buy your meat from me,  For three pennyworths of meat I sell      For the charge of one penny."
10148{_}806{_}Thus he sang blithely, while all who stood near listened amazedly.
10148{_}807{_}Then, when he had finished, he clattered the steel and cleaver still more loudly, shouting lustily, "Now, who'll buy?
10148{_}808{_}Who'll buy?
10148{_}809{_}Four fixed prices have I.
10148{_}810{_}Three pennyworths of meat I sell to a fat friar or priest for sixpence, for I want not their custom; stout aldermen I charge threepence, for it doth not matter to me whether they buy or not; to buxom dames I sell three pennyworths of meat for one penny for I like their custom well; but to the bonny lass that hath a liking for a good tight butcher I charge nought but one fair kiss, for I like her custom the best of all."
10148{_}811{_}Then all began to stare and wonder and crowd around, laughing, for never was such selling heard of in all Nottingham Town; but when they came to buy they found it as he had said, for he gave goodwife or dame as much meat for one penny as they could buy elsewhere for three, and when a widow or a poor woman came to him, he gave her flesh for nothing; but when a merry lass came and gave him a kiss, he charged not one penny for his meat; and many such came to his stall, for his eyes were as blue as the skies of June, and he laughed merrily, giving to each full measure.
10148{_}812{_}Thus he sold his meat so fast that no butcher that stood near him could sell anything.
10148{_}813{_}Then they began to talk among themselves, and some said, "This must be some thief who has stolen cart, horse, and meat"; but others said, "Nay, when did ye ever see a thief who parted with his goods so freely and merrily?
10148{_}814{_}This must be some prodigal who hath sold his father's land, and would fain live merrily while the money lasts."
10148{_}815{_}And these latter being the greater number, the others came round, one by one to their way of thinking.
10148{_}816{_}Then some of the butchers came to him to make his acquaintance.
10148{_}817{_}"Come, brother," quoth one who was the head of them all, "we be all of one trade, so wilt thou go dine with us?
10148{_}818{_}For this day the Sheriff hath asked all the Butcher Guild to feast with him at the Guild Hall.
10148{_}819{_}There will be stout fare and much to drink, and that thou likest, or I much mistake thee."
10148{_}820{_}"Now, beshrew his heart," quoth jolly Robin, "that would deny a butcher.
10148{_}821{_}And, moreover, I will go dine with you all, my sweet lads, and that as fast as I can hie."
10148{_}822{_}Whereupon, having sold all his meat, he closed his stall and went with them to the great Guild Hall.
10148{_}823{_}There the Sheriff had already come in state, and with him many butchers.
10148{_}824{_}When Robin and those that were with him came in, all laughing at some merry jest he had been telling them, those that were near the Sheriff whispered to him, "Yon is a right mad blade, for he hath sold more meat for one penny this day than we could sell for three, and to whatsoever merry lass gave him a kiss he gave meat for nought."
10148{_}825{_}And others said, "He is some prodigal that hath sold his land for silver and gold, and meaneth to spend all right merrily."
10148{_}826{_}Then the Sheriff called Robin to him, not knowing him in his butcher's dress, and made him sit close to him on his right hand; for he loved a rich young prodigal--especially when he thought that he might lighten that prodigal's pockets into his own most worshipful purse.
10148{_}827{_}So he made much of Robin, and laughed and talked with him more than with any of the others.
10148{_}828{_}At last the dinner was ready to be served and the Sheriff bade Robin say grace, so Robin stood up and said, "Now Heaven bless us all and eke good meat and good sack within this house, and may all butchers be and remain as honest men as I am."
10148{_}829{_}At this all laughed, the Sheriff loudest of all, for he said to himself, "Surely this is indeed some prodigal, and perchance I may empty his purse of some of the money that the fool throweth about so freely."
10148{_}830{_}Then he spake aloud to Robin, saying, "Thou art a jolly young blade, and I love thee mightily"; and he smote Robin upon the shoulder.
10148{_}831{_}Then Robin laughed loudly too.
10148{_}832{_}"Yea," quoth he, "I know thou dost love a jolly blade, for didst thou not have jolly Robin Hood at thy shooting match and didst thou not gladly give him a bright golden arrow for his own?"
10148{_}833{_}At this the Sheriff looked grave and all the guild of butchers too, so that none laughed but Robin, only some winked slyly at each other.
10148{_}834{_}"Come, fill us some sack!"
10148{_}835{_}cried Robin.
10148{_}836{_}"Let us e'er be merry while we may, for man is but dust, and he hath but a span to live here till the worm getteth him, as our good gossip Swanthold sayeth; so let life be merry while it lasts, say I. Nay, never look down i' the mouth, Sir Sheriff.
10148{_}837{_}Who knowest but that thou mayest catch Robin Hood yet, if thou drinkest less good sack and Malmsey, and bringest down the fat about thy paunch and the dust from out thy brain.
10148{_}838{_}Be merry, man."
10148{_}839{_}Then the Sheriff laughed again, but not as though he liked the jest, while the butchers said, one to another, "Before Heaven, never have we seen such a mad rollicking blade.
10148{_}840{_}Mayhap, though, he will make the Sheriff mad."
10148{_}841{_}"How now, brothers," cried Robin, "be merry!
10148{_}842{_}nay, never count over your farthings, for by this and by that I will pay this shot myself, e'en though it cost two hundred pounds.
10148{_}843{_}So let no man draw up his lip, nor thrust his forefinger into his purse, for I swear that neither butcher nor Sheriff shall pay one penny for this feast."
10148{_}844{_}"Now thou art a right merry soul," quoth the Sheriff, "and I wot thou must have many a head of horned beasts and many an acre of land, that thou dost spend thy money so freely."
10148{_}845{_}"Ay, that have I," quoth Robin, laughing loudly again, "five hundred and more horned beasts have I and my brothers, and none of them have we been able to sell, else I might not have turned butcher.
10148{_}846{_}As for my land, I have never asked my steward how many acres I have."
10148{_}847{_}At this the Sheriff's eyes twinkled, and he chuckled to himself.
10148{_}848{_}"Nay, good youth," quoth he, "if thou canst not sell thy cattle, it may be I will find a man that will lift them from thy hands; perhaps that man may be myself, for I love a merry youth and would help such a one along the path of life.
10148{_}849{_}Now how much dost thou want for thy horned cattle?"
10148{_}850{_}"Well," quoth Robin, "they are worth at least five hundred pounds."
10148{_}851{_}"Nay," answered the Sheriff slowly, and as if he were thinking within himself, "well do I love thee, and fain would I help thee along, but five hundred pounds in money is a good round sum; besides I have it not by me.
10148{_}852{_}Yet I will give thee three hundred pounds for them all, and that in good hard silver and gold."
10148{_}853{_}"Now thou old miser!"
10148{_}854{_}quoth Robin, "well thou knowest that so many horned cattle are worth seven hundred pounds and more, and even that is but small for them, and yet thou, with thy gray hairs and one foot in the grave, wouldst trade upon the folly of a wild youth."
10148{_}855{_}At this the Sheriff looked grimly at Robin.
10148{_}856{_}"Nay," quoth Robin, "look not on me as though thou hadst sour beer in thy mouth, man.
10148{_}857{_}I will take thine offer, for I and my brothers do need the money.
10148{_}858{_}We lead a merry life, and no one leads a merry life for a farthing, so I will close the bargain with thee.
10148{_}859{_}But mind that thou bringest a good three hundred pounds with thee, for I trust not one that driveth so shrewd a bargain."
10148{_}860{_}"I will bring the money," said the Sheriff.
10148{_}861{_}"But what is thy name, good youth?"
10148{_}862{_}"Men call me Robert o' Locksley," quoth bold Robin.
10148{_}863{_}"Then, good Robert o' Locksley," quoth the Sheriff, "I will come this day to see thy horned beasts.
10148{_}864{_}But first my clerk shall draw up a paper in which thou shalt be bound to the sale, for thou gettest not my money without I get thy beasts in return."
10148{_}865{_}Then Robin Hood laughed again.
10148{_}866{_}"So be it," he said, smiting his palm upon the Sheriff's hand.
10148{_}867{_}"Truly my brothers will be thankful to thee for thy money."
10148{_}868{_}Thus the bargain was closed, but many of the butchers talked among themselves of the Sheriff, saying that it was but a scurvy trick to beguile a poor spendthrift youth in this way.
10148{_}869{_}The afternoon had come when the Sheriff mounted his horse and joined Robin Hood, who stood outside the gateway of the paved court waiting for him, for he had sold his horse and cart to a trader for two marks.
10148{_}870{_}Then they set forth upon their way, the Sheriff riding upon his horse and Robin running beside him.
10148{_}871{_}Thus they left Nottingham Town and traveled forward along the dusty highway, laughing and jesting together as though they had been old friends.
10148{_}872{_}But all the time the Sheriff said within himself, "Thy jest to me of Robin Hood shall cost thee dear, good fellow, even four hundred pounds, thou fool."
10148{_}873{_}For he thought he would make at least that much by his bargain.
10148{_}874{_}So they journeyed onward till they came within the verge of Sherwood Forest, when presently the Sheriff looked up and down and to the right and to the left of him, and then grew quiet and ceased his laughter.
10148{_}875{_}"Now," quoth he, "may Heaven and its saints preserve us this day from a rogue men call Robin Hood."
10148{_}876{_}Then Robin laughed aloud.
10148{_}877{_}"Nay," said he, "thou mayst set thy mind at rest, for well do I know Robin Hood and well do I know that thou art in no more danger from him this day than thou art from me."
10148{_}878{_}At this the Sheriff looked askance at Robin, saying to himself, "I like not that thou seemest so well acquainted with this bold outlaw, and I wish that I were well out of Sherwood Forest."
10148{_}879{_}But still they traveled deeper into the forest shades, and the deeper they went, the more quiet grew the Sheriff.
10148{_}880{_}At last they came to where the road took a sudden bend, and before them a herd of dun deer went tripping across the path.
10148{_}881{_}Then Robin Hood came close to the Sheriff and pointing his finger, he said, "These are my horned beasts, good Master Sheriff.
10148{_}882{_}How dost thou like them?
10148{_}883{_}Are they not fat and fair to see?"
10148{_}884{_}At this the Sheriff drew rein quickly.
10148{_}885{_}"Now fellow," quoth he, "I would I were well out of this forest, for I like not thy company.
10148{_}886{_}Go thou thine own path, good friend, and let me but go mine."
10148{_}887{_}But Robin only laughed and caught the Sheriff's bridle rein.
10148{_}888{_}"Nay," cried he, "stay awhile, for I would thou shouldst see my brothers, who own these fair horned beasts with me."
10148{_}889{_}So saying, he clapped his bugle to his mouth and winded three merry notes, and presently up the path came leaping fivescore good stout yeomen with Little John at their head.
10148{_}890{_}"What wouldst thou have, good master?"
10148{_}891{_}quoth Little John.
10148{_}892{_}"Why," answered Robin, "dost thou not see that I have brought goodly company to feast with us today?
10148{_}893{_}Fye, for shame!
10148{_}894{_}Do you not see our good and worshipful master, the Sheriff of Nottingham?
10148{_}895{_}Take thou his bridle, Little John, for he has honored us today by coming to feast with us."
10148{_}896{_}Then all doffed their hats humbly, without smiling or seeming to be in jest, while Little John took the bridle rein and led the palfrey still deeper into the forest, all marching in order, with Robin Hood walking beside the Sheriff, hat in hand.
10148{_}897{_}All this time the Sheriff said never a word but only looked about him like one suddenly awakened from sleep; but when he found himself going within the very depths of Sherwood his heart sank within him, for he thought, "Surely my three hundred pounds will be taken from me, even if they take not my life itself, for I have plotted against their lives more than once."
10148{_}898{_}But all seemed humble and meek and not a word was said of danger, either to life or money.
10148{_}899{_}So at last they came to that part of Sherwood Forest where a noble oak spread its branches wide, and beneath it was a seat all made of moss, on which Robin sat down, placing the Sheriff at his right hand.
10148{_}900{_}"Now busk ye, my merry men all," quoth he, "and bring forth the best we have, both of meat and wine, for his worship the Sheriff hath feasted me in Nottingham Guild Hall today, and I would not have him go back empty."
10148{_}901{_}All this time nothing had been said of the Sheriff's money, so presently he began to pluck up heart.
10148{_}902{_}"For," said he to himself, "maybe Robin Hood hath forgotten all about it."
10148{_}903{_}Then, while beyond in the forest bright fires crackled and savory smells of sweetly roasting venison and fat capons filled the glade, and brown pasties warmed beside the blaze, did Robin Hood entertain the Sheriff right royally.
10148{_}904{_}First, several couples stood forth at quarterstaff, and so shrewd were they at the game, and so quickly did they give stroke and parry, that the Sheriff, who loved to watch all lusty sports of the kind, clapped his hands, forgetting where he was, and crying aloud, "Well struck!
10148{_}905{_}Well struck, thou fellow with the black beard!"
10148{_}906{_}little knowing that the man he called upon was the Tinker that tried to serve his warrant upon Robin Hood.
10148{_}907{_}Then several yeomen came forward and spread cloths upon the green grass, and placed a royal feast; while others still broached barrels of sack and Malmsey and good stout ale, and set them in jars upon the cloth, with drinking horns about them.
10148{_}908{_}Then all sat down and feasted and drank merrily together until the sun was low and the half-moon glimmered with a pale light betwixt the leaves of the trees overhead.
10148{_}909{_}Then the Sheriff arose and said, "I thank you all, good yeomen, for the merry entertainment ye have given me this day.
10148{_}910{_}Right courteously have ye used me, showing therein that ye have much respect for our glorious King and his deputy in brave Nottinghamshire.
10148{_}911{_}But the shadows grow long, and I must away before darkness comes, lest I lose myself within the forest."
10148{_}912{_}Then Robin Hood and all his merry men arose also, and Robin said to the Sheriff, "If thou must go, worshipful sir, go thou must; but thou hast forgotten one thing."
10148{_}913{_}"Nay, I forgot nought," said the Sheriff; yet all the same his heart sank within him.
10148{_}914{_}"But I say thou hast forgot something," quoth Robin.
10148{_}915{_}"We keep a merry inn here in the greenwood, but whoever becometh our guest must pay his reckoning."
10148{_}916{_}Then the Sheriff laughed, but the laugh was hollow.
10148{_}917{_}"Well, jolly boys," quoth he, "we have had a merry time together today, and even if ye had not asked me, I would have given you a score of pounds for the sweet entertainment I have had."
10148{_}918{_}"Nay," quoth Robin seriously, "it would ill beseem us to treat Your Worship so meanly.
10148{_}919{_}By my faith, Sir Sheriff, I would be ashamed to show my face if I did not reckon the King's deputy at three hundred pounds.
10148{_}920{_}Is it not so, my merry men all?"
10148{_}921{_}Then "Ay!"
10148{_}922{_}cried all, in a loud voice.
10148{_}923{_}"Three hundred devils!"
10148{_}924{_}roared the Sheriff.
10148{_}925{_}"Think ye that your beggarly feast was worth three pounds, let alone three hundred?"
10148{_}926{_}"Nay," quoth Robin gravely.
10148{_}927{_}"Speak not so roundly, Your Worship.
10148{_}928{_}I do love thee for the sweet feast thou hast given me this day in merry Nottingham Town; but there be those here who love thee not so much.
10148{_}929{_}If thou wilt look down the cloth thou wilt see Will Stutely, in whose eyes thou hast no great favor; then two other stout fellows are there here that thou knowest not, that were wounded in a brawl nigh Nottingham Town, some time ago--thou wottest when; one of them was sore hurt in one arm, yet he hath got the use of it again.
10148{_}930{_}Good Sheriff, be advised by me; pay thy score without more ado, or maybe it may fare ill with thee."
10148{_}931{_}As he spoke the Sheriff's ruddy cheeks grew pale, and he said nothing more but looked upon the ground and gnawed his nether lip.
10148{_}932{_}Then slowly he drew forth his fat purse and threw it upon the cloth in front of him.
10148{_}933{_}"Now take the purse, Little John," quoth Robin Hood, "and see that the reckoning be right.
10148{_}934{_}We would not doubt our Sheriff, but he might not like it if he should find he had not paid his full score."
10148{_}935{_}Then Little John counted the money and found that the bag held three hundred pounds in silver and gold.
10148{_}936{_}But to the Sheriff it seemed as if every clink of the bright money was a drop of blood from his veins.
10148{_}937{_}And when he saw it all counted out in a heap of silver and gold, filling a wooden platter, he turned away and silently mounted his horse.
10148{_}938{_}"Never have we had so worshipful a guest before!"
10148{_}939{_}quoth Robin, "and, as the day waxeth late, I will send one of my young men to guide thee out of the forest depths."
10148{_}940{_}"Nay, Heaven forbid!"
10148{_}941{_}cried the Sheriff hastily.
10148{_}942{_}"I can find mine own way, good man, without aid."
10148{_}943{_}"Then I will put thee on the right track mine own self," quoth Robin, and, taking the Sheriff's horse by the bridle rein, he led him into the main forest path.
10148{_}944{_}Then, before he let him go, he said, "Now, fare thee well, good Sheriff, and when next thou thinkest to despoil some poor prodigal, remember thy feast in Sherwood Forest.
10148{_}945{_}'Ne'er buy a horse, good friend, without first looking into its mouth,' as our good gaffer Swanthold says.
10148{_}946{_}And so, once more, fare thee well."
10148{_}947{_}Then he clapped his hand to the horse's back, and off went nag and Sheriff through the forest glades.
10148{_}948{_}Then bitterly the Sheriff rued the day that first he meddled with Robin Hood, for all men laughed at him and many ballads were sung by folk throughout the country, of how the Sheriff went to shear and came home shorn to the very quick.
10148{_}949{_}For thus men sometimes overreach themselves through greed and guile.
10148{_}950{_}Little John Goes to Nottingham Fair  SPRING HAD GONE since the Sheriff's feast in Sherwood, and summer also, and the mellow month of October had come.
10148{_}951{_}All the air was cool and fresh; the harvests were gathered home, the young birds were full fledged, the hops were plucked, and apples were ripe.
10148{_}952{_}But though time had so smoothed things over that men no longer talked of the horned beasts that the Sheriff wished to buy, he was still sore about the matter and could not bear to hear Robin Hood's name spoken in his presence.
10148{_}953{_}With October had come the time for holding the great Fair which was celebrated every five years at Nottingham Town, to which folk came from far and near throughout the country.
10148{_}954{_}At such times archery was always the main sport of the day, for the Nottinghamshire yeomen were the best hand at the longbow in all merry England, but this year the Sheriff hesitated a long time before he issued proclamation of the Fair, fearing lest Robin Hood and his band might come to it.
10148{_}955{_}At first he had a great part of a mind not to proclaim the Fair, but second thought told him that men would laugh at him and say among themselves that he was afraid of Robin Hood, so he put that thought by.
10148{_}956{_}At last he fixed in his mind that he would offer such a prize as they would not care to shoot for.
10148{_}957{_}At such times it had been the custom to offer a half score of marks or a tun of ale, so this year he proclaimed that a prize of two fat steers should be given to the best bowman.
10148{_}958{_}When Robin Hood heard what had been proclaimed he was vexed, and said, "Now beshrew this Sheriff that he should offer such a prize that none but shepherd hinds will care to shoot for it!
10148{_}959{_}I would have loved nothing better than to have had another bout at merry Nottingham Town, but if I should win this prize nought would it pleasure or profit me."
10148{_}960{_}Then up spoke Little John:  "Nay, but hearken, good master," said he, "only today Will Stutely, young David of Doncaster, and I were at the Sign of the Blue Boar, and there we heard all the news of this merry Fair, and also that the Sheriff hath offered this prize, that we of Sherwood might not care to come to the Fair; so, good master, if thou wilt, I would fain go and strive to win even this poor thing among the stout yeomen who will shoot at Nottingham Town."
10148{_}961{_}"Nay, Little John," quoth Robin, "thou art a sound stout fellow, yet thou lackest the cunning that good Stutely hath, and I would not have harm befall thee for all Nottinghamshire.
10148{_}962{_}Nevertheless, if thou wilt go, take some disguise lest there be those there who may know thee."
10148{_}963{_}"So be it, good master," quoth Little John, "yet all the disguise that I wish is a good suit of scarlet instead of this of Lincoln green.
10148{_}964{_}I will draw the cowl of my jacket about my head so that it will hide my brown hair and beard, and then, I trust, no one will know me."
10148{_}965{_}"It is much against my will," said Robin Hood, "ne'ertheless, if thou dost wish it, get thee gone, but bear thyself seemingly, Little John, for thou art mine own right-hand man and I could ill bear to have harm befall thee."
10148{_}966{_}So Little John clad himself all in scarlet and started off to the Fair at Nottingham Town.
10148{_}967{_}Right merry were these Fair days at Nottingham, when the green before the great town gate was dotted with booths standing in rows, with tents of many-colored canvas, hung about with streamers and garlands of flowers, and the folk came from all the countryside, both gentle and common.
10148{_}968{_}In some booths there was dancing to merry music, in others flowed ale and beer, and in others yet again sweet cakes and barley sugar were sold; and sport was going outside the booths also, where some minstrel sang ballads of the olden time, playing a second upon the harp, or where the wrestlers struggled with one another within the sawdust ring, but the people gathered most of all around a raised platform where stout fellows played at quarterstaff.
10148{_}969{_}So Little John came to the Fair.
10148{_}970{_}All scarlet were his hose and jerkin, and scarlet was his cowled cap, with a scarlet feather stuck in the side of it.
10148{_}971{_}Over his shoulders was slung a stout bow of yew, and across his back hung a quiver of good round arrows.
10148{_}972{_}Many turned to look after such a stout, tall fellow, for his shoulders were broader by a palm's-breadth than any that were there, and he stood a head taller than all the other men.
10148{_}973{_}The lasses, also, looked at him askance, thinking they had never seen a lustier youth.
10148{_}974{_}First of all he went to the booth where stout ale was sold and, standing aloft on a bench, he called to all that were near to come and drink with him.
10148{_}975{_}"Hey, sweet lads!"
10148{_}976{_}cried he "who will drink ale with a stout yeoman?
10148{_}977{_}Come, all!
10148{_}978{_}Come, all!
10148{_}979{_}Let us be merry, for the day is sweet and the ale is tingling.
10148{_}980{_}Come hither, good yeoman, and thou, and thou; for not a farthing shall one of you pay.
10148{_}981{_}Nay, turn hither, thou lusty beggar, and thou jolly tinker, for all shall be merry with me."
10148{_}982{_}Thus he shouted, and all crowded around, laughing, while the brown ale flowed; and they called Little John a brave fellow, each swearing that he loved him as his own brother; for when one has entertainment with nothing to pay, one loves the man that gives it to one.
10148{_}983{_}Then he strolled to the platform where they were at cudgel play, for he loved a bout at quarterstaff as he loved meat and drink; and here befell an adventure that was sung in ballads throughout the mid-country for many a day.
10148{_}984{_}One fellow there was that cracked crowns of everyone who threw cap into the ring.
10148{_}985{_}This was Eric o' Lincoln, of great renown, whose name had been sung in ballads throughout the countryside.
10148{_}986{_}When Little John reached the stand he found none fighting, but only bold Eric walking up and down the platform, swinging his staff and shouting lustily, "Now, who will come and strike a stroke for the lass he loves the best, with a good Lincolnshire yeoman?
10148{_}987{_}How now, lads?
10148{_}988{_}Step up!
10148{_}989{_}Step up!
10148{_}990{_}Or else the lasses' eyes are not bright hereabouts, or the blood of Nottingham youth is sluggish and cold.
10148{_}991{_}Lincoln against Nottingham, say I!
10148{_}992{_}For no one hath put foot upon the boards this day such as we of Lincoln call a cudgel player."
10148{_}993{_}At this, one would nudge another with his elbow, saying, "Go thou, Ned!"
10148{_}994{_}or "Go thou, Thomas!"
10148{_}995{_}but no lad cared to gain a cracked crown for nothing.
10148{_}996{_}Presently Eric saw where Little John stood among the others, a head and shoulders above them all, and he called to him loudly, "Halloa, thou long-legged fellow in scarlet!
10148{_}997{_}Broad are thy shoulders and thick thy head; is not thy lass fair enough for thee to take cudgel in hand for her sake?
10148{_}998{_}In truth, I believe that Nottingham men do turn to bone and sinew, for neither heart nor courage have they!
10148{_}999{_}Now, thou great lout, wilt thou not twirl staff for Nottingham?"
10148{_}1000{_}"Ay," quoth Little John, "had I but mine own good staff here, it would pleasure me hugely to crack thy knave's pate, thou saucy braggart!
10148{_}1001{_}I wot it would be well for thee an thy cock's comb were cut!"
10148{_}1002{_}Thus he spoke, slowly at first, for he was slow to move; but his wrath gathered headway like a great stone rolling down a hill, so that at the end he was full of anger.
10148{_}1003{_}Then Eric o' Lincoln laughed aloud.
10148{_}1004{_}"Well spoken for one who fears to meet me fairly, man to man," said he.
10148{_}1005{_}"Saucy art thou thine own self, and if thou puttest foot upon these boards, I will make thy saucy tongue rattle within thy teeth!"
10148{_}1006{_}"Now," quoth Little John, "is there never a man here that will lend me a good stout staff till I try the mettle of yon fellow?"
10148{_}1007{_}At this, half a score reached him their staves, and he took the stoutest and heaviest of them all.
10148{_}1008{_}Then, looking up and down the cudgel, he said, "Now, I have in my hand but a splint of wood--a barley straw, as it were--yet I trow it will have to serve me, so here goeth."
10148{_}1009{_}Thereupon he cast the cudgel upon the stand and, leaping lightly after it, snatched it up in his hand again.
10148{_}1010{_}Then each man stood in his place and measured the other with fell looks until he that directed the sport cried, "Play!"
10148{_}1011{_}At this they stepped forth, each grasping his staff tightly in the middle.
10148{_}1012{_}Then those that stood around saw the stoutest game of quarterstaff that e'er Nottingham Town beheld.
10148{_}1013{_}At first Eric o' Lincoln thought that he would gain an easy advantage, so he came forth as if he would say, "Watch, good people, how that I carve you this cockerel right speedily"; but he presently found it to be no such speedy matter.
10148{_}1014{_}Right deftly he struck, and with great skill of fence, but he had found his match in Little John.
10148{_}1015{_}Once, twice, thrice, he struck, and three times Little John turned the blows to the left hand and to the right.
10148{_}1016{_}Then quickly and with a dainty backhanded blow, he rapped Eric beneath his guard so shrewdly that it made his head ring again.
10148{_}1017{_}Then Eric stepped back to gather his wits, while a great shout went up and all were glad that Nottingham had cracked Lincoln's crown; and thus ended the first bout of the game.
10148{_}1018{_}Then presently the director of the sport cried, "Play!"
10148{_}1019{_}and they came together again; but now Eric played warily, for he found his man was of right good mettle, and also he had no sweet memory of the blow that he had got; so this bout neither Little John nor the Lincoln man caught a stroke within his guard.
10148{_}1020{_}Then, after a while, they parted again, and this made the second bout.
10148{_}1021{_}Then for the third time they came together, and at first Eric strove to be wary, as he had been before; but, growing mad at finding himself so foiled, he lost his wits and began to rain blows so fiercely and so fast that they rattled like hail on penthouse roof; but, in spite of all, he did not reach within Little John's guard.
10148{_}1022{_}Then at last Little John saw his chance and seized it right cleverly.
10148{_}1023{_}Once more, with a quick blow, he rapped Eric beside the head, and ere he could regain himself, Little John slipped his right hand down to his left and, with a swinging blow, smote the other so sorely upon the crown that down he fell as though he would never move again.
10148{_}1024{_}Then the people shouted so loud that folk came running from all about to see what was the ado; while Little John leaped down from the stand and gave the staff back to him that had lent it to him.
10148{_}1025{_}And thus ended the famous bout between Little John and Eric o' Lincoln of great renown.
10148{_}1026{_}But now the time had come when those who were to shoot with the longbow were to take their places, so the people began flocking to the butts where the shooting was to be.
10148{_}1027{_}Near the target, in a good place, sat the Sheriff upon a raised dais, with many gentlefolk around him.
10148{_}1028{_}When the archers had taken their places, the herald came forward and proclaimed the rules of the game, and how each should shoot three shots, and to him that should shoot the best the prize of two fat steers was to belong.
10148{_}1029{_}A score of brave shots were gathered there, and among them some of the keenest hands at the longbow in Lincoln and Nottinghamshire; and among them Little John stood taller than all the rest.
10148{_}1030{_}"Who is yon stranger clad all in scarlet?"
10148{_}1031{_}said some, and others answered, "It is he that hath but now so soundly cracked the crown of Eric o' Lincoln."
10148{_}1032{_}Thus the people talked among themselves, until at last it reached even the Sheriff's ears.
10148{_}1033{_}And now each man stepped forward and shot in turn; but though each shot well, Little John was the best of all, for three times he struck the clout, and once only the length of a barleycorn from the center.
10148{_}1034{_}"Hey for the tall archer!"
10148{_}1035{_}shouted the crowd, and some among them shouted, "Hey for Reynold Greenleaf!"
10148{_}1036{_}for this was the name that Little John had called himself that day.
10148{_}1037{_}Then the Sheriff stepped down from the raised seat and came to where the archers stood, while all doffed their caps that saw him coming.
10148{_}1038{_}He looked keenly at Little John but did not know him, though he said, after a while, "How now, good fellow, methinks there is that about thy face that I have seen erewhile."
10148{_}1039{_}"Mayhap it may be so," quoth Little John, "for often have I seen Your Worship."
10148{_}1040{_}And, as he spoke, he looked steadily into the Sheriff's eyes so that the latter did not suspect who he was.
10148{_}1041{_}"A brave blade art thou, good friend," said the Sheriff, "and I hear that thou hast well upheld the skill of Nottinghamshire against that of Lincoln this day.
10148{_}1042{_}What may be thy name, good fellow?"
10148{_}1043{_}"Men do call me Reynold Greenleaf, Your Worship," said Little John; and the old ballad that tells of this, adds, "So, in truth, was he a green leaf, but of what manner of tree the Sheriff wotted not."
10148{_}1044{_}"Now, Reynold Greenleaf," quoth the Sheriff, "thou art the fairest hand at the longbow that mine eyes ever beheld, next to that false knave, Robin Hood, from whose wiles Heaven forfend me!
10148{_}1045{_}Wilt thou join my service, good fellow?
10148{_}1046{_}Thou shalt be paid right well, for three suits of clothes shalt thou have a year, with good food and as much ale as thou canst drink; and, besides this, I will pay thee forty marks each Michaelmastide."
10148{_}1047{_}"Then here stand I a free man, and right gladly will I enter thy household," said Little John, for he thought he might find some merry jest, should he enter the Sheriff's service.
10148{_}1048{_}"Fairly hast thou won the fat steers," said the Sheriff, "and hereunto I will add a butt of good March beer, for joy of having gotten such a man; for, I wot, thou shootest as fair a shaft as Robin Hood himself."
10148{_}1049{_}"Then," said Little John, "for joy of having gotten myself into thy service, I will give fat steers and brown ale to all these good folk, to make them merry withal."
10148{_}1050{_}At this arose a great shout, many casting their caps aloft, for joy of the gift.
10148{_}1051{_}Then some built great fires and roasted the steers, and others broached the butt of ale, with which all made themselves merry.
10148{_}1052{_}Then, when they had eaten and drunk as much as they could, and when the day faded and the great moon arose, all red and round, over the spires and towers of Nottingham Town, they joined hands and danced around the fires, to the music of bagpipes and harps.
10148{_}1053{_}But long before this merrymaking had begun, the Sheriff and his new servant Reynold Greenleaf were in the Castle of Nottingham.
10148{_}1054{_}How Little John Lived at the Sheriff's  THUS LITTLE JOHN entered into the Sheriff's service and found the life he led there easy enough, for the Sheriff made him his right-hand man and held him in great favor.
10148{_}1055{_}He sat nigh the Sheriff at meat, and he ran beside his horse when he went a-hunting; so that, what with hunting and hawking a little, and eating rich dishes and drinking good sack, and sleeping until late hours in the morning, he grew as fat as a stall-fed ox.
10148{_}1056{_}Thus things floated easily along with the tide, until one day when the Sheriff went a-hunting, there happened that which broke the smooth surface of things.
10148{_}1057{_}This morning the Sheriff and many of his men set forth to meet certain lords, to go a-hunting.
10148{_}1058{_}He looked all about him for his good man, Reynold Greenleaf, but, not finding him, was vexed, for he wished to show Little John's skill to his noble friends.
10148{_}1059{_}As for Little John, he lay abed, snoring lustily, till the sun was high in the heavens.
10148{_}1060{_}At last he opened his eyes and looked about him but did not move to arise.
10148{_}1061{_}Brightly shone the sun in at the window, and all the air was sweet with the scent of woodbine that hung in sprays about the wall without, for the cold winter was past and spring was come again, and Little John lay still, thinking how sweet was everything on this fair morn.
10148{_}1062{_}Just then he heard, faint and far away, a distant bugle note sounding thin and clear.
10148{_}1063{_}The sound was small, but, like a little pebble dropped into a glassy fountain, it broke all the smooth surface of his thoughts, until his whole soul was filled with disturbance.
10148{_}1064{_}His spirit seemed to awaken from its sluggishness, and his memory brought back to him all the merry greenwood life--how the birds were singing blithely there this bright morning, and how his loved companions and friends were feasting and making merry, or perhaps talking of him with sober speech; for when he first entered the Sheriff's service he did so in jest; but the hearthstone was warm during the winter, and the fare was full, and so he had abided, putting off from day to day his going back to Sherwood, until six long months had passed.
10148{_}1065{_}But now he thought of his good master and of Will Stutely, whom he loved better than anyone in all the world, and of young David of Doncaster, whom he had trained so well in all manly sports, till there came over his heart a great and bitter longing for them all, so that his eyes filled with tears.
10148{_}1066{_}Then he said aloud, "Here I grow fat like a stall-fed ox and all my manliness departeth from me while I become a sluggard and dolt.
10148{_}1067{_}But I will arouse me and go back to mine own dear friends once more, and never will I leave them again till life doth leave my lips."
10148{_}1068{_}So saying, he leaped from bed, for he hated his sluggishness now.
10148{_}1069{_}When he came downstairs he saw the Steward standing near the pantry door--a great, fat man, with a huge bundle of keys hanging to his girdle.
10148{_}1070{_}Then Little John said, "Ho, Master Steward, a hungry man am I, for nought have I had for all this blessed morn.
10148{_}1071{_}Therefore, give me to eat."
10148{_}1072{_}Then the Steward looked grimly at him and rattled the keys in his girdle, for he hated Little John because he had found favor with the Sheriff.
10148{_}1073{_}"So, Master Reynold Greenleaf, thou art anhungered, art thou?"
10148{_}1074{_}quoth he.
10148{_}1075{_}"But, fair youth, if thou livest long enough, thou wilt find that he who getteth overmuch sleep for an idle head goeth with an empty stomach.
10148{_}1076{_}For what sayeth the old saw, Master Greenleaf?
10148{_}1077{_}Is it not 'The late fowl findeth but ill faring'?"
10148{_}1078{_}"Now, thou great purse of fat!"
10148{_}1079{_}cried Little John, "I ask thee not for fool's wisdom, but for bread and meat.
10148{_}1080{_}Who art thou, that thou shouldst deny me to eat?
10148{_}1081{_}By Saint Dunstan, thou hadst best tell me where my breakfast is, if thou wouldst save broken bones!"
10148{_}1082{_}"Thy breakfast, Master Fireblaze, is in the pantry," answered the Steward.
10148{_}1083{_}"Then fetch it hither!"
10148{_}1084{_}cried Little John, who waxed angry by this time.
10148{_}1085{_}"Go thou and fetch it thine own self," quoth the Steward.
10148{_}1086{_}"Am I thy slave, to fetch and carry for thee?"
10148{_}1087{_}"I say, go thou, bring it me!"
10148{_}1088{_}"I say, go thou, fetch it for thyself!"
10148{_}1089{_}"Ay, marry, that will I, right quickly!"
10148{_}1090{_}quoth Little John in a rage.
10148{_}1091{_}And, so saying, he strode to the pantry and tried to open the door but found it locked, whereat the Steward laughed and rattled his keys.
10148{_}1092{_}Then the wrath of Little John boiled over, and, lifting his clenched fist, he smote the pantry door, bursting out three panels and making so large an opening that he could easily stoop and walk through it.
10148{_}1093{_}When the Steward saw what was done, he waxed mad with rage; and, as Little John stooped to look within the pantry, he seized him from behind by the nape of the neck, pinching him sorely and smiting him over the head with his keys till the yeoman's ears rang again.
10148{_}1094{_}At this Little John turned upon the Steward and smote him such a buffet that the fat man fell to the floor and lay there as though he would never move again.
10148{_}1095{_}"There," quoth Little John, "think well of that stroke and never keep a good breakfast from a hungry man again."
10148{_}1096{_}So saying, he crept into the pantry and looked about him to see if he could find something to appease his hunger.
10148{_}1097{_}He saw a great venison pasty and two roasted capons, beside which was a platter of plover's eggs; moreover, there was a flask of sack and one of canary--a sweet sight to a hungry man.
10148{_}1098{_}These he took down from the shelves and placed upon a sideboard, and prepared to make himself merry.
10148{_}1099{_}Now the Cook, in the kitchen across the courtyard, heard the loud talking between Little John and the Steward, and also the blow that Little John struck the other, so he came running across the court and up the stairway to where the Steward's pantry was, bearing in his hands the spit with the roast still upon it.
10148{_}1100{_}Meanwhile the Steward had gathered his wits about him and risen to his feet, so that when the Cook came to the Steward's pantry he saw him glowering through the broken door at Little John, who was making ready for a good repast, as one dog glowers at another that has a bone.
10148{_}1101{_}When the Steward saw the Cook, he came to him, and, putting one arm over his shoulder, "Alas, sweet friend!"
10148{_}1102{_}quoth he--for the Cook was a tall, stout man--"seest thou what that vile knave Reynold Greenleaf hath done?
10148{_}1103{_}He hath broken in upon our master's goods, and hath smitten me a buffet upon the ear, so that I thought I was dead.
10148{_}1104{_}Good Cook, I love thee well, and thou shalt have a good pottle of our master's best wine every day, for thou art an old and faithful servant.
10148{_}1105{_}Also, good Cook, I have ten shillings that I mean to give as a gift to thee.
10148{_}1106{_}But hatest thou not to see a vile upstart like this Reynold Greenleaf taking it upon him so bravely?"
10148{_}1107{_}"Ay, marry, that do I," quoth the Cook boldly, for he liked the Steward because of his talk of the wine and of the ten shillings.
10148{_}1108{_}"Get thee gone straightway to thy room, and I will bring out this knave by his ears."
10148{_}1109{_}So saying, he laid aside his spit and drew the sword that hung by his side; whereupon the Steward left as quickly as he could, for he hated the sight of naked steel.
10148{_}1110{_}Then the Cook walked straightway to the broken pantry door, through which he saw Little John tucking a napkin beneath his chin and preparing to make himself merry.
10148{_}1111{_}"Why, how now, Reynold Greenleaf?"
10148{_}1112{_}said the Cook, "thou art no better than a thief, I wot.
10148{_}1113{_}Come thou straight forth, man, or I will carve thee as I would carve a sucking pig."
10148{_}1114{_}"Nay, good Cook, bear thou thyself more seemingly, or else I will come forth to thy dole.
10148{_}1115{_}At most times I am as a yearling lamb, but when one cometh between me and my meat, I am a raging lion, as it were."
10148{_}1116{_}"Lion or no lion," quoth the valorous Cook, "come thou straight forth, else thou art a coward heart as well as a knavish thief."
10148{_}1117{_}"Ha!"
10148{_}1118{_}cried Little John, "coward's name have I never had; so, look to thyself, good Cook, for I come forth straight, the roaring lion I did speak of but now."
10148{_}1119{_}Then he, too, drew his sword and came out of the pantry; then, putting themselves into position, they came slowly together, with grim and angry looks; but suddenly Little John lowered his point.
10148{_}1120{_}"Hold, good Cook!"
10148{_}1121{_}said he.
10148{_}1122{_}"Now, I bethink me it were ill of us to fight with good victuals standing so nigh, and such a feast as would befit two stout fellows such as we are.
10148{_}1123{_}Marry, good friend, I think we should enjoy this fair feast ere we fight.
10148{_}1124{_}What sayest thou, jolly Cook?"
10148{_}1125{_}At this speech the Cook looked up and down, scratching his head in doubt, for he loved good feasting.
10148{_}1126{_}At last he drew a long breath and said to Little John, "Well, good friend, I like thy plan right well; so, pretty boy, say I, let us feast, with all my heart, for one of us may sup in Paradise before nightfall."
10148{_}1127{_}So each thrust his sword back into the scabbard and entered the pantry.
10148{_}1128{_}Then, after they had seated themselves, Little John drew his dagger and thrust it into the pie.
10148{_}1129{_}"A hungry man must be fed," quoth he, "so, sweet chuck, I help myself without leave."
10148{_}1130{_}But the Cook did not lag far behind, for straightway his hands also were deeply thrust within the goodly pasty.
10148{_}1131{_}After this, neither of them spoke further, but used their teeth to better purpose.
10148{_}1132{_}But though neither spoke, they looked at one another, each thinking within himself that he had never seen a more lusty fellow than the one across the board.
10148{_}1133{_}At last, after a long time had passed, the Cook drew a full, deep breath, as though of much regret, and wiped his hands upon the napkin, for he could eat no more.
10148{_}1134{_}Little John, also, had enough, for he pushed the pasty aside, as though he would say, "I want thee by me no more, good friend."
10148{_}1135{_}Then he took the pottle of sack, and said he, "Now, good fellow, I swear by all that is bright, that thou art the stoutest companion at eating that ever I had.
10148{_}1136{_}Lo!
10148{_}1137{_}I drink thy health."
10148{_}1138{_}So saying, he clapped the flask to his lips and cast his eyes aloft, while the good wine flooded his throat.
10148{_}1139{_}Then he passed the pottle to the Cook, who also said, "Lo, I drink thy health, sweet fellow!"
10148{_}1140{_}Nor was he behind Little John in drinking any more than in eating.
10148{_}1141{_}"Now," quoth Little John, "thy voice is right round and sweet, jolly lad.
10148{_}1142{_}I doubt not thou canst sing a ballad most blithely; canst thou not?"
10148{_}1143{_}"Truly, I have trolled one now and then," quoth the Cook, "yet I would not sing alone."
10148{_}1144{_}"Nay, truly," said Little John, "that were but ill courtesy.
10148{_}1145{_}Strike up thy ditty, and I will afterward sing one to match it, if I can.
10148{_}1146{_}"So be it, pretty boy," quoth the Cook.
10148{_}1147{_}"And hast thou e'er heard the song of the Deserted Shepherdess?"
10148{_}1148{_}"Truly, I know not," answered Little John, "but sing thou and let me hear."
10148{_}1149{_}Then the Cook took another draught from the pottle, and, clearing his throat, sang right sweetly:  THE SONG OF THE DESERTED SHEPHERDESS   "_In Lententime, when leaves wax green,      And pretty birds begin to mate,  When lark cloth sing, and thrush, I ween,      And stockdove cooeth soon and late,  Fair Phillis sat beside a stone,  And thus I heard her make her moan:      'O willow, willow, willow, willow!
10148{_}1150{_}I'll take me of thy branches fair  And twine a wreath to deck my hair.
10148{_}1151{_}"'The thrush hath taken him a she,      The robin, too, and eke the dove;  My Robin hath deserted me,      And left me for another love.
10148{_}1152{_}So here, by brookside, all alone,  I sit me down and make my moan.
10148{_}1153{_}O willow, willow, willow, willow!
10148{_}1154{_}I'll take me of thy branches fair  And twine a wreath to deck my hair.'
10148{_}1155{_}"But ne'er came herring from the sea,      But good as he were in the tide;  Young Corydon came o'er the lea,      And sat him Phillis down beside.
10148{_}1156{_}So, presently, she changed her tone,  And 'gan to cease her from her moan,      'O willow, willow, willow, willow!
10148{_}1157{_}Thou mayst e'en keep thy garlands fair,  I want them not to deck my hair_.'"
10148{_}1158{_}"Now, by my faith," cried Little John, "that same is a right good song, and hath truth in it, also."
10148{_}1159{_}"Glad am I thou likest it, sweet lad," said the Cook.
10148{_}1160{_}"Now sing thou one also, for ne'er should a man be merry alone, or sing and list not."
10148{_}1161{_}"Then I will sing thee a song of a right good knight of Arthur's court, and how he cured his heart's wound without running upon the dart again, as did thy Phillis; for I wot she did but cure one smart by giving herself another.
10148{_}1162{_}So, list thou while I sing:  THE GOOD KNIGHT AND HIS LOVE   "_When Arthur, King, did rule this land,      A goodly king was he,  And had he of stout knights a band      Of merry company.
10148{_}1163{_}"Among them all, both great and small,      A good stout knight was there,  A lusty childe, and eke a tall,      That loved a lady fair.
10148{_}1164{_}"But nought would she to do with he,      But turned her face away;  So gat he gone to far countrye,      And left that lady gay.
