10148{_}1165{_}"There all alone he made his moan,      And eke did sob and sigh,  And weep till it would move a stone,      And he was like to die.
10148{_}1166{_}"But still his heart did feel the smart,      And eke the dire distress,  And rather grew his pain more sharp      As grew his body less.
10148{_}1167{_}"Then gat he back where was good sack      And merry com panye,  And soon did cease to cry 'Alack!'
10148{_}1168{_}When blithe and gay was he.
10148{_}1169{_}"From which I hold, and feel full bold      To say, and eke believe,  That gin the belly go not cold      The heart will cease to grieve_."
10148{_}1170{_}"Now, by my faith," cried the Cook, as he rattled the pottle against the sideboard, "I like that same song hugely, and eke the motive of it, which lieth like a sweet kernel in a hazelnut"  "Now thou art a man of shrewd opinions," quoth Little John, "and I love thee truly as thou wert my brother."
10148{_}1171{_}"And I love thee, too.
10148{_}1172{_}But the day draweth on, and I have my cooking to do ere our master cometh home; so let us e'en go and settle this brave fight we have in hand."
10148{_}1173{_}"Ay, marry," quoth Little John, "and that right speedily.
10148{_}1174{_}Never have I been more laggard in fighting than in eating and drinking.
10148{_}1175{_}So come thou straight forth into the passageway, where there is good room to swing a sword, and I will try to serve thee."
10148{_}1176{_}Then they both stepped forth into the broad passage that led to the Steward's pantry, where each man drew his sword again and without more ado fell upon the other as though he would hew his fellow limb from limb.
10148{_}1177{_}Then their swords clashed upon one another with great din, and sparks flew from each blow in showers.
10148{_}1178{_}So they fought up and down the hall for an hour and more, neither striking the other a blow, though they strove their best to do so; for both were skillful at the fence; so nothing came of all their labor.
10148{_}1179{_}Ever and anon they rested, panting; then, after getting their wind, at it they would go again more fiercely than ever.
10148{_}1180{_}At last Little John cried aloud, "Hold, good Cook!"
10148{_}1181{_}whereupon each rested upon his sword, panting.
10148{_}1182{_}"Now will I make my vow," quoth Little John, "thou art the very best swordsman that ever mine eyes beheld.
10148{_}1183{_}Truly, I had thought to carve thee ere now."
10148{_}1184{_}"And I had thought to do the same by thee," quoth the Cook, "but I have missed the mark somehow."
10148{_}1185{_}"Now I have been thinking within myself," quoth Little John, "what we are fighting for; but albeit I do not rightly know."
10148{_}1186{_}"Why, no more do I," said the Cook.
10148{_}1187{_}"I bear no love for that pursy Steward, but I thought that we had engaged to fight with one another and that it must be done."
10148{_}1188{_}"Now," quoth Little John, "it doth seem to me that instead of striving to cut one another's throats, it were better for us to be boon companions.
10148{_}1189{_}What sayst thou, jolly Cook, wilt thou go with me to Sherwood Forest and join with Robin Hood's band?
10148{_}1190{_}Thou shalt live a merry life within the woodlands, and sevenscore good companions shalt thou have, one of whom is mine own self.
10148{_}1191{_}Thou shalt have three suits of Lincoln green each year, and forty marks in pay."
10148{_}1192{_}"Now, thou art a man after mine own heart!"
10148{_}1193{_}cried the Cook right heartily, "and, as thou speakest of it, that is the very service for me.
10148{_}1194{_}I will go with thee, and that right gladly.
10148{_}1195{_}Give me thy palm, sweet fellow, and I will be thine own companion from henceforth.
10148{_}1196{_}What may be thy name, lad?"
10148{_}1197{_}"Men do call me Little John, good fellow."
10148{_}1198{_}"How?
10148{_}1199{_}And art thou indeed Little John, and Robin Hood's own right-hand man?
10148{_}1200{_}Many a time and oft I heard of thee, but never did I hope to set eyes upon thee.
10148{_}1201{_}And thou art indeed the famous Little John!"
10148{_}1202{_}And the Cook seemed lost in amazement, and looked upon his companion with open eyes.
10148{_}1203{_}"I am Little John, indeed, and I will bring to Robin Hood this day a right stout fellow to join his merry band.
10148{_}1204{_}But ere we go, good friend, it seemeth to me to be a vast pity that, as we have had so much of the Sheriff's food, we should not also carry off some of his silver plate to Robin Hood, as a present from his worship."
10148{_}1205{_}"Ay, marry is it," said the Cook.
10148{_}1206{_}And so they began hunting about, and took as much silver as they could lay hands upon, clapping it into a bag, and when they had filled the sack they set forth to Sherwood Forest.
10148{_}1207{_}Plunging into the woods, they came at last to the greenwood tree, where they found Robin Hood and threescore of his merry men lying upon the fresh green grass.
10148{_}1208{_}When Robin and his men saw who it was that came, they leaped to their feet.
10148{_}1209{_}"Now welcome!"
10148{_}1210{_}cried Robin Hood.
10148{_}1211{_}"Now welcome, Little John!
10148{_}1212{_}For long hath it been since we have heard from thee, though we all knew that thou hadst joined the Sheriff's service.
10148{_}1213{_}And how hast thou fared all these long days?"
10148{_}1214{_}"Right merrily have I lived at the Lord Sheriff's," answered Little John, "and I have come straight thence.
10148{_}1215{_}See, good master!
10148{_}1216{_}I have brought thee his cook, and even his silver plate."
10148{_}1217{_}Thereupon he told Robin Hood and his merry men that were there, all that had befallen him since he had left them to go to the Fair at Nottingham Town.
10148{_}1218{_}Then all shouted with laughter, except Robin Hood; but he looked grave.
10148{_}1219{_}"Nay, Little John," said he, "thou art a brave blade and a trusty fellow.
10148{_}1220{_}I am glad thou hast brought thyself back to us, and with such a good companion as the Cook, whom we all welcome to Sherwood.
10148{_}1221{_}But I like not so well that thou hast stolen the Sheriff's plate like some paltry thief.
10148{_}1222{_}The Sheriff hath been punished by us, and hath lost three hundred pounds, even as he sought to despoil another; but he hath done nought that we should steal his household plate from him."
10148{_}1223{_}Though Little John was vexed with this, he strove to pass it off with a jest.
10148{_}1224{_}"Nay, good master," quoth he, "if thou thinkest the Sheriff gave us not the plate, I will fetch him, that he may tell us with his own lips he giveth it all to us."
10148{_}1225{_}So saying he leaped to his feet, and was gone before Robin could call him back.
10148{_}1226{_}Little John ran for full five miles till he came to where the Sheriff of Nottingham and a gay company were hunting near the forest.
10148{_}1227{_}When Little John came to the Sheriff he doffed his cap and bent his knee.
10148{_}1228{_}"God save thee, good master," quoth he.
10148{_}1229{_}"Why, Reynold Greenleaf!"
10148{_}1230{_}cried the Sheriff, "whence comest thou and where hast thou been?"
10148{_}1231{_}"I have been in the forest," answered Little John, speaking amazedly, "and there I saw a sight such as ne'er before man's eyes beheld!
10148{_}1232{_}Yonder I saw a young hart all in green from top to toe, and about him was a herd of threescore deer, and they, too, were all of green from head to foot.
10148{_}1233{_}Yet I dared not shoot, good master, for fear lest they should slay me."
10148{_}1234{_}"Why, how now, Reynold Greenleaf," cried the Sheriff, "art thou dreaming or art thou mad, that thou dost bring me such, a tale?"
10148{_}1235{_}"Nay, I am not dreaming nor am I mad," said Little John, "and if thou wilt come with me, I will show thee this fair sight, for I have seen it with mine own eyes.
10148{_}1236{_}But thou must come alone, good master, lest the others frighten them and they get away."
10148{_}1237{_}So the party all rode forward, and Little John led them downward into the forest.
10148{_}1238{_}"Now, good master," quoth he at last, "we are nigh where I saw this herd."
10148{_}1239{_}Then the Sheriff descended from his horse and bade them wait for him until he should return; and Little John led him forward through a close copse until suddenly they came to a great open glade, at the end of which Robin Hood sat beneath the shade of the great oak tree, with his merry men all about him.
10148{_}1240{_}"See, good Master Sheriff," quoth Little John, "yonder is the hart of which I spake to thee."
10148{_}1241{_}At this the Sheriff turned to Little John and said bitterly, "Long ago I thought I remembered thy face, but now I know thee.
10148{_}1242{_}Woe betide thee, Little John, for thou hast betrayed me this day."
10148{_}1243{_}In the meantime Robin Hood had come to them.
10148{_}1244{_}"Now welcome, Master Sheriff," said he.
10148{_}1245{_}"Hast thou come today to take another feast with me?"
10148{_}1246{_}"Nay, Heaven forbid!"
10148{_}1247{_}said the Sheriff in tones of deep earnest.
10148{_}1248{_}"I care for no feast and have no hunger today."
10148{_}1249{_}"Nevertheless," quoth Robin, "if thou hast no hunger, maybe thou hast thirst, and well I know thou wilt take a cup of sack with me.
10148{_}1250{_}But I am grieved that thou wilt not feast with me, for thou couldst have victuals to thy liking, for there stands thy Cook."
10148{_}1251{_}Then he led the Sheriff, willy-nilly, to the seat he knew so well beneath the greenwood tree.
10148{_}1252{_}"Ho, lads!"
10148{_}1253{_}cried Robin, "fill our good friend the Sheriff a right brimming cup of sack and fetch it hither, for he is faint and weary."
10148{_}1254{_}Then one of the band brought the Sheriff a cup of sack, bowing low as he handed it to him; but the Sheriff could not touch the wine, for he saw it served in one of his own silver flagons, on one of his own silver plates.
10148{_}1255{_}"How now," quoth Robin, "dost thou not like our new silver service?
10148{_}1256{_}We have gotten a bag of it this day."
10148{_}1257{_}So saying, he held up the sack of silver that Little John and the Cook had brought with them.
10148{_}1258{_}Then the Sheriff's heart was bitter within him; but, not daring to say anything, he only gazed upon the ground.
10148{_}1259{_}Robin looked keenly at him for a time before he spoke again.
10148{_}1260{_}Then said he, "Now, Master Sheriff, the last time thou camest to Sherwood Forest thou didst come seeking to despoil a poor spendthrift, and thou wert despoiled thine own self; but now thou comest seeking to do no harm, nor do I know that thou hast despoiled any man.
10148{_}1261{_}I take my tithes from fat priests and lordly squires, to help those that they despoil and to raise up those that they bow down; but I know not that thou hast tenants of thine own whom thou hast wronged in any way.
10148{_}1262{_}Therefore, take thou thine own again, nor will I dispossess thee today of so much as one farthing.
10148{_}1263{_}Come with me, and I will lead thee from the forest back to thine own party again."
10148{_}1264{_}Then, slinging the bag upon his shoulder, he turned away, the Sheriff following him, all too perplexed in mind to speak.
10148{_}1265{_}So they went forward until they came to within a furlong of the spot where the Sheriff's companions were waiting for him.
10148{_}1266{_}Then Robin Hood gave the sack of silver back to the Sheriff.
10148{_}1267{_}"Take thou thine own again," he said, "and hearken to me, good Sheriff, take thou a piece of advice with it.
10148{_}1268{_}Try thy servants well ere thou dost engage them again so readily."
10148{_}1269{_}Then, turning, he left the other standing bewildered, with the sack in his hands.
10148{_}1270{_}The company that waited for the Sheriff were all amazed to see him come out of the forest bearing a heavy sack upon his shoulders; but though they questioned him, he answered never a word, acting like one who walks in a dream.
10148{_}1271{_}Without a word, he placed the bag across his nag's back and then, mounting, rode away, all following him; but all the time there was a great turmoil of thoughts within his head, tumbling one over the other.
10148{_}1272{_}And thus ends the merry tale of Little John and how he entered the Sheriff's service.
10148{_}1273{_}Little John and the Tanner of Blyth  ONE FINE DAY, not long after Little John had left abiding with the Sheriff and had come back, with his worship's cook, to the merry greenwood, as has just been told, Robin Hood and a few chosen fellows of his band lay upon the soft sward beneath the greenwood tree where they dwelled.
10148{_}1274{_}The day was warm and sultry, so that while most of the band were scattered through the forest upon this mission and upon that, these few stout fellows lay lazily beneath the shade of the tree, in the soft afternoon, passing jests among themselves and telling merry stories, with laughter and mirth.
10148{_}1275{_}All the air was laden with the bitter fragrance of the May, and all the bosky shades of the woodlands beyond rang with the sweet song of birds-- the throstle cock, the cuckoo, and the wood pigeon--and with the song of birds mingled the cool sound of the gurgling brook that leaped out of the forest shades, and ran fretting amid its rough, gray stones across the sunlit open glade before the trysting tree.
10148{_}1276{_}And a fair sight was that halfscore of tall, stout yeomen, all clad in Lincoln green, lying beneath the broad-spreading branches of the great oak tree, amid the quivering leaves of which the sunlight shivered and fell in dancing patches upon the grass.
10148{_}1277{_}Suddenly Robin Hood smote his knee.
10148{_}1278{_}"By Saint Dunstan," quoth he, "I had nigh forgot that quarter-day cometh on apace, and yet no cloth of Lincoln green in all our store.
10148{_}1279{_}It must be looked to, and that in quick season.
10148{_}1280{_}Come, busk thee, Little John!
10148{_}1281{_}Stir those lazy bones of thine, for thou must get thee straightway to our good gossip, the draper Hugh Longshanks of Ancaster.
10148{_}1282{_}Bid him send us straightway twentyscore yards of fair cloth of Lincoln green; and mayhap the journey may take some of the fat from off thy bones, that thou hast gotten from lazy living at our dear Sheriff's."
10148{_}1283{_}"Nay," muttered Little John (for he had heard so much upon this score that he was sore upon the point), "nay, truly, mayhap I have more flesh upon my joints than I once had, yet, flesh or no flesh, I doubt not that I could still hold my place and footing upon a narrow bridge against e'er a yeoman in Sherwood, or Nottinghamshire, for the matter of that, even though he had no more fat about his bones than thou hast, good master."
10148{_}1284{_}At this reply a great shout of laughter went up, and all looked at Robin Hood, for each man knew that Little John spake of a certain fight that happened between their master and himself, through which they first became acquainted.
10148{_}1285{_}"Nay," quoth Robin Hood, laughing louder than all.
10148{_}1286{_}"Heaven forbid that I should doubt thee, for I care for no taste of thy staff myself, Little John.
10148{_}1287{_}I must needs own that there are those of my band can handle a seven-foot staff more deftly than I; yet no man in all Nottinghamshire can draw gray goose shaft with my fingers.
10148{_}1288{_}Nevertheless, a journey to Ancaster may not be ill for thee; so go thou, as I bid, and thou hadst best go this very evening, for since thou hast abided at the Sheriff's many know thy face, and if thou goest in broad daylight, thou mayst get thyself into a coil with some of his worship's men-at-arms.
10148{_}1289{_}Bide thou here till I bring thee money to pay our good Hugh.
10148{_}1290{_}I warrant he hath no better customers in all Nottinghamshire than we."
10148{_}1291{_}So saying, Robin left them and entered the forest.
10148{_}1292{_}Not far from the trysting tree was a great rock in which a chamber had been hewn, the entrance being barred by a massive oaken door two palms'- breadth in thickness, studded about with spikes, and fastened with a great padlock.
10148{_}1293{_}This was the treasure house of the band, and thither Robin Hood went and, unlocking the door, entered the chamber, from which he brought forth a bag of gold which he gave to Little John, to pay Hugh Longshanks withal, for the cloth of Lincoln green.
10148{_}1294{_}Then up got Little John, and, taking the bag of gold, which he thrust into his bosom, he strapped a girdle about his loins, took a stout pikestaff full seven feet long in his hand, and set forth upon his journey.
10148{_}1295{_}So he strode whistling along the leafy forest path that led to Fosse Way, turning neither to the right hand nor the left, until at last he came to where the path branched, leading on the one hand onward to Fosse Way, and on the other, as well Little John knew, to the merry Blue Boar Inn.
10148{_}1296{_}Here Little John suddenly ceased whistling and stopped in the middle of the path.
10148{_}1297{_}First he looked up and then he looked down, and then, tilting his cap over one eye, he slowly scratched the back part of his head.
10148{_}1298{_}For thus it was:  at the sight of these two roads, two voices began to alarum within him, the one crying, "There lies the road to the Blue Boar Inn, a can of brown October, and a merry night with sweet companions such as thou mayst find there"; the other, "There lies the way to Ancaster and the duty thou art sent upon."
10148{_}1299{_}Now the first of these two voices was far the louder, for Little John had grown passing fond of good living through abiding at the Sheriff's house; so, presently, looking up into the blue sky, across which bright clouds were sailing like silver boats, and swallows skimming in circling flight, quoth he, "I fear me it will rain this evening, so I'll e'en stop at the Blue Boar till it passes by, for I know my good master would not have me wet to the skin."
10148{_}1300{_}So, without more ado, off he strode down the path that lay the way of his likings.
10148{_}1301{_}Now there was no sign of any foul weather, but when one wishes to do a thing, as Little John did, one finds no lack of reasons for the doing.
10148{_}1302{_}Four merry wags were at the Blue Boar Inn; a butcher, a beggar, and two barefoot friars.
10148{_}1303{_}Little John heard them singing from afar, as he walked through the hush of the mellow twilight that was now falling over hill and dale.
10148{_}1304{_}Right glad were they to welcome such a merry blade as Little John.
10148{_}1305{_}Fresh cans of ale were brought, and with jest and song and merry tales the hours slipped away on fleeting wings.
10148{_}1306{_}None thought of time or tide till the night was so far gone that Little John put by the thought of setting forth upon his journey again that night, and so bided at the Blue Boar Inn until the morrow.
10148{_}1307{_}Now it was an ill piece of luck for Little John that he left his duty for his pleasure, and he paid a great score for it, as we are all apt to do in the same case, as you shall see.
10148{_}1308{_}Up he rose at the dawn of the next day, and, taking his stout pikestaff in his hand, he set forth upon his journey once more, as though he would make up for lost time.
10148{_}1309{_}In the good town of Blyth there lived a stout tanner, celebrated far and near for feats of strength and many tough bouts at wrestling and the quarterstaff.
10148{_}1310{_}For five years he had held the mid-country champion belt for wrestling, till the great Adam o' Lincoln cast him in the ring and broke one of his ribs; but at quarterstaff he had never yet met his match in all the country about.
10148{_}1311{_}Besides all this, he dearly loved the longbow, and a sly jaunt in the forest when the moon was full and the dun deer in season; so that the King's rangers kept a shrewd eye upon him and his doings, for Arthur a Bland's house was apt to have aplenty of meat in it that was more like venison than the law allowed.
10148{_}1312{_}Now Arthur had been to Nottingham Town the day before Little John set forth on his errand, there to sell a halfscore of tanned cowhides.
10148{_}1313{_}At the dawn of the same day that Little John left the inn, he started from Nottingham, homeward for Blyth.
10148{_}1314{_}His way led, all in the dewy morn, past the verge of Sherwood Forest, where the birds were welcoming the lovely day with a great and merry jubilee.
10148{_}1315{_}Across the Tanner's shoulders was slung his stout quarterstaff, ever near enough to him to be gripped quickly, and on his head was a cap of doubled cowhide, so tough that it could hardly be cloven even by a broadsword.
10148{_}1316{_}"Now," quoth Arthur a Bland to himself, when he had come to that part of the road that cut through a corner of the forest, "no doubt at this time of year the dun deer are coming from the forest depths nigher to the open meadow lands.
10148{_}1317{_}Mayhap I may chance to catch a sight of the dainty brown darlings thus early in the morn."
10148{_}1318{_}For there was nothing he loved better than to look upon a tripping herd of deer, even when he could not tickle their ribs with a clothyard shaft.
10148{_}1319{_}Accordingly, quitting the path, he went peeping this way and that through the underbrush, spying now here and now there, with all the wiles of a master of woodcraft, and of one who had more than once donned a doublet of Lincoln green.
10148{_}1320{_}Now as Little John stepped blithely along, thinking of nothing but of such things as the sweetness of the hawthorn buds that bedecked the hedgerows, or gazing upward at the lark, that, springing from the dewy grass, hung aloft on quivering wings in the yellow sunlight, pouring forth its song that fell like a falling star from the sky, his luck led him away from the highway, not far from the spot where Arthur a Bland was peeping this way and that through the leaves of the thickets.
10148{_}1321{_}Hearing a rustling of the branches, Little John stopped and presently caught sight of the brown cowhide cap of the Tanner moving among the bushes.
10148{_}1322{_}"I do much wonder," quoth Little John to himself, "what yon knave is after, that he should go thus peeping and peering about I verily believe that yon scurvy varlet is no better than a thief, and cometh here after our own and the good King's dun deer."
10148{_}1323{_}For by much roving in the forest, Little John had come to look upon all the deer in Sherwood as belonging to Robin Hood and his band as much as to good King Harry.
10148{_}1324{_}"Nay," quoth he again, after a time, "this matter must e'en be looked into."
10148{_}1325{_}So, quitting the highroad, he also entered the thickets, and began spying around after stout Arthur a Bland.
10148{_}1326{_}So for a long time they both of them went hunting about, Little John after the Tanner, and the Tanner after the deer.
10148{_}1327{_}At last Little John trod upon a stick, which snapped under his foot, whereupon, hearing the noise, the Tanner turned quickly and caught sight of the yeoman.
10148{_}1328{_}Seeing that the Tanner had spied him out, Little John put a bold face upon the matter.
10148{_}1329{_}"Hilloa," quoth he, "what art thou doing here, thou naughty fellow?
10148{_}1330{_}Who art thou that comest ranging Sherwood's paths?
10148{_}1331{_}In very sooth thou hast an evil cast of countenance, and I do think, truly, that thou art no better than a thief, and comest after our good King's deer."
10148{_}1332{_}"Nay," quoth the Tanner boldly--for, though taken by surprise, he was not a man to be frightened by big words--"thou liest in thy teeth.
10148{_}1333{_}I am no thief, but an honest craftsman.
10148{_}1334{_}As for my countenance, it is what it is; and, for the matter of that, thine own is none too pretty, thou saucy fellow."
10148{_}1335{_}"Ha!"
10148{_}1336{_}quoth Little John in a great loud voice, "wouldst thou give me backtalk?
10148{_}1337{_}Now I have a great part of a mind to crack thy pate for thee.
10148{_}1338{_}I would have thee know, fellow, that I am, as it were, one of the King's foresters.
10148{_}1339{_}Leastwise," muttered he to himself, "I and my friends do take good care of our good sovereign's deer."
10148{_}1340{_}"I care not who thou art," answered the bold Tanner, "and unless thou hast many more of thy kind by thee, thou canst never make Arthur a Bland cry 'A mercy.'"
10148{_}1341{_}"Is it so?"
10148{_}1342{_}cried Little John in a rage.
10148{_}1343{_}"Now, by my faith, thou saucy rogue, thy tongue hath led thee into a pit thou wilt have a sorry time getting out of; for I will give thee such a drubbing as ne'er hast thou had in all thy life before.
10148{_}1344{_}Take thy staff in thy hand, fellow, for I will not smite an unarmed man.
10148{_}1345{_}"Marry come up with a murrain!"
10148{_}1346{_}cried the Tanner, for he, too, had talked himself into a fume.
10148{_}1347{_}"Big words ne'er killed so much as a mouse.
10148{_}1348{_}Who art thou that talkest so freely of cracking the head of Arthur a Bland?
10148{_}1349{_}If I do not tan thy hide this day as ne'er I tanned a calf's hide in all my life before, split my staff into skewers for lamb's flesh and call me no more brave man!
10148{_}1350{_}Now look to thyself, fellow!"
10148{_}1351{_}"Stay!"
10148{_}1352{_}said Little John.
10148{_}1353{_}"Let us first measure our cudgels.
10148{_}1354{_}I do reckon my staff longer than thine, and I would not take vantage of thee by even so much as an inch."
10148{_}1355{_}"Nay, I pass not for length," answered the Tanner.
10148{_}1356{_}"My staff is long enough to knock down a calf; so look to thyself, fellow, I say again."
10148{_}1357{_}So, without more ado, each gripped his staff in the middle, and, with fell and angry looks, they came slowly together.
10148{_}1358{_}Now news had been brought to Robin Hood how that Little John, instead of doing his bidding, had passed by duty for pleasure, and so had stopped overnight with merry company at the Blue Boar Inn, instead of going straight to Ancaster.
10148{_}1359{_}So, being vexed to his heart by this, he set forth at dawn of day to seek Little John at the Blue Boar, or at least to meet the yeoman on the way, and ease his heart of what he thought of the matter.
10148{_}1360{_}As thus he strode along in anger, putting together the words he would use to chide Little John, he heard, of a sudden, loud and angry voices, as of men in a rage, passing fell words back and forth from one to the other.
10148{_}1361{_}At this, Robin Hood stopped and listened.
10148{_}1362{_}"Surely," quoth he to himself, "that is Little John's voice, and he is talking in anger also.
10148{_}1363{_}Methinks the other is strange to my ears.
10148{_}1364{_}Now Heaven forfend that my good trusty Little John should have fallen into the hands of the King's rangers.
10148{_}1365{_}I must see to this matter, and that quickly."
10148{_}1366{_}Thus spoke Robin Hood to himself, all his anger passing away like a breath from the windowpane, at the thought that perhaps his trusty right-hand man was in some danger of his life.
10148{_}1367{_}So cautiously he made his way through the thickets whence the voices came, and, pushing aside the leaves, peeped into the little open space where the two men, staff in hand, were coming slowly together.
10148{_}1368{_}"Ha!"
10148{_}1369{_}quoth Robin to himself, "here is merry sport afoot.
10148{_}1370{_}Now I would give three golden angels from my own pocket if yon stout fellow would give Little John a right sound drubbing!
10148{_}1371{_}It would please me to see him well thumped for having failed in my bidding.
10148{_}1372{_}I fear me, though, there is but poor chance of my seeing such a pleasant sight."
10148{_}1373{_}So saying, he stretched himself at length upon the ground, that he might not only see the sport the better, but that he might enjoy the merry sight at his ease.
10148{_}1374{_}As you may have seen two dogs that think to fight, walking slowly round and round each other, neither cur wishing to begin the combat, so those two stout yeomen moved slowly around, each watching for a chance to take the other unaware, and so get in the first blow.
10148{_}1375{_}At last Little John struck like a flash, and--"rap!"
10148{_}1376{_}--the Tanner met the blow and turned it aside, and then smote back at Little John, who also turned the blow; and so this mighty battle began.
10148{_}1377{_}Then up and down and back and forth they trod, the blows falling so thick and fast that, at a distance, one would have thought that half a score of men were fighting.
10148{_}1378{_}Thus they fought for nigh a half an hour, until the ground was all plowed up with the digging of their heels, and their breathing grew labored like the ox in the furrow.
10148{_}1379{_}But Little John suffered the most, for he had become unused to such stiff labor, and his joints were not as supple as they had been before he went to dwell with the Sheriff.
10148{_}1380{_}All this time Robin Hood lay beneath the bush, rejoicing at such a comely bout of quarterstaff.
10148{_}1381{_}"By my faith!"
10148{_}1382{_}quoth he to himself, "never had I thought to see Little John so evenly matched in all my life.
10148{_}1383{_}Belike, though, he would have overcome yon fellow before this had he been in his former trim."
10148{_}1384{_}At last Little John saw his chance, and, throwing all the strength he felt going from him into one blow that might have felled an ox, he struck at the Tanner with might and main.
10148{_}1385{_}And now did the Tanner's cowhide cap stand him in good stead, and but for it he might never have held staff in hand again.
10148{_}1386{_}As it was, the blow he caught beside the head was so shrewd that it sent him staggering across the little glade, so that, if Little John had had the strength to follow up his vantage, it would have been ill for stout Arthur.
10148{_}1387{_}But he regained himself quickly and, at arm's length, struck back a blow at Little John, and this time the stroke reached its mark, and down went Little John at full length, his cudgel flying from his hand as he fell.
10148{_}1388{_}Then, raising his staff, stout Arthur dealt him another blow upon the ribs.
10148{_}1389{_}"Hold!"
10148{_}1390{_}roared Little John.
10148{_}1391{_}"Wouldst thou strike a man when he is down?"
10148{_}1392{_}"Ay, marry would I," quoth the Tanner, giving him another thwack with his staff.
10148{_}1393{_}"Stop!"
10148{_}1394{_}roared Little John.
10148{_}1395{_}"Help!
10148{_}1396{_}Hold, I say!
10148{_}1397{_}I yield me!
10148{_}1398{_}I yield me, I say, good fellow!"
10148{_}1399{_}"Hast thou had enough?"
10148{_}1400{_}asked the Tanner grimly, holding his staff aloft.
10148{_}1401{_}"Ay, marry, and more than enough."
10148{_}1402{_}"And thou dost own that I am the better man of the two?"
10148{_}1403{_}"Yea, truly, and a murrain seize thee!"
10148{_}1404{_}said Little John, the first aloud and the last to his beard.
10148{_}1405{_}"Then thou mayst go thy ways; and thank thy patron saint that I am a merciful man," said the Tanner.
10148{_}1406{_}"A plague o' such mercy as thine!"
10148{_}1407{_}said Little John, sitting up and feeling his ribs where the Tanner had cudgeled him.
10148{_}1408{_}"I make my vow, my ribs feel as though every one of them were broken in twain.
10148{_}1409{_}I tell thee, good fellow, I did think there was never a man in all Nottinghamshire could do to me what thou hast done this day."
10148{_}1410{_}"And so thought I, also," cried Robin Hood, bursting out of the thicket and shouting with laughter till the tears ran down his cheeks.
10148{_}1411{_}"O man, man!"
10148{_}1412{_}said he, as well as he could for his mirth, "'a didst go over like a bottle knocked from a wall.
10148{_}1413{_}I did see the whole merry bout, and never did I think to see thee yield thyself so, hand and foot, to any man in all merry England.
10148{_}1414{_}I was seeking thee, to chide thee for leaving my bidding undone; but thou hast been paid all I owed thee, full measure, pressed down and overflowing, by this good fellow.
10148{_}1415{_}Marry, 'a did reach out his arm full length while thou stood gaping at him, and, with a pretty rap, tumbled thee over as never have I seen one tumbled before."
10148{_}1416{_}So spoke bold Robin, and all the time Little John sat upon the ground, looking as though he had sour curds in his mouth.
10148{_}1417{_}"What may be thy name, good fellow?"
10148{_}1418{_}said Robin, next, turning to the Tanner.
10148{_}1419{_}"Men do call me Arthur a Bland," spoke up the Tanner boldly, "and now what may be thy name?"
10148{_}1420{_}"Ha, Arthur a Bland!"
10148{_}1421{_}quoth Robin, "I have heard thy name before, good fellow.
10148{_}1422{_}Thou didst break the crown of a friend of mine at the fair at Ely last October.
10148{_}1423{_}The folk there call him Jock o' Nottingham; we call him Will Scathelock.
10148{_}1424{_}This poor fellow whom thou hast so belabored is counted the best hand at the quarterstaff in all merry England.
10148{_}1425{_}His name is Little John, and mine Robin Hood."
10148{_}1426{_}"How!"
10148{_}1427{_}cried the Tanner, "art thou indeed the great Robin Hood, and is this the famous Little John?
10148{_}1428{_}Marry, had I known who thou art, I would never have been so bold as to lift my hand against thee.
10148{_}1429{_}Let me help thee to thy feet, good Master Little John, and let me brush the dust from off thy coat."
10148{_}1430{_}"Nay," quoth Little John testily, at the same time rising carefully, as though his bones had been made of glass, "I can help myself, good fellow, without thy aid; and let me tell thee, had it not been for that vile cowskin cap of thine, it would have been ill for thee this day."
10148{_}1431{_}At this Robin laughed again, and, turning to the Tanner, he said, "Wilt thou join my band, good Arthur?
10148{_}1432{_}For I make my vow thou art one of the stoutest men that ever mine eyes beheld."
10148{_}1433{_}"Will I join thy band?"
10148{_}1434{_}cried the Tanner joyfully.
10148{_}1435{_}"Ay, marry, will I!
10148{_}1436{_}Hey for a merry life!"
10148{_}1437{_}cried he, leaping aloft and snapping his fingers, "and hey for the life I love!
10148{_}1438{_}Away with tanbark and filthy vats and foul cowhides!
10148{_}1439{_}I will follow thee to the ends of the earth, good master, and not a herd of dun deer in all the forest but shall know the sound of the twang of my bowstring."
10148{_}1440{_}"As for thee, Little John," said Robin, turning to him and laughing, "thou wilt start once more for Ancaster, and we will go part way with thee, for I will not have thee turn again to either the right hand or the left till thou hast fairly gotten away from Sherwood.
10148{_}1441{_}There are other inns that thou knowest yet, hereabouts."
10148{_}1442{_}Thereupon, leaving the thickets, they took once more to the highway and departed upon their business.
10148{_}1443{_}Robin Hood and Will Scarlet  THUS THEY traveled along the sunny road, three stout fellows such as you could hardly match anywhere else in all merry England.
10148{_}1444{_}Many stopped to gaze after them as they strode along, so broad were their shoulders and so sturdy their gait.
10148{_}1445{_}Quoth Robin Hood to Little John, "Why didst thou not go straight to Ancaster, yesterday, as I told thee?
10148{_}1446{_}Thou hadst not gotten thyself into such a coil hadst thou done as I ordered."
10148{_}1447{_}"I feared the rain that threatened," said Little John in a sullen tone, for he was vexed at being so chaffed by Robin with what had happened to him.
10148{_}1448{_}"The rain!"
10148{_}1449{_}cried Robin, stopping of a sudden in the middle of the road, and looking at Little John in wonder.
10148{_}1450{_}"Why, thou great oaf!
10148{_}1451{_}not a drop of rain has fallen these three days, neither has any threatened, nor hath there been a sign of foul weather in earth or sky or water."
10148{_}1452{_}"Nevertheless," growled Little John, "the holy Saint Swithin holdeth the waters of the heavens in his pewter pot, and he could have poured them out, had he chosen, even from a clear sky; and wouldst thou have had me wet to the skin?"
10148{_}1453{_}At this Robin Hood burst into a roar of laughter.
10148{_}1454{_}"O Little John!"
10148{_}1455{_}said he, "what butter wits hast thou in that head of thine!
10148{_}1456{_}Who could hold anger against such a one as thou art?"
10148{_}1457{_}So saying, they all stepped out once more, with the right foot foremost, as the saying is.
10148{_}1458{_}After they had traveled some distance, the day being warm and the road dusty, Robin Hood waxed thirsty; so, there being a fountain of water as cold as ice, just behind the hedgerow, they crossed the stile and came to where the water bubbled up from beneath a mossy stone.
10148{_}1459{_}Here, kneeling and making cups of the palms of their hands, they drank their fill, and then, the spot being cool and shady, they stretched their limbs and rested them for a space.
10148{_}1460{_}In front of them, over beyond the hedge, the dusty road stretched away across the plain; behind them the meadow lands and bright green fields of tender young corn lay broadly in the sun, and overhead spread the shade of the cool, rustling leaves of the beechen tree.
10148{_}1461{_}Pleasantly to their nostrils came the tender fragrance of the purple violets and wild thyme that grew within the dewy moisture of the edge of the little fountain, and pleasantly came the soft gurgle of the water.
10148{_}1462{_}All was so pleasant and so full of the gentle joy of the bright Maytime, that for a long time no one of the three cared to speak, but each lay on his back, gazing up through the trembling leaves of the trees to the bright sky overhead.
10148{_}1463{_}At last, Robin, whose thoughts were not quite so busy wool- gathering as those of the others, and who had been gazing around him now and then, broke the silence.
10148{_}1464{_}"Heyday!"
10148{_}1465{_}quoth he, "yon is a gaily feathered bird, I take my vow."
10148{_}1466{_}The others looked and saw a young man walking slowly down the highway.
10148{_}1467{_}Gay was he, indeed, as Robin had said, and a fine figure he cut, for his doublet was of scarlet silk and his stockings also; a handsome sword hung by his side, the embossed leathern scabbard being picked out with fine threads of gold; his cap was of scarlet velvet, and a broad feather hung down behind and back of one ear.
10148{_}1468{_}His hair was long and yellow and curled upon his shoulders, and in his hand he bore an early rose, which he smelled at daintily now and then.
10148{_}1469{_}"By my life!"
10148{_}1470{_}quoth Robin Hood, laughing, "saw ye e'er such a pretty, mincing fellow?"
10148{_}1471{_}"Truly, his clothes have overmuch prettiness for my taste," quoth Arthur a Bland, "but, ne'ertheless, his shoulders are broad and his loins are narrow, and seest thou, good master, how that his arms hang from his body?
10148{_}1472{_}They dangle not down like spindles, but hang stiff and bend at the elbow.
10148{_}1473{_}I take my vow, there be no bread and milk limbs in those fine clothes, but stiff joints and tough thews."
10148{_}1474{_}"Methinks thou art right, friend Arthur," said Little John.
10148{_}1475{_}"I do verily think that yon is no such roseleaf and whipped-cream gallant as he would have one take him to be."
10148{_}1476{_}"Pah!"
10148{_}1477{_}quoth Robin Hood, "the sight of such a fellow doth put a nasty taste into my mouth!
10148{_}1478{_}Look how he doth hold that fair flower betwixt his thumb and finger, as he would say, 'Good rose, I like thee not so ill but I can bear thy odor for a little while.'
10148{_}1479{_}I take it ye are both wrong, and verily believe that were a furious mouse to run across his path, he would cry, 'La!'
10148{_}1480{_}or 'Alack-a-day!'
10148{_}1481{_}and fall straightway into a swoon.
10148{_}1482{_}I wonder who he may be."
10148{_}1483{_}"Some great baron's son, I doubt not," answered Little John, "with good and true men's money lining his purse."
10148{_}1484{_}"Ay, marry, that is true, I make no doubt," quoth Robin.
10148{_}1485{_}"What a pity that such men as he, that have no thought but to go abroad in gay clothes, should have good fellows, whose shoes they are not fit to tie, dancing at their bidding.
10148{_}1486{_}By Saint Dunstan, Saint Alfred, Saint Withold, and all the good men in the Saxon calendar, it doth make me mad to see such gay lordlings from over the sea go stepping on the necks of good Saxons who owned this land before ever their great-grandsires chewed rind of brawn!
10148{_}1487{_}By the bright bow of Heaven, I will have their ill-gotten gains from them, even though I hang for it as high as e'er a forest tree in Sherwood!"
10148{_}1488{_}"Why, how now, master," quoth Little John, "what heat is this?
10148{_}1489{_}Thou dost set thy pot a-boiling, and mayhap no bacon to cook!
10148{_}1490{_}Methinks yon fellow's hair is overlight for Norman locks.
10148{_}1491{_}He may be a good man and true for aught thou knowest."
10148{_}1492{_}"Nay," said Robin, "my head against a leaden farthing, he is what I say.
10148{_}1493{_}So, lie ye both here, I say, till I show you how I drub this fellow."
10148{_}1494{_}So saying, Robin Hood stepped forth from the shade of the beech tree, crossed the stile, and stood in the middle of the road, with his hands on his hips, in the stranger's path.
10148{_}1495{_}Meantime the stranger, who had been walking so slowly that all this talk was held before he came opposite the place where they were, neither quickened his pace nor seemed to see that such a man as Robin Hood was in the world.
10148{_}1496{_}So Robin stood in the middle of the road, waiting while the other walked slowly forward, smelling his rose, and looking this way and that, and everywhere except at Robin.
10148{_}1497{_}"Hold!"
10148{_}1498{_}cried Robin, when at last the other had come close to him.
10148{_}1499{_}"Hold!
10148{_}1500{_}Stand where thou art!"
10148{_}1501{_}"Wherefore should I hold, good fellow?"
10148{_}1502{_}said the stranger in soft and gentle voice.
10148{_}1503{_}"And wherefore should I stand where I am?
10148{_}1504{_}Ne'ertheless, as thou dost desire that I should stay, I will abide for a short time, that I may hear what thou mayst have to say to me."
10148{_}1505{_}"Then," quoth Robin, "as thou dost so fairly do as I tell thee, and dost give me such soft speech, I will also treat thee with all due courtesy.
10148{_}1506{_}I would have thee know, fair friend, that I am, as it were, a votary at the shrine of Saint Wilfred who, thou mayst know, took, willy-nilly, all their gold from the heathen, and melted it up into candlesticks.
10148{_}1507{_}Wherefore, upon such as come hereabouts, I levy a certain toll, which I use for a better purpose, I hope, than to make candlesticks withal.
10148{_}1508{_}Therefore, sweet chuck, I would have thee deliver to me thy purse, that I may look into it, and judge, to the best of my poor powers, whether thou hast more wealth about thee than our law allows.
10148{_}1509{_}For, as our good Gaffer Swanthold sayeth, 'He who is fat from overliving must needs lose blood.'"
10148{_}1510{_}All this time the youth had been sniffing at the rose that he held betwixt his thumb and finger.
10148{_}1511{_}"Nay," said he with a gentle smile, when Robin Hood had done, "I do love to hear thee talk, thou pretty fellow, and if, haply, thou art not yet done, finish, I beseech thee.
10148{_}1512{_}I have yet some little time to stay."
10148{_}1513{_}"I have said all," quoth Robin, "and now, if thou wilt give me thy purse, I will let thee go thy way without let or hindrance so soon as I shall see what it may hold.
10148{_}1514{_}I will take none from thee if thou hast but little."
10148{_}1515{_}"Alas!
10148{_}1516{_}It doth grieve me much," said the other, "that I cannot do as thou dost wish.
10148{_}1517{_}I have nothing to give thee.
10148{_}1518{_}Let me go my way, I prythee.
10148{_}1519{_}I have done thee no harm."
10148{_}1520{_}"Nay, thou goest not," quoth Robin, "till thou hast shown me thy purse."
10148{_}1521{_}"Good friend," said the other gently, "I have business elsewhere.
10148{_}1522{_}I have given thee much time and have heard thee patiently.
10148{_}1523{_}Prythee, let me depart in peace."
10148{_}1524{_}"I have spoken to thee, friend," said Robin sternly, "and I now tell thee again, that thou goest not one step forward till thou hast done as I bid thee."
10148{_}1525{_}So saying, he raised his quarterstaff above his head in a threatening way.
10148{_}1526{_}"Alas!"
10148{_}1527{_}said the stranger sadly, "it doth grieve me that this thing must be.
10148{_}1528{_}I fear much that I must slay thee, thou poor fellow!"
10148{_}1529{_}So saying, he drew his sword.
10148{_}1530{_}"Put by thy weapon," quoth Robin.
10148{_}1531{_}"I would take no vantage of thee.
10148{_}1532{_}Thy sword cannot stand against an oaken staff such as mine.
10148{_}1533{_}I could snap it like a barley straw.
10148{_}1534{_}Yonder is a good oaken thicket by the roadside; take thee a cudgel thence and defend thyself fairly, if thou hast a taste for a sound drubbing."
10148{_}1535{_}First the stranger measured Robin with his eye, and then he measured the oaken staff.
10148{_}1536{_}"Thou art right, good fellow," said he presently, "truly, my sword is no match for that cudgel of thine.
10148{_}1537{_}Bide thee awhile till I get me a staff."
10148{_}1538{_}So saying, he threw aside the rose that he had been holding all this time, thrust his sword back into the scabbard, and, with a more hasty step than he had yet used, stepped to the roadside where grew the little clump of ground oaks Robin had spoken of.
10148{_}1539{_}Choosing among them, he presently found a sapling to his liking.
10148{_}1540{_}He did not cut it, but, rolling up his sleeves a little way, he laid hold of it, placed his heel against the ground, and, with one mighty pull, plucked the young tree up by the roots from out the very earth.
10148{_}1541{_}Then he came back, trimming away the roots and tender stems with his sword as quietly as if he had done nought to speak of.
10148{_}1542{_}Little John and the Tanner had been watching all that passed, but when they saw the stranger drag the sapling up from the earth, and heard the rending and snapping of its roots, the Tanner pursed his lips together, drawing his breath between them in a long inward whistle.
10148{_}1543{_}"By the breath of my body!"
10148{_}1544{_}said Little John, as soon as he could gather his wits from their wonder, "sawest thou that, Arthur?
10148{_}1545{_}Marry, I think our poor master will stand but an ill chance with yon fellow.
10148{_}1546{_}By Our Lady, he plucked up yon green tree as it were a barley straw."
10148{_}1547{_}Whatever Robin Hood thought, he stood his ground, and now he and the stranger in scarlet stood face to face.
10148{_}1548{_}Well did Robin Hood hold his own that day as a mid-country yeoman.
10148{_}1549{_}This way and that they fought, and back and forth, Robin's skill against the stranger's strength.
10148{_}1550{_}The dust of the highway rose up around them like a cloud, so that at times Little John and the Tanner could see nothing, but only hear the rattle of the staves against one another.
10148{_}1551{_}Thrice Robin Hood struck the stranger; once upon the arm and twice upon the ribs, and yet had he warded all the other's blows, only one of which, had it met its mark, would have laid stout Robin lower in the dust than he had ever gone before.
10148{_}1552{_}At last the stranger struck Robin's cudgel so fairly in the middle that he could hardly hold his staff in his hand; again he struck, and Robin bent beneath the blow; a third time he struck, and now not only fairly beat down Robin's guard, but gave him such a rap, also, that down he tumbled into the dusty road.
10148{_}1553{_}"Hold!"
10148{_}1554{_}cried Robin Hood, when he saw the stranger raising his staff once more.
10148{_}1555{_}"I yield me!"
10148{_}1556{_}"Hold!"
10148{_}1557{_}cried Little John, bursting from his cover, with the Tanner at his heels.
10148{_}1558{_}"Hold!
10148{_}1559{_}give over, I say!"
10148{_}1560{_}"Nay," answered the stranger quietly, "if there be two more of you, and each as stout as this good fellow, I am like to have my hands full.
10148{_}1561{_}Nevertheless, come on, and I will strive my best to serve you all."
10148{_}1562{_}"Stop!"
10148{_}1563{_}cried Robin Hood, "we will fight no more.
10148{_}1564{_}I take my vow, this is an ill day for thee and me, Little John.
10148{_}1565{_}I do verily believe that my wrist, and eke my arm, are palsied by the jar of the blow that this stranger struck me."
10148{_}1566{_}Then Little John turned to Robin Hood.
10148{_}1567{_}"Why, how now, good master," said he.
10148{_}1568{_}"Alas!
10148{_}1569{_}Thou art in an ill plight.
10148{_}1570{_}Marry, thy jerkin is all befouled with the dust of the road.
10148{_}1571{_}Let me help thee to arise."
10148{_}1572{_}"A plague on thy aid!"
10148{_}1573{_}cried Robin angrily.
10148{_}1574{_}"I can get to my feet without thy help, good fellow."
10148{_}1575{_}"Nay, but let me at least dust thy coat for thee.
10148{_}1576{_}I fear thy poor bones are mightily sore," quoth Little John soberly, but with a sly twinkle in his eyes.
10148{_}1577{_}"Give over, I say!"
10148{_}1578{_}quoth Robin in a fume.
10148{_}1579{_}"My coat hath been dusted enough already, without aid of thine."
10148{_}1580{_}Then, turning to the stranger, he said, "What may be thy name, good fellow?"
10148{_}1581{_}"My name is Gamwell," answered the other.
10148{_}1582{_}"Ha!"
10148{_}1583{_}cried Robin, "is it even so?
10148{_}1584{_}I have near kin of that name.
10148{_}1585{_}Whence camest thou, fair friend?"
10148{_}1586{_}"From Maxfield Town I come," answered the stranger.
10148{_}1587{_}"There was I born and bred, and thence I come to seek my mother's young brother, whom men call Robin Hood.
10148{_}1588{_}So, if perchance thou mayst direct me--"  "Ha!
10148{_}1589{_}Will Gamwell!"
10148{_}1590{_}cried Robin, placing both hands upon the other's shoulders and holding him off at arm's length.
10148{_}1591{_}"Surely, it can be none other!
10148{_}1592{_}I might have known thee by that pretty maiden air of thine--that dainty, finicking manner of gait.
10148{_}1593{_}Dost thou not know me, lad?
10148{_}1594{_}Look upon me well."
10148{_}1595{_}"Now, by the breath of my body!"
10148{_}1596{_}cried the other, "I do believe from my heart that thou art mine own Uncle Robin.
10148{_}1597{_}Nay, certain it is so!"
10148{_}1598{_}And each flung his arms around the other, kissing him upon the cheek.
10148{_}1599{_}Then once more Robin held his kinsman off at arm's length and scanned him keenly from top to toe.
10148{_}1600{_}"Why, how now," quoth he, "what change is here?
10148{_}1601{_}Verily, some eight or ten years ago I left thee a stripling lad, with great joints and ill-hung limbs, and lo!
10148{_}1602{_}here thou art, as tight a fellow as e'er I set mine eyes upon.
10148{_}1603{_}Dost thou not remember, lad, how I showed thee the proper way to nip the goose feather betwixt thy fingers and throw out thy bow arm steadily?
10148{_}1604{_}Thou gayest great promise of being a keen archer.
10148{_}1605{_}And dost thou not mind how I taught thee to fend and parry with the cudgel?"
10148{_}1606{_}"Yea," said young Gamwell, "and I did so look up to thee, and thought thee so above all other men that, I make my vow, had I known who thou wert, I would never have dared to lift hand against thee this day.
10148{_}1607{_}I trust I did thee no great harm."
10148{_}1608{_}"No, no," quoth Robin hastily, and looking sideways at Little John, "thou didst not harm me.
10148{_}1609{_}But say no more of that, I prythee.
10148{_}1610{_}Yet I will say, lad, that I hope I may never feel again such a blow as thou didst give me.
10148{_}1611{_}By'r Lady, my arm doth tingle yet from fingernail to elbow.
10148{_}1612{_}Truly, I thought that I was palsied for life.
10148{_}1613{_}I tell thee, coz, that thou art the strongest man that ever I laid mine eyes upon.
10148{_}1614{_}I take my vow, I felt my stomach quake when I beheld thee pluck up yon green tree as thou didst.
10148{_}1615{_}But tell me, how camest thou to leave Sir Edward and thy mother?"
10148{_}1616{_}"Alas!"
10148{_}1617{_}answered young Gamwell, "it is an ill story, uncle, that I have to tell thee.
10148{_}1618{_}My father's steward, who came to us after old Giles Crookleg died, was ever a saucy varlet, and I know not why my father kept him, saving that he did oversee with great judgment.
10148{_}1619{_}It used to gall me to hear him speak up so boldly to my father, who, thou knowest, was ever a patient man to those about him, and slow to anger and harsh words.
10148{_}1620{_}Well, one day--and an ill day it was for that saucy fellow--he sought to berate my father, I standing by.
10148{_}1621{_}I could stand it no longer, good uncle, so, stepping forth, I gave him a box o' the ear, and-- wouldst thou believe it?--the fellow straightway died o't.
10148{_}1622{_}I think they said I broke his neck, or something o' the like.
10148{_}1623{_}So off they packed me to seek thee and escape the law.
10148{_}1624{_}I was on my way when thou sawest me, and here I am."
10148{_}1625{_}"Well, by the faith of my heart," quoth Robin Hood, "for anyone escaping the law, thou wast taking it the most easily that ever I beheld in all my life.
10148{_}1626{_}Whenever did anyone in all the world see one who had slain a man, and was escaping because of it, tripping along the highway like a dainty court damsel, sniffing at a rose the while?"
10148{_}1627{_}"Nay, uncle," answered Will Gamwell, "overhaste never churned good butter, as the old saying hath it.
10148{_}1628{_}Moreover, I do verily believe that this overstrength of my body hath taken the nimbleness out of my heels.
10148{_}1629{_}Why, thou didst but just now rap me thrice, and I thee never a once, save by overbearing thee by my strength."
10148{_}1630{_}"Nay," quoth Robin, "let us say no more on that score.
10148{_}1631{_}I am right glad to see thee, Will, and thou wilt add great honor and credit to my band of merry fellows.
10148{_}1632{_}But thou must change thy name, for warrants will be out presently against thee; so, because of thy gay clothes, thou shalt henceforth and for aye be called Will Scarlet."
10148{_}1633{_}"Will Scarlet," quoth Little John, stepping forward and reaching out his great palm, which the other took, "Will Scarlet, the name fitteth thee well.
10148{_}1634{_}Right glad am I to welcome thee among us.
10148{_}1635{_}I am called Little John; and this is a new member who has just joined us, a stout tanner named Arthur a Bland.
10148{_}1636{_}Thou art like to achieve fame, Will, let me tell thee, for there will be many a merry ballad sung about the country, and many a merry story told in Sherwood of how Robin Hood taught Little John and Arthur a Bland the proper way to use the quarterstaff; likewise, as it were, how our good master bit off so large a piece of cake that he choked on it."
10148{_}1637{_}"Nay, good Little John," quoth Robin gently, for he liked ill to have such a jest told of him.
10148{_}1638{_}"Why should we speak of this little matter?
10148{_}1639{_}Prythee, let us keep this day's doings among ourselves."
10148{_}1640{_}"With all my heart," quoth Little John.
10148{_}1641{_}"But, good master, I thought that thou didst love a merry story, because thou hast so often made a jest about a certain increase of fatness on my joints, of flesh gathered by my abiding with the Sheriff of--"  "Nay, good Little John," said Robin hastily, "I do bethink me I have said full enough on that score."
10148{_}1642{_}"It is well," quoth Little John, "for in truth I myself have tired of it somewhat.
10148{_}1643{_}But now I bethink me, thou didst also seem minded to make a jest of the rain that threatened last night; so--"  "Nay, then," said Robin Hood testily, "I was mistaken.
10148{_}1644{_}I remember me now it did seem to threaten rain."
10148{_}1645{_}"Truly, I did think so myself," quoth Little John, "therefore, no doubt, thou dost think it was wise of me to abide all night at the Blue Boar Inn, instead of venturing forth in such stormy weather; dost thou not?"
10148{_}1646{_}"A plague of thee and thy doings!"
10148{_}1647{_}cried Robin Hood.
10148{_}1648{_}"If thou wilt have it so, thou wert right to abide wherever thou didst choose."
10148{_}1649{_}"Once more, it is well," quoth Little John.
10148{_}1650{_}"As for myself, I have been blind this day.
10148{_}1651{_}I did not see thee drubbed; I did not see thee tumbled heels over head in the dust; and if any man says that thou wert, I can with a clear conscience rattle his lying tongue betwixt his teeth."
10148{_}1652{_}"Come," cried Robin, biting his nether lip, while the others could not forbear laughing.
10148{_}1653{_}"We will go no farther today, but will return to Sherwood, and thou shalt go to Ancaster another time, Little John."
10148{_}1654{_}So said Robin, for now that his bones were sore, he felt as though a long journey would be an ill thing for him.
10148{_}1655{_}So, turning their backs, they retraced their steps whence they came.
10148{_}1656{_}The Adventure with Midge the Miller's Son  WHEN THE four yeomen had traveled for a long time toward Sherwood again, high noontide being past, they began to wax hungry.
10148{_}1657{_}Quoth Robin Hood, "I would that I had somewhat to eat.
10148{_}1658{_}Methinks a good loaf of white bread, with a piece of snow-white cheese, washed down with a draught of humming ale, were a feast for a king."
10148{_}1659{_}"Since thou speakest of it," said Will Scarlet, "methinks it would not be amiss myself.
10148{_}1660{_}There is that within me crieth out, 'Victuals, good friend, victuals!'"
10148{_}1661{_}"I know a house near by," said Arthur a Bland, "and, had I but the money, I would bring ye that ye speak of; to wit, a sweet loaf of bread, a fair cheese, and a skin of brown ale."
10148{_}1662{_}"For the matter of that, thou knowest I have money by me, good master," quoth Little John.
10148{_}1663{_}"Why, so thou hast, Little John," said Robin.
10148{_}1664{_}"How much money will it take, good Arthur, to buy us meat and drink?"
10148{_}1665{_}"I think that six broad pennies will buy food enow for a dozen men," said the Tanner.
10148{_}1666{_}"Then give him six pennies, Little John," quoth Robin, "for methinks food for three men will about fit my need.
10148{_}1667{_}Now get thee gone, Arthur, with the money, and bring the food here, for there is a sweet shade in that thicket yonder, beside the road, and there will we eat our meal."
10148{_}1668{_}So Little John gave Arthur the money, and the others stepped to the thicket, there to await the return of the Tanner.
10148{_}1669{_}After a time he came back, bearing with him a great brown loaf of bread, and a fair, round cheese, and a goatskin full of stout March beer, slung over his shoulders.
10148{_}1670{_}Then Will Scarlet took his sword and divided the loaf and the cheese into four fair portions, and each man helped himself.
10148{_}1671{_}Then Robin Hood took a deep pull at the beer.
10148{_}1672{_}"Aha!"
10148{_}1673{_}said he, drawing in his breath, "never have I tasted sweeter drink than this."
10148{_}1674{_}After this no man spake more, but each munched away at his bread and cheese lustily, with ever and anon a pull at the beer.
10148{_}1675{_}At last Will Scarlet looked at a small piece of bread he still held in his hand, and quoth he, "Methinks I will give this to the sparrows."
10148{_}1676{_}So, throwing it from him, he brushed the crumbs from his jerkin.
10148{_}1677{_}"I, too," quoth Robin, "have had enough, I think."
10148{_}1678{_}As for Little John and the Tanner, they had by this time eaten every crumb of their bread and cheese.
10148{_}1679{_}"Now," quoth Robin, "I do feel myself another man, and would fain enjoy something pleasant before going farther upon our journey.
10148{_}1680{_}I do bethink me, Will, that thou didst use to have a pretty voice, and one that tuned sweetly upon a song.
10148{_}1681{_}Prythee, give us one ere we journey farther."
10148{_}1682{_}"Truly, I do not mind turning a tune," answered Will Scarlet, "but I would not sing alone."
10148{_}1683{_}"Nay, others will follow.
10148{_}1684{_}Strike up, lad," quoth Robin.
10148{_}1685{_}"In that case, 'tis well," said Will Scarlet.
10148{_}1686{_}"I do call to mind a song that a certain minstrel used to sing in my father's hall, upon occasion.
10148{_}1687{_}I know no name for it and so can give you none; but thus it is."
10148{_}1688{_}Then, clearing his throat, he sang:   "_In the merry blossom time,      When love longings food the breast,  When the flower is on the lime,      When the small fowl builds her nest,  Sweetly sings the nightingale      And the throstle cock so bold;  Cuckoo in the dewy dale  And the turtle in the word.
10148{_}1689{_}But the robin I love dear,  For he singeth through the year.
10148{_}1690{_}Robin!
10148{_}1691{_}Robin!
10148{_}1692{_}Merry Robin!
10148{_}1693{_}So I'd have my true love be:           Not to fly           At the nigh  Sign of cold adversity_.
10148{_}1694{_}"_When the spring brings sweet delights,      When aloft the lark doth rise,  Lovers woo o' mellow nights,      And youths peep in maidens' eyes,  That time blooms the eglantine,      Daisies pied upon the hill,  Cowslips fair and columbine,      Dusky violets by the rill.
10148{_}1695{_}But the ivy green cloth grow  When the north wind bringeth snow.
10148{_}1696{_}Ivy!
10148{_}1697{_}Ivy!
10148{_}1698{_}Stanch and true!
10148{_}1699{_}Thus I'd have her love to be:           Not to die           At the nigh  Breath of cold adversity_."
10148{_}1700{_}"'Tis well sung," quoth Robin, "but, cousin, I tell thee plain, I would rather hear a stout fellow like thee sing some lusty ballad than a finicking song of flowers and birds, and what not.
10148{_}1701{_}Yet, thou didst sing it fair, and 'tis none so bad a snatch of a song, for the matter of that.
10148{_}1702{_}Now, Tanner, it is thy turn."
10148{_}1703{_}"I know not," quoth Arthur, smiling, with his head on one side, like a budding lass that is asked to dance, "I know not that I can match our sweet friend's song; moreover, I do verily think that I have caught a cold and have a certain tickling and huskiness in the windpipe."
10148{_}1704{_}"Nay, sing up, friend," quoth Little John, who sat next to him, patting him upon the shoulder.
10148{_}1705{_}"Thou hast a fair, round, mellow voice; let us have a touch of it."
10148{_}1706{_}"Nay, an ye will ha' a poor thing," said Arthur, "I will do my best.
10148{_}1707{_}Have ye ever heard of the wooing of Sir Keith, the stout young Cornish knight, in good King Arthur's time?"
10148{_}1708{_}"Methinks I have heard somewhat of it," said Robin; "but ne'ertheless strike up thy ditty and let us hear it, for, as I do remember me, it is a gallant song; so out with it, good fellow."
10148{_}1709{_}Thereupon, clearing his throat, the Tanner, without more ado, began to sing:  THE WOOING OF SIR KEITH   "_King Arthur sat in his royal hall,      And about on either hand  Was many a noble lordling tall,      The greatest in the land.
10148{_}1710{_}"Sat  Lancelot with raven locks,      Gawaine with golden hair,  Sir Tristram, Kay who kept the locks,      And many another there.
10148{_}1711{_}"And through the stained windows bright,      From o'er the red-tiled eaves,  The sunlight blazed with colored light      On golden helms and greaves.
10148{_}1712{_}"But suddenly a silence came      About the Table Round,  For up the hall there walked a dame      Bent nigh unto the ground.
10148{_}1713{_}"Her nose was hooked, her eyes were bleared,      Her locks were lank and white;  Upon her chin there grew a beard;      She was a gruesome sight.
10148{_}1714{_}"And so with crawling step she came      And kneeled at Arthur's feet;  Quoth Kay, 'She is the foulest dame      That e'er my sight did greet.'
10148{_}1715{_}"'O mighty King!
10148{_}1716{_}of thee I crave      A boon on bended knee';  'Twas thus she spoke.
10148{_}1717{_}'What wouldst thou have.'
10148{_}1718{_}Quoth Arthur, King, 'of me_?'
10148{_}1719{_}"_Quoth she, 'I have a foul disease      Doth gnaw my very heart,  And but one thing can bring me ease      Or cure my bitter smart.
10148{_}1720{_}"'There is no rest, no ease for me      North, east, or west, or south,  Till Christian knight will willingly      Thrice kiss me on the mouth.
10148{_}1721{_}"'Nor wedded may this childe have been      That giveth ease to me;  Nor may he be constrained, I ween,      But kiss me willingly.
10148{_}1722{_}"'So is there here one Christian knight      Of such a noble strain  That he will give a tortured wight      Sweet ease of mortal pain?'
10148{_}1723{_}"'A wedded man,' quoth Arthur, King,      'A wedded man I be  Else would I deem it noble thing      To kiss thee willingly.
10148{_}1724{_}"'Now, Lancelot, in all men's sight      Thou art the head and chief  Of chivalry.
10148{_}1725{_}Come, noble knight,      And give her quick relief.'
10148{_}1726{_}"But Lancelot he turned aside      And looked upon the ground,  For it did sting his haughty pride      To hear them laugh around.
10148{_}1727{_}"'Come thou, Sir Tristram,' quoth the King.
10148{_}1728{_}Quoth he, 'It cannot be,  For ne'er can I my stomach bring      To do it willingly.'
10148{_}1729{_}"'Wilt thou, Sir Kay, thou scornful wight?'
10148{_}1730{_}Quoth Kay, 'Nay, by my troth!
10148{_}1731{_}What noble dame would kiss a knight      That kissed so foul a mouth_?'
10148{_}1732{_}"'_Wilt thou, Gawaine?'
10148{_}1733{_}'I cannot, King.'
10148{_}1734{_}'Sir Geraint?'
10148{_}1735{_}'Nay, not I;  My kisses no relief could bring,      For sooner would I die.'
10148{_}1736{_}"Then up and spake the youngest man      Of all about the board,  'Now such relief as Christian can      I'll give to her, my lord.'
10148{_}1737{_}"It was Sir Keith, a youthful knight,      Yet strong of limb and bold,  With beard upon his chin as light      As finest threads of gold.
10148{_}1738{_}"Quoth Kay, 'He hath no mistress yet      That he may call his own,  But here is one that's quick to get,      As she herself has shown.'
10148{_}1739{_}"He kissed her once, he kissed her twice,      He kissed her three times o'er,  A wondrous change came in a trice,      And she was foul no more.
10148{_}1740{_}"Her cheeks grew red as any rose,      Her brow as white as lawn,  Her bosom like the winter snows,      Her eyes like those of fawn.
10148{_}1741{_}"Her breath grew sweet as summer breeze      That blows the meadows o'er;  Her voice grew soft as rustling trees,      And cracked and harsh no more.
10148{_}1742{_}"Her hair grew glittering, like the gold,      Her hands as white as milk;  Her filthy rags, so foul and old,      Were changed to robes of silk.
10148{_}1743{_}"In great amaze the knights did stare.
10148{_}1744{_}Quoth Kay, 'I make my vow  If it will please thee, lady fair,      I'll gladly kiss thee now_.'
10148{_}1745{_}"_But young Sir Keith kneeled on one knee      And kissed her robes so fair.
10148{_}1746{_}'O let me be thy slave,' said he,      'For none to thee compare.'
10148{_}1747{_}"She bent her down, she kissed his brow,      She kissed his lips and eyes.
10148{_}1748{_}Quoth she, 'Thou art my master now,      My lord, my love, arise!
10148{_}1749{_}"'And all the wealth that is mine own,      My lands, I give to thee,  For never knight hath lady shown      Such noble courtesy.
10148{_}1750{_}"'Bewitched was I, in bitter pain,      But thou hast set me free,  So now I am myself again,      I give myself to thee_.'"
10148{_}1751{_}"Yea, truly," quoth Robin Hood, when the Tanner had made an end of singing, "it is as I remember it, a fair ditty, and a ballad with a pleasing tune of a song."
10148{_}1752{_}"It hath oftentimes seemed to me," said Will Scarlet, "that it hath a certain motive in it, e'en such as this: That a duty which seemeth to us sometimes ugly and harsh, when we do kiss it fairly upon the mouth, so to speak, is no such foul thing after all."
10148{_}1753{_}"Methinks thou art right," quoth Robin, "and, contrariwise, that when we kiss a pleasure that appeareth gay it turneth foul to us; is it not so, Little John?
10148{_}1754{_}Truly such a thing hath brought thee sore thumps this day.
10148{_}1755{_}Nay, man, never look down in the mouth.
10148{_}1756{_}Clear thy pipes and sing us a ditty."
10148{_}1757{_}"Nay," said Little John, "I have none as fair as that merry Arthur has trolled.
10148{_}1758{_}They are all poor things that I know.
10148{_}1759{_}Moreover, my voice is not in tune today, and I would not spoil even a tolerable song by ill singing."
10148{_}1760{_}Upon this all pressed Little John to sing, so that when he had denied them a proper length of time, such as is seemly in one that is asked to sing, he presently yielded.
10148{_}1761{_}Quoth he, 'Well, an ye will ha' it so, I will give you what I can.
10148{_}1762{_}Like to fair Will, I have no title to my ditty, but thus it runs:   "_O Lady mine, the spring is here,      With a hey nonny nonny;  The sweet love season of the year,      With a ninny ninny nonny;           Now lad and lass           Lie in the grass           That groweth green           With flowers between.
10148{_}1763{_}The buck doth rest           The leaves do start,           The cock doth crow,           The breeze doth blow,           And all things laugh in_--"  "Who may yon fellow be coming along the road?"
10148{_}1764{_}said Robin, breaking into the song.
10148{_}1765{_}"I know not," quoth Little John in a surly voice.
10148{_}1766{_}"But this I do know, that it is an ill thing to do to check the flow of a good song."
10148{_}1767{_}"Nay, Little John," said Robin, "be not vexed, I prythee; but I have been watching him coming along, bent beneath that great bag over his shoulder, ever since thou didst begin thy song.
10148{_}1768{_}Look, Little John, I pray, and see if thou knowest him."
10148{_}1769{_}Little John looked whither Robin Hood pointed.
10148{_}1770{_}"Truly," quoth he, after a time, "I think yon fellow is a certain young miller I have seen now and then around the edge of Sherwood; a poor wight, methinks, to spoil a good song about."
10148{_}1771{_}"Now thou speakest of him," quoth Robin Hood, "methinks I myself have seen him now and then.
10148{_}1772{_}Hath he not a mill over beyond Nottingham Town, nigh to the Salisbury road?"
10148{_}1773{_}"Thou art right; that is the man," said Little John.
10148{_}1774{_}"A good stout fellow," quoth Robin.
10148{_}1775{_}"I saw him crack Ned o' Bradford's crown about a fortnight since, and never saw I hair lifted more neatly in all my life before."
10148{_}1776{_}By this time the young miller had come so near that they could see him clearly.
10148{_}1777{_}His clothes were dusted with flour, and over his back he carried a great sack of meal, bending so as to bring the whole weight upon his shoulders, and across the sack was a thick quarterstaff.
10148{_}1778{_}His limbs were stout and strong, and he strode along the dusty road right sturdily with the heavy sack across his shoulders.
10148{_}1779{_}His cheeks were ruddy as a winter hip, his hair was flaxen in color, and on his chin was a downy growth of flaxen beard.
10148{_}1780{_}"A good honest fellow," quoth Robin Hood, "and such an one as is a credit to English yeomanrie.
10148{_}1781{_}Now let us have a merry jest with him.
10148{_}1782{_}We will forth as though we were common thieves and pretend to rob him of his honest gains.
10148{_}1783{_}Then will we take him into the forest and give him a feast such as his stomach never held in all his life before.
10148{_}1784{_}We will flood his throat with good canary and send him home with crowns in his purse for every penny he hath.
10148{_}1785{_}What say ye, lads?"
10148{_}1786{_}"Truly, it is a merry thought," said Will Scarlet.
10148{_}1787{_}"It is well planned," quoth Little John, "but all the saints preserve us from any more drubbings this day!
10148{_}1788{_}Marry, my poor bones ache so that I--"  "Prythee peace, Little John," quoth Robin.
10148{_}1789{_}"Thy foolish tongue will get us both well laughed at yet."
10148{_}1790{_}"My foolish tongue, forsooth," growled Little John to Arthur a Bland.
10148{_}1791{_}"I would it could keep our master from getting us into another coil this day."
10148{_}1792{_}But now the Miller, plodding along the road, had come opposite to where the yeomen lay hidden, whereupon all four of them ran at him and surrounded him.
10148{_}1793{_}"Hold, friend!"
10148{_}1794{_}cried Robin to the Miller; whereupon he turned slowly, with the weight of the bag upon his shoulder, and looked at each in turn all bewildered, for though a good stout man his wits did not skip like roasting chestnuts.
10148{_}1795{_}"Who bids me stay?"
10148{_}1796{_}said the Miller in a voice deep and gruff, like the growl of a great dog.
10148{_}1797{_}"Marry, that do I," quoth Robin; "and let me tell thee, friend, thou hadst best mind my bidding."
10148{_}1798{_}"And who art thou, good friend?"
10148{_}1799{_}said the Miller, throwing the great sack of meal from his shoulder to the ground, "and who are those with thee?"
10148{_}1800{_}"We be four good Christian men," quoth Robin, "and would fain help thee by carrying part of thy heavy load."
10148{_}1801{_}"I give you all thanks," said the Miller, "but my bag is none that heavy that I cannot carry it e'en by myself."
10148{_}1802{_}"Nay, thou dost mistake," quoth Robin, "I meant that thou mightest perhaps have some heavy farthings or pence about thee, not to speak of silver and gold.
10148{_}1803{_}Our good Gaffer Swanthold sayeth that gold is an overheavy burden for a two-legged ass to carry; so we would e'en lift some of this load from thee."
10148{_}1804{_}"Alas!"
10148{_}1805{_}cried the Miller, "what would ye do to me?
10148{_}1806{_}I have not about me so much as a clipped groat.
10148{_}1807{_}Do me no harm, I pray you, but let me depart in peace.
10148{_}1808{_}Moreover, let me tell you that ye are upon Robin Hood's ground, and should he find you seeking to rob an honest craftsman, he will clip your ears to your heads and scourge you even to the walls of Nottingham.
10148{_}1809{_}"In truth I fear Robin Hood no more than I do myself," quoth jolly Robin.
10148{_}1810{_}"Thou must this day give up to me every penny thou hast about thee.
10148{_}1811{_}Nay, if thou dost budge an inch I will rattle this staff about thine ears."
10148{_}1812{_}"Nay, smite me not!"
10148{_}1813{_}cried the Miller, throwing up his elbow as though he feared the blow.
10148{_}1814{_}"Thou mayst search me if thou wilt, but thou wilt find nothing upon me, pouch, pocket, or skin."
10148{_}1815{_}"Is it so?"
10148{_}1816{_}quoth Robin Hood, looking keenly upon him.
10148{_}1817{_}"Now I believe that what thou tellest is no true tale.
10148{_}1818{_}If I am not much mistook thou hast somewhat in the bottom of that fat sack of meal.
10148{_}1819{_}Good Arthur, empty the bag upon the ground; I warrant thou wilt find a shilling or two in the flour."
10148{_}1820{_}"Alas!"
10148{_}1821{_}cried the Miller, falling upon his knees, "spoil not all my good meal!
10148{_}1822{_}It can better you not, and will ruin me.
10148{_}1823{_}Spare it, and I will give up the money in the bag."
10148{_}1824{_}"Ha!"
10148{_}1825{_}quoth Robin, nudging Will Scarlet.
10148{_}1826{_}"Is it so?
10148{_}1827{_}And have I found where thy money lies?
10148{_}1828{_}Marry, I have a wondrous nose for the blessed image of good King Harry.
10148{_}1829{_}I thought that I smelled gold and silver beneath the barley meal.
10148{_}1830{_}Bring it straight forth, Miller."
10148{_}1831{_}Then slowly the Miller arose to his feet, and slowly and unwillingly he untied the mouth of the bag, and slowly thrust his hands into the meal and began fumbling about with his arms buried to the elbows in the barley flour.
10148{_}1832{_}The others gathered round him, their heads together, looking and wondering what he would bring forth.
10148{_}1833{_}So they stood, all with their heads close together gazing down into the sack.
10148{_}1834{_}But while he pretended to be searching for the money, the Miller gathered two great handfuls of meal.
10148{_}1835{_}"Ha," quoth he, "here they are, the beauties."
10148{_}1836{_}Then, as the others leaned still more forward to see what he had, he suddenly cast the meal into their faces, filling their eyes and noses and mouths with the flour, blinding and half choking them.
10148{_}1837{_}Arthur a Bland was worse off than any, for his mouth was open, agape with wonder of what was to come, so that a great cloud of flour flew down his throat, setting him a-coughing till he could scarcely stand.
10148{_}1838{_}Then, while all four stumbled about, roaring with the smart of the meal in their eyeballs, and while they rubbed their eyes till the tears made great channels on their faces through the meal, the Miller seized another handful of flour and another and another, throwing it in their faces, so that even had they had a glimmering of light before they were now as blind as ever a beggar in Nottinghamshire, while their hair and beards and clothes were as white as snow.
10148{_}1839{_}Then catching up his great crabstaff, the Miller began laying about him as though he were clean gone mad.
10148{_}1840{_}This way and that skipped the four, like peas on a drumhead, but they could see neither to defend themselves nor to run away.
10148{_}1841{_}Thwack!
10148{_}1842{_}thwack!
10148{_}1843{_}went the Miller's cudgel across their backs, and at every blow great white clouds of flour rose in the air from their jackets and went drifting down the breeze.
10148{_}1844{_}"Stop!"
10148{_}1845{_}roared Robin at last.
10148{_}1846{_}"Give over, good friend, I am Robin Hood!"
10148{_}1847{_}"Thou liest, thou knave," cried the Miller, giving him a rap on the ribs that sent up a great cloud of flour like a puff of smoke.
10148{_}1848{_}"Stout Robin never robbed an honest tradesman.
10148{_}1849{_}Ha!
10148{_}1850{_}thou wouldst have my money, wouldst thou?"
10148{_}1851{_}And he gave him another blow.
10148{_}1852{_}"Nay, thou art not getting thy share, thou long-legged knave.
10148{_}1853{_}Share and share alike."
10148{_}1854{_}And he smote Little John across the shoulders so that he sent him skipping half across the road.
10148{_}1855{_}"Nay, fear not, it is thy turn now, black beard."
10148{_}1856{_}And he gave the Tanner a crack that made him roar for all his coughing.
10148{_}1857{_}"How now, red coat, let me brush the dust from thee!"
10148{_}1858{_}cried he, smiting Will Scarlet.
10148{_}1859{_}And so he gave them merry words and blows until they could scarcely stand, and whenever he saw one like to clear his eyes he threw more flour in his face.
10148{_}1860{_}At last Robin Hood found his horn and clapping it to his lips, blew three loud blasts upon it.
10148{_}1861{_}Now it chanced that Will Stutely and a party of Robin's men were in the glade not far from where this merry sport was going forward.
10148{_}1862{_}Hearing the hubbub of voices, and blows that sounded like the noise of a flail in the barn in wintertime, they stopped, listening and wondering what was toward.
10148{_}1863{_}Quoth Will Stutely, "Now if I mistake not there is some stout battle with cudgels going forward not far hence.
10148{_}1864{_}I would fain see this pretty sight."
