10148{_}2520{_}Said the Bishop to himself, while he looked grimly at Robin, "When this wedding is gone by I will have this fellow well whipped for his saucy tongue and bold speech."
10148{_}2521{_}And now fair Ellen and Sir Stephen stood before the altar, and the Bishop himself came in his robes and opened his book, whereat fair Ellen looked up and about her in bitter despair, like the fawn that finds the hounds on her haunch.
10148{_}2522{_}Then, in all his fluttering tags and ribbons of red and yellow, Robin Hood strode forward.
10148{_}2523{_}Three steps he took from the pillar whereby he leaned, and stood between the bride and bridegroom.
10148{_}2524{_}"Let me look upon this lass," he said in a loud voice.
10148{_}2525{_}"Why, how now!
10148{_}2526{_}What have we here?
10148{_}2527{_}Here be lilies in the cheeks, and not roses such as befit a bonny bride.
10148{_}2528{_}This is no fit wedding.
10148{_}2529{_}Thou, Sir Knight, so old, and she so young, and thou thinkest to make her thy wife?
10148{_}2530{_}I tell thee it may not be, for thou art not her own true love."
10148{_}2531{_}At this all stood amazed, and knew not where to look nor what to think or say, for they were all bewildered with the happening; so, while everyone looked at Robin as though they had been changed to stone, he clapped his bugle horn to his lips and blew three blasts so loud and clear, they echoed from floor to rafter as though they were sounded by the trump of doom.
10148{_}2532{_}Then straightway Little John and Will Stutely came leaping and stood upon either side of Robin Hood, and quickly drew their broadswords, the while a mighty voice rolled over the heads of all, "Here be I, good master, when thou wantest me"; for it was Friar Tuck that so called from the organ loft.
10148{_}2533{_}And now all was hubbub and noise.
10148{_}2534{_}Stout Edward strode forward raging, and would have seized his daughter to drag her away, but Little John stepped between and thrust him back.
10148{_}2535{_}"Stand back, old man," said he, "thou art a hobbled horse this day."
10148{_}2536{_}"Down with the villains!"
10148{_}2537{_}cried Sir Stephen, and felt for his sword, but it hung not beside him on his wedding day.
10148{_}2538{_}Then the men-at-arms drew their swords, and it seemed like that blood would wet the stones; but suddenly came a bustle at the door and loud voices, steel flashed in the light, and the crash of blows sounded.
10148{_}2539{_}The men-at-arms fell back, and up the aisle came leaping eighteen stout yeomen all clad in Lincoln green, with Allan a Dale at their head.
10148{_}2540{_}In his hand he bore Robin Hood's good stout trusty bow of yew, and this he gave to him, kneeling the while upon one knee.
10148{_}2541{_}Then up spake Edward of Deirwold in a deep voice of anger, "Is it thou, Allan a Dale, that hath bred all this coil in a church?"
10148{_}2542{_}"Nay," quoth merry Robin, "that have I done, and I care not who knoweth it, for my name is Robin Hood."
10148{_}2543{_}At this name a sudden silence fell.
10148{_}2544{_}The Prior of Emmet and those that belonged to him gathered together like a flock of frightened sheep when the scent of the wolf is nigh, while the Bishop of Hereford, laying aside his book, crossed himself devoutly.
10148{_}2545{_}"Now Heaven keep us this day," said he, "from that evil man!"
10148{_}2546{_}"Nay," quoth Robin, "I mean you no harm; but here is fair Ellen's betrothed husband, and she shall marry him or pain will be bred to some of you."
10148{_}2547{_}Then up spake stout Edward in a loud and angry voice, "Now I say nay!
10148{_}2548{_}I am her father, and she shall marry Sir Stephen and none other."
10148{_}2549{_}Now all this time, while everything was in turmoil about him, Sir Stephen had been standing in proud and scornful silence.
10148{_}2550{_}"Nay, fellow," said he coldly, "thou mayst take thy daughter back again; I would not marry her after this day's doings could I gain all merry England thereby.
10148{_}2551{_}I tell thee plainly, I loved thy daughter, old as I am, and would have taken her up like a jewel from the sty, yet, truly, I knew not that she did love this fellow, and was beloved by him.
10148{_}2552{_}Maiden, if thou dost rather choose a beggarly minstrel than a high-born knight, take thy choice.
10148{_}2553{_}I do feel it shame that I should thus stand talking amid this herd, and so I will leave you."
10148{_}2554{_}Thus saying, he turned and, gathering his men about him, walked proudly down the aisle.
10148{_}2555{_}Then all the yeomen were silenced by the scorn of his words.
10148{_}2556{_}Only Friar Tuck leaned over the edge of the choir loft and called out to him ere he had gone, "Good den, Sir Knight.
10148{_}2557{_}Thou wottest old bones must alway make room for young blood."
10148{_}2558{_}Sir Stephen neither answered nor looked up, but passed out from the church as though he had heard nought, his men following him.
10148{_}2559{_}Then the Bishop of Hereford spoke hastily, "I, too, have no business here, and so will depart."
10148{_}2560{_}And he made as though he would go.
10148{_}2561{_}But Robin Hood laid hold of his clothes and held him.
10148{_}2562{_}"Stay, my Lord Bishop," said he, "I have yet somewhat to say to thee."
10148{_}2563{_}The Bishop's face fell, but he stayed as Robin bade him, for he saw he could not go.
10148{_}2564{_}Then Robin Hood turned to stout Edward of Deirwold, and said he, "Give thy blessing on thy daughter's marriage to this yeoman, and all will be well.
10148{_}2565{_}Little John, give me the bags of gold.
10148{_}2566{_}Look, farmer.
10148{_}2567{_}Here are two hundred bright golden angels; give thy blessing, as I say, and I will count them out to thee as thy daughter's dower.
10148{_}2568{_}Give not thy blessing, and she shall be married all the same, but not so much as a cracked farthing shall cross thy palm.
10148{_}2569{_}Choose."
10148{_}2570{_}Then Edward looked upon the ground with bent brows, turning the matter over and over in his mind; but he was a shrewd man and one, withal, that made the best use of a cracked pipkin; so at last he looked up and said, but in no joyous tone, "If the wench will go her own gait, let her go.
10148{_}2571{_}I had thought to make a lady of her; yet if she chooses to be what she is like to be, I have nought to do with her henceforth.
10148{_}2572{_}Ne'ertheless I will give her my blessing when she is duly wedded."
10148{_}2573{_}"It may not be," spake up one of those of Emmet.
10148{_}2574{_}"The banns have not been duly published, neither is there any priest here to marry them."
10148{_}2575{_}"How sayst thou?"
10148{_}2576{_}roared Tuck from the choir loft.
10148{_}2577{_}"No priest?
10148{_}2578{_}Marry, here stands as holy a man as thou art, any day of the week, a clerk in orders, I would have thee know.
10148{_}2579{_}As for the question of banns, stumble not over that straw, brother, for I will publish them."
10148{_}2580{_}So saying, he called the banns; and, says the old ballad, lest three times should not be enough, he published them nine times o'er.
10148{_}2581{_}Then straightway he came down from the loft and forthwith performed the marriage service; and so Allan and Ellen were duly wedded.
10148{_}2582{_}And now Robin counted out two hundred golden angels to Edward of Deirwold, and he, upon his part, gave his blessing, yet not, I wot, as though he meant it with overmuch good will.
10148{_}2583{_}Then the stout yeomen crowded around and grasped Allan's palm, and he, holding Ellen's hand within his own, looked about him all dizzy with his happiness.
10148{_}2584{_}Then at last jolly Robin turned to the Bishop of Hereford, who had been looking on at all that passed with a grim look.
10148{_}2585{_}"My Lord Bishop," quoth he, "thou mayst bring to thy mind that thou didst promise me that did I play in such wise as to cause this fair lass to love her husband, thou wouldst give me whatsoever I asked in reason.
10148{_}2586{_}I have played my play, and she loveth her husband, which she would not have done but for me; so now fulfill thy promise.
10148{_}2587{_}Thou hast upon thee that which, methinks, thou wouldst be the better without; therefore, I prythee, give me that golden chain that hangeth about thy neck as a wedding present for this fair bride."
10148{_}2588{_}Then the Bishop's cheeks grew red with rage and his eyes flashed.
10148{_}2589{_}He looked at Robin with a fell look, but saw that in the yeoman's face which bade him pause.
10148{_}2590{_}Then slowly he took the chain from about his neck and handed it to Robin, who flung it over Ellen's head so that it hung glittering about her shoulders.
10148{_}2591{_}Then said merry Robin, "I thank thee, on the bride's part, for thy handsome gift, and truly thou thyself art more seemly without it.
10148{_}2592{_}Now, shouldst thou ever come nigh to Sherwood I much hope that I shall give thee there such a feast as thou hast ne'er had in all thy life before."
10148{_}2593{_}"May Heaven forfend!"
10148{_}2594{_}cried the Bishop earnestly; for he knew right well what manner of feast it was that Robin Hood gave his guests in Sherwood Forest.
10148{_}2595{_}But now Robin Hood gathered his men together, and, with Allan and his young bride in their midst, they all turned their footsteps toward the woodlands.
10148{_}2596{_}On the way thither Friar Tuck came close to Robin and plucked him by the sleeve.
10148{_}2597{_}"Thou dost lead a merry life, good master," quoth he, "but dost thou not think that it would be for the welfare of all your souls to have a good stout chaplain, such as I, to oversee holy matters?
10148{_}2598{_}Truly, I do love this life mightily."
10148{_}2599{_}At this merry Robin Hood laughed amain, and bade him stay and become one of their band if he wished.
10148{_}2600{_}That night there was such a feast held in the greenwood as Nottinghamshire never saw before.
10148{_}2601{_}To that feast you and I were not bidden, and pity it is that we were not; so, lest we should both feel the matter the more keenly, I will say no more about it.
10148{_}2602{_}Robin Hood Aids a Sorrowful Knight  SO PASSED the gentle springtime away in budding beauty; its silver showers and sunshine, its green meadows and its flowers.
10148{_}2603{_}So, likewise, passed the summer with its yellow sunlight, its quivering heat and deep, bosky foliage, its long twilights and its mellow nights, through which the frogs croaked and fairy folk were said to be out on the hillsides.
10148{_}2604{_}All this had passed and the time of fall had come, bringing with it its own pleasures and joyousness; for now, when the harvest was gathered home, merry bands of gleaners roamed the country about, singing along the roads in the daytime, and sleeping beneath the hedgerows and the hay-ricks at night.
10148{_}2605{_}Now the hips burned red in the tangled thickets and the hews waxed black in the hedgerows, the stubble lay all crisp and naked to the sky, and the green leaves were fast turning russet and brown.
10148{_}2606{_}Also, at this merry season, good things of the year are gathered in in great store.
10148{_}2607{_}Brown ale lies ripening in the cellar, hams and bacon hang in the smoke-shed, and crabs are stowed away in the straw for roasting in the wintertime, when the north wind piles the snow in drifts around the gables and the fire crackles warm upon the hearth.
10148{_}2608{_}So passed the seasons then, so they pass now, and so they will pass in time to come, while we come and go like leaves of the tree that fall and are soon forgotten.
10148{_}2609{_}Quoth Robin Hood, snuffing the air, "Here is a fair day, Little John, and one that we can ill waste in idleness.
10148{_}2610{_}Choose such men as thou dost need, and go thou east while I will wend to the west, and see that each of us bringeth back some goodly guest to dine this day beneath the greenwood tree."
10148{_}2611{_}"Marry," cried Little John, clapping his palms together for joy, "thy bidding fitteth my liking like heft to blade.
10148{_}2612{_}I'll bring thee back a guest this day, or come not back mine own self."
10148{_}2613{_}Then they each chose such of the band as they wished, and so went forth by different paths from the forest.
10148{_}2614{_}Now, you and I cannot go two ways at the same time while we join in these merry doings; so we will e'en let Little John follow his own path while we tuck up our skirts and trudge after Robin Hood.
10148{_}2615{_}And here is good company, too; Robin Hood, Will Scarlet, Allan a Dale, Will Scathelock, Midge, the Miller's son, and others.
10148{_}2616{_}A score or more of stout fellows had abided in the forest, with Friar Tuck, to make ready for the homecoming, but all the rest were gone either with Robin Hood or Little John.
10148{_}2617{_}They traveled onward, Robin following his fancy and the others following Robin.
10148{_}2618{_}Now they wended their way through an open dale with cottage and farm lying therein, and now again they entered woodlands once more.
10148{_}2619{_}Passing by fair Mansfield Town, with its towers and battlements and spires all smiling in the sun, they came at last out of the forest lands.
10148{_}2620{_}Onward they journeyed, through highway and byway, through villages where goodwives and merry lasses peeped through the casements at the fine show of young men, until at last they came over beyond Alverton in Derbyshire.
10148{_}2621{_}By this time high noontide had come, yet they had met no guest such as was worth their while to take back to Sherwood; so, coming at last to a certain spot where a shrine stood at the crossing of two roads, Robin called upon them to stop, for here on either side was shelter of high hedgerows, behind which was good hiding, whence they could watch the roads at their ease, while they ate their midday meal.
10148{_}2622{_}Quoth merry Robin, "Here, methinks, is good lodging, where peaceful folk, such as we be, can eat in quietness; therefore we will rest here, and see what may, perchance, fall into our luck-pot."
10148{_}2623{_}So they crossed a stile and came behind a hedgerow where the mellow sunlight was bright and warm, and where the grass was soft, and there sat them down.
10148{_}2624{_}Then each man drew from the pouch that hung beside him that which he had brought to eat, for a merry walk such as this had been sharpens the appetite till it is as keen as a March wind.
10148{_}2625{_}So no more words were spoken, but each man saved his teeth for better use--munching at brown crust and cold meat right lustily.
10148{_}2626{_}In front of them, one of the highroads crawled up the steep hill and then dipped suddenly over its crest, sharp-cut with hedgerow and shaggy grass against the sky.
10148{_}2627{_}Over the top of the windy hill peeped the eaves of a few houses of the village that fell back into the valley behind; there, also, showed the top of a windmill, the sails slowly rising and dipping from behind the hill against the clear blue sky, as the light wind moved them with creaking and labored swing.
10148{_}2628{_}So the yeomen lay behind the hedge and finished their midday meal; but still the time slipped along and no one came.
10148{_}2629{_}At last, a man came slowly riding over the hill and down the stony road toward the spot where Robin and his band lay hidden.
10148{_}2630{_}He was a good stout knight, but sorrowful of face and downcast of mien.
10148{_}2631{_}His clothes were plain and rich, but no chain of gold, such as folk of his stand in life wore at most times, hung around his neck, and no jewel was about him; yet no one could mistake him for aught but one of proud and noble blood.
10148{_}2632{_}His head was bowed upon his breast and his hands drooped limp on either side; and so he came slowly riding, as though sunk in sad thoughts, while even his good horse, the reins loose upon his neck, walked with hanging head, as though he shared his master's grief.
10148{_}2633{_}Quoth Robin Hood, "Yon is verily a sorry-looking gallant, and doth seem to have donned ill-content with his jerkin this morning; nevertheless, I will out and talk with him, for there may be some pickings here for a hungry daw.
10148{_}2634{_}Methinks his dress is rich, though he himself is so downcast.
10148{_}2635{_}Bide ye here till I look into this matter."
10148{_}2636{_}So saying, he arose and left them, crossed the road to the shrine, and there stood, waiting for the sorrowful knight to come near him.
10148{_}2637{_}So, presently, when the knight came riding slowly along, jolly Robin stepped forward and laid his hand upon the bridle rein.
10148{_}2638{_}"Hold, Sir Knight," quoth he.
10148{_}2639{_}"I prythee tarry for a short time, for I have a few words to say to thee."
10148{_}2640{_}"What art thou, friend, who dost stop a traveler in this manner upon his most gracious Majesty's highway?"
10148{_}2641{_}said the Knight.
10148{_}2642{_}"Marry," quoth Robin, "that is a question hard to answer.
10148{_}2643{_}One man calleth me kind, another calleth me cruel; this one calleth me good honest fellow, and that one, vile thief.
10148{_}2644{_}Truly, the world hath as many eyes to look upon a man withal as there are spots on a toad; so, with what pair of eyes thou regardest me lieth entirely with thine own self.
10148{_}2645{_}My name is Robin Hood."
10148{_}2646{_}"Truly, good Robin," said the Knight, a smile twitching at the corners of his mouth, "thou hast a quaint conceit.
10148{_}2647{_}As for the pair of eyes with which I regard thee, I would say that they are as favorable as may be, for I hear much good of thee and little ill.  What is thy will of me?"
10148{_}2648{_}"Now, I make my vow, Sir Knight," quoth Robin, "thou hast surely learned thy wisdom of good Gaffer Swanthold, for he sayeth, 'Fair words are as easy spoke as foul, and bring good will in the stead of blows.'
10148{_}2649{_}Now I will show thee the truth of this saying; for, if thou wilt go with me this day to Sherwood Forest, I will give thee as merry a feast as ever thou hadst in all thy life."
10148{_}2650{_}"Thou art indeed kind," said the Knight, "but methinks thou wilt find me but an ill-seeming and sorrowful guest.
10148{_}2651{_}Thou hadst best let me pass on my way in peace."
10148{_}2652{_}"Nay," quoth Robin, "thou mightst go thine own way but for one thing, and that I will tell thee.
10148{_}2653{_}We keep an inn, as it were, in the very depths of Sherwood, but so far from highroads and beaten paths that guests do not often come nigh us; so I and my friends set off merrily and seek them when we grow dull of ourselves.
10148{_}2654{_}Thus the matter stands, Sir Knight; yet I will furthermore tell thee that we count upon our guests paying a reckoning."
10148{_}2655{_}"I take thy meaning, friend," said the Knight gravely, "but I am not thy man, for I have no money by me."
10148{_}2656{_}"Is it sooth?"
10148{_}2657{_}said Robin, looking at the Knight keenly.
10148{_}2658{_}"I can scarce choose but believe thee; yet, Sir Knight, there be those of thy order whose word is not to be trusted as much as they would have others believe.
10148{_}2659{_}Thou wilt think no ill if I look for myself in this matter."
10148{_}2660{_}Then, still holding the horse by the bridle rein, he put his fingers to his lips and blew a shrill whistle, whereupon fourscore yeomen came leaping over the stile and ran to where the Knight and Robin stood.
10148{_}2661{_}"These," said Robin, looking upon them proudly, "are some of my merry men.
10148{_}2662{_}They share and share alike with me all joys and troubles, gains and losses.
10148{_}2663{_}Sir Knight, I prythee tell me what money thou hast about thee."
10148{_}2664{_}For a time the Knight said not a word, but a slow red arose into his cheeks; at last he looked Robin in the face and said, "I know not why I should be ashamed, for it should be no shame to me; but, friend, I tell thee the truth, when I say that in my purse are ten shillings, and that that is every groat that Sir Richard of the Lea hath in all the wide world."
10148{_}2665{_}When Sir Richard ended a silence fell, until at last Robin said, "And dost thou pledge me thy knightly word that this is all thou hast with thee?"
10148{_}2666{_}"Yea," answered Sir Richard, "I do pledge thee my most solemn word, as a true knight, that it is all the money I have in the world.
10148{_}2667{_}Nay, here is my purse, ye may find for yourselves the truth of what I say."
10148{_}2668{_}And he held his purse out to Robin.
10148{_}2669{_}"Put up thy purse, Sir Richard," quoth Robin.
10148{_}2670{_}"Far be it from me to doubt the word of so gentle a knight.
10148{_}2671{_}The proud I strive to bring low, but those that walk in sorrow I would aid if I could.
10148{_}2672{_}Come, Sir Richard, cheer up thy heart and go with us into the greenwood.
10148{_}2673{_}Even I may perchance aid thee, for thou surely knowest how the good Athelstane was saved by the little blind mole that digged a trench over which he that sought the king's life stumbled."
10148{_}2674{_}"Truly, friend," said Sir Richard, "methinks thou meanest kindness in thine own way; nevertheless my troubles are such that it is not likely that thou canst cure them.
10148{_}2675{_}But I will go with thee this day into Sherwood."
10148{_}2676{_}Hereupon he turned his horse's head, and they all wended their way to the woodlands, Robin walking on one side of the Knight and Will Scarlet on the other, while the rest of the band trudged behind.
10148{_}2677{_}After they had traveled thus for a time Robin Hood spake.
10148{_}2678{_}"Sir Knight," said he, "I would not trouble thee with idle questions; but dost thou find it in thy heart to tell me thy sorrows?"
10148{_}2679{_}"Truly, Robin," quoth the Knight, "I see no reason why I should not do so.
10148{_}2680{_}Thus it is:  My castle and my lands are in pawn for a debt that I owe.
10148{_}2681{_}Three days hence the money must be paid or else all mine estate is lost forever, for then it falls into the hands of the Priory of Emmet, and what they swallow they never give forth again."
10148{_}2682{_}Quoth Robin, "I understand not why those of thy kind live in such a manner that all their wealth passeth from them like snow beneath the springtide sun."
10148{_}2683{_}"Thou wrongest me, Robin," said the Knight, "for listen: I have a son but twenty winters old, nevertheless he has won his spurs as knight.
10148{_}2684{_}Last year, on a certain evil day, the jousts were held at Chester, and thither my son went, as did I and my lady wife.
10148{_}2685{_}I wot it was a proud time for us, for he unhorsed each knight that he tilted against.
10148{_}2686{_}At last he ran a course with a certain great knight, Sir Walter of Lancaster, yet, though my son was so youthful, he kept his seat, albeit both spears were shivered to the heft; but it happened that a splinter of my boy's lance ran through the visor of Sir Walter's helmet and pierced through his eye into his brain, so that he died ere his esquire could unlace his helm.
10148{_}2687{_}Now, Robin, Sir Walter had great friends at court, therefore his kinsmen stirred up things against my son so that, to save him from prison, I had to pay a ransom of six hundred pounds in gold.
10148{_}2688{_}All might have gone well even yet, only that, by ins and outs and crookedness of laws, I was shorn like a sheep that is clipped to the quick.
10148{_}2689{_}So it came that I had to pawn my lands to the Priory of Emmet for more money, and a hard bargain they drove with me in my hour of need.
10148{_}2690{_}Yet I would have thee understand I grieve so for my lands only because of my dear lady wife."
10148{_}2691{_}"But where is thy son now?"
10148{_}2692{_}asked Robin, who had listened closely to all the Knight had said.
10148{_}2693{_}"In Palestine," said Sir Richard, "battling like a brave Christian soldier for the cross and the holy sepulcher.
10148{_}2694{_}Truly, England was an ill place for him because of Sir Walter's death and the hate of the Lancastrian's kinsmen."
10148{_}2695{_}"Truly," said Robin, much moved, "thine is a hard lot.
10148{_}2696{_}But tell me, what is owing to Emmet for thine estates?"
10148{_}2697{_}"Only four hundred pounds," said Sir Richard.
10148{_}2698{_}At this, Robin smote his thigh in anger.
10148{_}2699{_}"O the bloodsuckers!"
10148{_}2700{_}cried he.
10148{_}2701{_}"A noble estate to be forfeit for four hundred pounds!
10148{_}2702{_}But what will befall thee if thou dost lose thy lands, Sir Richard?"
10148{_}2703{_}"It is not mine own lot that doth trouble me in that case," said the Knight, "but my dear lady's; for should I lose my land she will have to betake herself to some kinsman and there abide in charity, which, methinks, would break her proud heart.
10148{_}2704{_}As for me, I will over the salt sea, and so to Palestine to join my son in fight for the holy sepulcher."
10148{_}2705{_}Then up spake Will Scarlet.
10148{_}2706{_}"But hast thou no friend that will help thee in thy dire need?"
10148{_}2707{_}"Never a man," said Sir Richard.
10148{_}2708{_}"While I was rich enow at home, and had friends, they blew great boasts of how they loved me.
10148{_}2709{_}But when the oak falls in the forest the swine run from beneath it lest they should be smitten down also.
10148{_}2710{_}So my friends have left me; for not only am I poor but I have great enemies."
10148{_}2711{_}Then Robin said, "Thou sayst thou hast no friends, Sir Richard.
10148{_}2712{_}I make no boast, but many have found Robin Hood a friend in their troubles.
10148{_}2713{_}Cheer up, Sir Knight, I may help thee yet."
10148{_}2714{_}The Knight shook his head with a faint smile, but for all that, Robin's words made him more blithe of heart, for in truth hope, be it never so faint, bringeth a gleam into darkness, like a little rushlight that costeth but a groat.
10148{_}2715{_}The day was well-nigh gone when they came near to the greenwood tree.
10148{_}2716{_}Even at a distance they saw by the number of men that Little John had come back with some guest, but when they came near enough, whom should they find but the Lord Bishop of Hereford!
10148{_}2717{_}The good Bishop was in a fine stew, I wot.
10148{_}2718{_}Up and down he walked beneath the tree like a fox caught in a hencoop.
10148{_}2719{_}Behind him were three Black Friars standing close together in a frightened group, like three black sheep in a tempest.
10148{_}2720{_}Hitched to the branches of the trees close at hand were six horses, one of them a barb with gay trappings upon which the Bishop was wont to ride, and the others laden with packs of divers shapes and kinds, one of which made Robin's eyes glisten, for it was a box not overlarge, but heavily bound with bands and ribs of iron.
10148{_}2721{_}When the Bishop saw Robin and those with him come into the open he made as though he would have run toward the yeoman, but the fellow that guarded the Bishop and the three friars thrust his quarterstaff in front, so that his lordship was fain to stand back, though with frowning brow and angry speech.
10148{_}2722{_}"Stay, my Lord Bishop," cried jolly Robin in a loud voice, when he saw what had passed, "I will come to thee with all speed, for I would rather see thee than any man in merry England."
10148{_}2723{_}So saying, he quickened his steps and soon came to where the Bishop stood fuming.
10148{_}2724{_}"How now," quoth the Bishop in a loud and angry voice, when Robin had so come to him, "is this the way that thou and thy band treat one so high in the church as I am?
10148{_}2725{_}I and these brethren were passing peacefully along the highroad with our pack horses, and a half score of men to guard them, when up comes a great strapping fellow full seven feet high, with fourscore or more men back of him, and calls upon me to stop--me, the Lord Bishop of Hereford, mark thou!
10148{_}2726{_}Whereupon my armed guards-- beshrew them for cowards!--straight ran away.
10148{_}2727{_}But look ye; not only did this fellow stop me, but he threatened me, saying that Robin Hood would strip me as bare as a winter hedge.
10148{_}2728{_}Then, besides all this, he called me such vile names as 'fat priest,' 'man-eating bishop,' 'money-gorging usurer,' and what not, as though I were no more than a strolling beggar or tinker."
10148{_}2729{_}At this, the Bishop glared like an angry cat, while even Sir Richard laughed; only Robin kept a grave face.
10148{_}2730{_}"Alas!
10148{_}2731{_}my lord," said he, "that thou hast been so ill-treated by my band!
10148{_}2732{_}I tell thee truly that we greatly reverence thy cloth.
10148{_}2733{_}Little John, stand forth straightway."
10148{_}2734{_}At these words Little John came forward, twisting his face into a whimsical look, as though he would say, "Ha' mercy upon me, good master."
10148{_}2735{_}Then Robin turned to the Bishop of Hereford and said, "Was this the man who spake so boldly to Your Lordship?"
10148{_}2736{_}"Ay, truly it was the same," said the Bishop, "a naughty fellow, I wot.
10148{_}2737{_}"And didst thou, Little John," said Robin in a sad voice, "call his lordship a fat priest?"
10148{_}2738{_}"Ay," said Little John sorrowfully.
10148{_}2739{_}"And a man-eating bishop?"
10148{_}2740{_}"Ay," said Little John, more sorrowfully than before.
10148{_}2741{_}"And a money-gorging usurer?"
10148{_}2742{_}"Ay," said Little John in so sorrowful a voice that it might have drawn tears from the Dragon of Wentley.
10148{_}2743{_}"Alas, that these things should be!"
10148{_}2744{_}said jolly Robin, turning to the Bishop, "for I have ever found Little John a truthful man."
10148{_}2745{_}At this, a roar of laughter went up, whereat the blood rushed into the Bishop's face till it was cherry red from crown to chin; but he said nothing and only swallowed his words, though they well-nigh choked him.
10148{_}2746{_}"Nay, my Lord Bishop," said Robin, "we are rough fellows, but I trust not such ill men as thou thinkest, after all.
10148{_}2747{_}There is not a man here that would harm a hair of thy reverence's head.
10148{_}2748{_}I know thou art galled by our jesting, but we are all equal here in the greenwood, for there are no bishops nor barons nor earls among us, but only men, so thou must share our life with us while thou dost abide here.
10148{_}2749{_}Come, busk ye, my merry men, and get the feast ready.
10148{_}2750{_}Meantime, we will show our guests our woodland sports."
10148{_}2751{_}So, while some went to kindle the fires for roasting meats, others ran leaping to get their cudgels and longbows.
10148{_}2752{_}Then Robin brought forward Sir Richard of the Lea.
10148{_}2753{_}"My Lord Bishop," said he, "here is another guest that we have with us this day.
10148{_}2754{_}I wish that thou mightest know him better, for I and all my men will strive to honor you both at this merrymaking."
10148{_}2755{_}"Sir Richard," said the Bishop in a reproachful tone, "methinks thou and I are companions and fellow sufferers in this den of--" He was about to say "thieves," but he stopped suddenly and looked askance at Robin Hood.
10148{_}2756{_}"Speak out, Bishop," quoth Robin, laughing.
10148{_}2757{_}"We of Sherwood check not an easy flow of words.
10148{_}2758{_}'Den of thieves' thou west about to say."
10148{_}2759{_}Quoth the Bishop, "Mayhap that was what I meant to say, Sir Richard; but this I will say, that I saw thee just now laugh at the scurrilous jests of these fellows.
10148{_}2760{_}It would have been more becoming of thee, methinks, to have checked them with frowns instead of spurring them on by laughter."
10148{_}2761{_}"I meant no harm to thee," said Sir Richard, "but a merry jest is a merry jest, and I may truly say I would have laughed at it had it been against mine own self."
10148{_}2762{_}But now Robin Hood called upon certain ones of his band who spread soft moss upon the ground and laid deerskins thereon.
10148{_}2763{_}Then Robin bade his guests be seated, and so they all three sat down, some of the chief men, such as Little John, Will Scarlet, Allan a Dale, and others, stretching themselves upon the ground near by.
10148{_}2764{_}Then a garland was set up at the far end of the glade, and thereat the bowmen shot, and such shooting was done that day as it would have made one's heart leap to see.
10148{_}2765{_}And all the while Robin talked so quaintly to the Bishop and the Knight that, the one forgetting his vexation and the other his troubles, they both laughed aloud again and again.
10148{_}2766{_}Then Allan a Dale came forth and tuned his harp, and all was hushed around, and he sang in his wondrous voice songs of love, of war, of glory, and of sadness, and all listened without a movement or a sound.
10148{_}2767{_}So Allan sang till the great round silver moon gleamed with its clear white light amid the upper tangle of the mazy branches of the trees.
10148{_}2768{_}At last two fellows came to say that the feast was ready spread, so Robin, leading his guests with either hand, brought them to where great smoking dishes that sent savory smells far and near stood along the white linen cloth spread on the grass.
10148{_}2769{_}All around was a glare of torches that lit everything up with a red light.
10148{_}2770{_}Then, straightway sitting down, all fell to with noise and hubbub, the rattling of platters blending with the sound of loud talking and laughter.
10148{_}2771{_}A long time the feast lasted, but at last all was over, and the bright wine and humming ale passed briskly.
10148{_}2772{_}Then Robin Hood called aloud for silence, and all was hushed till he spoke.
10148{_}2773{_}"I have a story to tell you all, so listen to what I have to say," quoth he; whereupon, without more ado, he told them all about Sir Richard, and how his lands were in pawn.
10148{_}2774{_}But, as he went on, the Bishop's face, that had erst been smiling and ruddy with merriment, waxed serious, and he put aside the horn of wine he held in his hand, for he knew the story of Sir Richard, and his heart sank within him with grim forebodings.
10148{_}2775{_}Then, when Robin Hood had done, he turned to the Bishop of Hereford.
10148{_}2776{_}"Now, my Lord Bishop," said he, "dost thou not think this is ill done of anyone, much more of a churchman, who should live in humbleness and charity?"
10148{_}2777{_}To this the Bishop answered not a word but looked upon the ground with moody eyes.
10148{_}2778{_}Quoth Robin, "Now, thou art the richest bishop in all England; canst thou not help this needy brother?"
10148{_}2779{_}But still the Bishop answered not a word.
10148{_}2780{_}Then Robin turned to Little John, and quoth he, "Go thou and Will Stutely and bring forth those five pack horses yonder."
10148{_}2781{_}Whereupon the two yeomen did as they were bidden, those about the cloth making room on the green, where the light was brightest, for the five horses which Little John and Will Stutely presently led forward.
10148{_}2782{_}"Who hath the score of the goods?"
10148{_}2783{_}asked Robin Hood, looking at the Black Friars.
10148{_}2784{_}Then up spake the smallest of all, in a trembling voice--an old man he was, with a gentle, wrinkled face.
10148{_}2785{_}"That have I; but, I pray thee, harm me not."
10148{_}2786{_}"Nay," quoth Robin, "I have never harmed harmless man yet; but give it to me, good father."
10148{_}2787{_}So the old man did as he was bidden, and handed Robin the tablet on which was marked down the account of the various packages upon the horses.
10148{_}2788{_}This Robin handed to Will Scarlet, bidding him to read the same.
10148{_}2789{_}So Will Scarlet, lifting his voice that all might hear, began:  "Three bales of silk to Quentin, the mercer at Ancaster."
10148{_}2790{_}"That we touch not," quoth Robin, "for this Quentin is an honest fellow, who hath risen by his own thrift."
10148{_}2791{_}So the bales of silk were laid aside unopened.
10148{_}2792{_}"One bale of silk velvet for the Abbey of Beaumont."
10148{_}2793{_}"What do these priests want of silk velvet?"
10148{_}2794{_}quoth Robin.
10148{_}2795{_}"Nevertheless, though they need it not, I will not take all from them.
10148{_}2796{_}Measure it off into three lots, one to be sold for charity, one for us, and one for the abbey."
10148{_}2797{_}So this, too, was done as Robin Hood bade.
10148{_}2798{_}"Twoscore of great wax candles for the Chapel of Saint Thomas."
10148{_}2799{_}"That belongeth fairly to the chapel," quoth Robin, "so lay it to one side.
10148{_}2800{_}Far be it from us to take from the blessed Saint Thomas that which belongeth to him."
10148{_}2801{_}So this, also, was done according to Robin's bidding, and the candles were laid to one side, along with honest Quentin's unopened bales of silk.
10148{_}2802{_}So the list was gone through with, and the goods adjudged according to what Robin thought most fit.
10148{_}2803{_}Some things were laid aside untouched, and many were opened and divided into three equal parts, for charity, for themselves, and for the owners.
10148{_}2804{_}And now all the ground in the torchlight was covered over with silks and velvets and cloths of gold and cases of rich wines, and so they came to the last line upon the tablet--"A box belonging to the Lord Bishop of Hereford."
10148{_}2805{_}At these words the Bishop shook as with a chill, and the box was set upon the ground.
10148{_}2806{_}"My Lord Bishop, hast thou the key of this box?"
10148{_}2807{_}asked Robin.
10148{_}2808{_}The Bishop shook his head.
10148{_}2809{_}"Go, Will Scarlet," said Robin, "thou art the strongest man here--bring a sword straightway, and cut this box open, if thou canst."
10148{_}2810{_}Then up rose Will Scarlet and left them, coming back in a short time, bearing a great two-handed sword.
10148{_}2811{_}Thrice he smote that strong, ironbound box, and at the third blow it burst open and a great heap of gold came rolling forth, gleaming red in the light of the torches.
10148{_}2812{_}At this sight a murmur went all around among the band, like the sound of the wind in distant trees; but no man came forward nor touched the money.
10148{_}2813{_}Quoth Robin, "Thou, Will Scarlet, thou, Allan a Dale, and thou, Little John, count it over."
10148{_}2814{_}A long time it took to count all the money, and when it had been duly scored up, Will Scarlet called out that there were fifteen hundred golden pounds in all.
10148{_}2815{_}But in among the gold they found a paper, and this Will Scarlet read in a loud voice, and all heard that this money was the rental and fines and forfeits from certain estates belonging to the Bishopric of Hereford.
10148{_}2816{_}"My Lord Bishop," said Robin Hood, "I will not strip thee, as Little John said, like a winter hedge, for thou shalt take back one third of thy money.
10148{_}2817{_}One third of it thou canst well spare to us for thy entertainment and that of thy train, for thou art very rich; one third of it thou canst better spare for charity, for, Bishop, I hear that thou art a hard master to those beneath thee and a close hoarder of gains that thou couldst better and with more credit to thyself give to charity than spend upon thy own likings."
10148{_}2818{_}At this the Bishop looked up, but he could say never a word; yet he was thankful to keep some of his wealth.
10148{_}2819{_}Then Robin turned to Sir Richard of the Lea, and quoth he, "Now, Sir Richard, the church seemed like to despoil thee, therefore some of the overplus of church gains may well be used in aiding thee.
10148{_}2820{_}Thou shalt take that five hundred pounds laid aside for people more in need than the Bishop is, and shalt pay thy debts to Emmet therewith."
10148{_}2821{_}Sir Richard looked at Robin until something arose in his eyes that made all the lights and the faces blur together.
10148{_}2822{_}At last he said, "I thank thee, friend, from my heart, for what thou doest for me; yet, think not ill if I cannot take thy gift freely.
10148{_}2823{_}But this I will do:  I will take the money and pay my debts, and in a year and a day hence will return it safe either to thee or to the Lord Bishop of Hereford.
10148{_}2824{_}For this I pledge my most solemn knightly word.
10148{_}2825{_}I feel free to borrow, for I know no man that should be more bound to aid me than one so high in that church that hath driven such a hard bargain."
10148{_}2826{_}"Truly, Sir Knight," quoth Robin, "I do not understand those fine scruples that weigh with those of thy kind; but, nevertheless, it shall all be as thou dost wish.
10148{_}2827{_}But thou hadst best bring the money to me at the end of the year, for mayhap I may make better use of it than the Bishop."
10148{_}2828{_}Thereupon, turning to those near him, he gave his orders, and five hundred pounds were counted out and tied up in a leathern bag for Sir Richard.
10148{_}2829{_}The rest of the treasure was divided, and part taken to the treasurehouse of the band, and part put by with the other things for the Bishop.
10148{_}2830{_}Then Sir Richard arose.
10148{_}2831{_}"I cannot stay later, good friends," said he, "for my lady will wax anxious if I come not home; so I crave leave to depart."
10148{_}2832{_}Then Robin Hood and all his merry men arose, and Robin said, "We cannot let thee go hence unattended, Sir Richard."
10148{_}2833{_}Then up spake Little John, "Good master, let me choose a score of stout fellows from the band, and let us arm ourselves in a seemly manner and so serve as retainers to Sir Richard till he can get others in our stead."
10148{_}2834{_}"Thou hast spoken well, Little John, and it shall be done," said Robin.
10148{_}2835{_}Then up spake Will Scarlet, "Let us give him a golden chain to hang about his neck, such as befits one of his blood, and also golden spurs to wear at his heels."
10148{_}2836{_}Then Robin Hood said, "Thou hast spoken well, Will Scarlet, and it shall be done."
10148{_}2837{_}Then up spake Will Stutely, "Let us give him yon bale of rich velvet and yon roll of cloth of gold to take home to his noble lady wife as a present from Robin Hood and his merry men all."
10148{_}2838{_}At this all clapped their hands for joy, and Robin said: "Thou hast well spoken, Will Stutely, and it shall be done."
10148{_}2839{_}Then Sir Richard of the Lea looked all around and strove to speak, but could scarcely do so for the feelings that choked him; at last he said in a husky, trembling voice, "Ye shall all see, good friends, that Sir Richard o' the Lea will ever remember your kindness this day.
10148{_}2840{_}And if ye be at any time in dire need or trouble, come to me and my lady, and the walls of Castle Lea shall be battered down ere harm shall befall you.
10148{_}2841{_}I--" He could say nothing further, but turned hastily away.
10148{_}2842{_}But now Little John and nineteen stout fellows whom he had chosen for his band, came forth all ready for the journey.
10148{_}2843{_}Each man wore upon his breast a coat of linked mail, and on his head a cap of steel, and at his side a good stout sword.
10148{_}2844{_}A gallant show they made as they stood all in a row.
10148{_}2845{_}Then Robin came and threw a chain of gold about Sir Richard's neck, and Will Scarlet knelt and buckled the golden spurs upon his heel; and now Little John led forward Sir Richard's horse, and the Knight mounted.
10148{_}2846{_}He looked down at Robin for a little time, then of a sudden stooped and kissed his cheek.
10148{_}2847{_}All the forest glades rang with the shout that went up as the Knight and the yeomen marched off through the woodland with glare of torches and gleam of steel, and so were gone.
10148{_}2848{_}Then up spake the Bishop of Hereford in a mournful voice, "I, too, must be jogging, good fellow, for the night waxes late."
10148{_}2849{_}But Robin laid his hand upon the Bishop's arm and stayed him.
10148{_}2850{_}"Be not so hasty, Lord Bishop," said he.
10148{_}2851{_}"Three days hence Sir Richard must pay his debts to Emmet; until that time thou must be content to abide with me lest thou breed trouble for the Knight.
10148{_}2852{_}I promise thee that thou shalt have great sport, for I know that thou art fond of hunting the dun deer.
10148{_}2853{_}Lay by thy mantle of melancholy, and strive to lead a joyous yeoman life for three stout days.
10148{_}2854{_}I promise thee thou shalt be sorry to go when the time has come."
10148{_}2855{_}So the Bishop and his train abided with Robin for three days, and much sport his lordship had in that time, so that, as Robin had said, when the time had come for him to go he was sorry to leave the greenwood.
10148{_}2856{_}At the end of three days Robin set him free, and sent him forth from the forest with a guard of yeomen to keep freebooters from taking what was left of the packs and bundles.
10148{_}2857{_}But, as the Bishop rode away, he vowed within himself that he would sometime make Robin rue the day that he stopped him in Sherwood.
10148{_}2858{_}But now we shall follow Sir Richard; so listen, and you shall hear what befell him, and how he paid his debts at Emmet Priory, and likewise in due season to Robin Hood.
10148{_}2859{_}How Sir Richard of the Lea Paid His Debts  THE LONG HIGHWAY stretched straight on, gray and dusty in the sun.
10148{_}2860{_}On either side were dikes full of water bordered by osiers, and far away in the distance stood the towers of Emmet Priory with tall poplar trees around.
10148{_}2861{_}Along the causeway rode a knight with a score of stout men-at-arms behind him.
10148{_}2862{_}The Knight was clad in a plain, long robe of gray serge, gathered in at the waist with a broad leathern belt, from which hung a long dagger and a stout sword.
10148{_}2863{_}But though he was so plainly dressed himself, the horse he rode was a noble barb, and its trappings were rich with silk and silver bells.
10148{_}2864{_}So thus the band journeyed along the causeway between the dikes, till at last they reached the great gate of Emmet Priory.
10148{_}2865{_}There the Knight called to one of his men and bade him knock at the porter's lodge with the heft of his sword.
10148{_}2866{_}The porter was drowsing on his bench within the lodge, but at the knock he roused himself and, opening the wicket, came hobbling forth and greeted the Knight, while a tame starling that hung in a wicker cage within piped out, "_In coelo quies!
10148{_}2867{_}In coelo quies!_" such being the words that the poor old lame porter had taught him to speak.
10148{_}2868{_}"Where is thy prior?"
10148{_}2869{_}asked the Knight of the old porter.
10148{_}2870{_}"He is at meat, good knight, and he looketh for thy coming," quoth the porter, "for, if I mistake not, thou art Sir Richard of the Lea."
10148{_}2871{_}"I am Sir Richard of the Lea; then I will go seek him forthwith," said the Knight.
10148{_}2872{_}"But shall I not send thy horse to stable?"
10148{_}2873{_}said the porter.
10148{_}2874{_}"By Our Lady, it is the noblest nag, and the best harnessed, that e'er I saw in all my life before."
10148{_}2875{_}And he stroked the horse's flank with his palm.
10148{_}2876{_}"Nay," quoth Sir Richard, "the stables of this place are not for me, so make way, I prythee."
10148{_}2877{_}So saying, he pushed forward, and, the gates being opened, he entered the stony courtyard of the Priory, his men behind him.
10148{_}2878{_}In they came with rattle of steel and clashing of swords, and ring of horses' feet on cobblestones, whereat a flock of pigeons that strutted in the sun flew with flapping wings to the high eaves of the round towers.
10148{_}2879{_}While the Knight was riding along the causeway to Emmet, a merry feast was toward in the refectory there.
10148{_}2880{_}The afternoon sun streamed in through the great arched windows and lay in broad squares of light upon the stone floor and across the board covered with a snowy linen cloth, whereon was spread a princely feast.
10148{_}2881{_}At the head of the table sat Prior Vincent of Emmet all clad in soft robes of fine cloth and silk; on his head was a black velvet cap picked out with gold, and around his neck hung a heavy chain of gold, with a great locket pendant therefrom.
10148{_}2882{_}Beside him, on the arm of his great chair, roosted his favorite falcon, for the Prior was fond of the gentle craft of hawking.
10148{_}2883{_}On his right hand sat the Sheriff of Nottingham in rich robes of purple all trimmed about with fur, and on his left a famous doctor of law in dark and sober garb.
10148{_}2884{_}Below these sat the high cellarer of Emmet, and others chief among the brethren.
10148{_}2885{_}Jest and laughter passed around, and all was as merry as merry could be.
10148{_}2886{_}The wizened face of the man of law was twisted into a wrinkled smile, for in his pouch were fourscore golden angels that the Prior had paid him in fee for the case betwixt him and Sir Richard of the Lea.
10148{_}2887{_}The learned doctor had been paid beforehand, for he had not overmuch trust in the holy Vincent of Emmet.
10148{_}2888{_}Quoth the Sheriff of Nottingham, "But art thou sure, Sir Prior, that thou hast the lands so safe?"
10148{_}2889{_}"Ay, marry," said Prior Vincent, smacking his lips after a deep draught of wine, "I have kept a close watch upon him, albeit he was unawares of the same, and I know right well that he hath no money to pay me withal."
10148{_}2890{_}"Ay, true," said the man of law in a dry, husky voice, "his land is surely forfeit if he cometh not to pay; but, Sir Prior, thou must get a release beneath his sign manual, or else thou canst not hope to hold the land without trouble from him."
10148{_}2891{_}"Yea," said the Prior, "so thou hast told me ere now, but I know that this knight is so poor that he will gladly sign away his lands for two hundred pounds of hard money."
10148{_}2892{_}Then up spake the high cellarer, "Methinks it is a shame to so drive a misfortunate knight to the ditch.
10148{_}2893{_}I think it sorrow that the noblest estate in Derbyshire should so pass away from him for a paltry five hundred pounds.
10148{_}2894{_}Truly, I--"  "How now," broke in the Prior in a quivering voice, his eyes glistening and his cheeks red with anger, "dost thou prate to my very beard, sirrah?
10148{_}2895{_}By Saint Hubert, thou hadst best save thy breath to cool thy pottage, else it may scald thy mouth."
10148{_}2896{_}"Nay," said the man of law smoothly, "I dare swear this same knight will never come to settlement this day, but will prove recreant.
10148{_}2897{_}Nevertheless, we will seek some means to gain his lands from him, so never fear."
10148{_}2898{_}But even as the doctor spoke, there came a sudden clatter of horses' hoofs and a jingle of iron mail in the courtyard below.
10148{_}2899{_}Then up spake the Prior and called upon one of the brethren that sat below the salt, and bade him look out of the window and see who was below, albeit he knew right well it could be none but Sir Richard.
10148{_}2900{_}So the brother arose and went and looked, and he said, "I see below a score of stout men-at-arms and a knight just dismounting from his horse.
10148{_}2901{_}He is dressed in long robes of gray which, methinks, are of poor seeming; but the horse he rideth upon hath the richest coursing that ever I saw.
10148{_}2902{_}The Knight dismounts and they come this way, and are even now below in the great hall."
10148{_}2903{_}"Lo, see ye there now," quoth Prior Vincent.
10148{_}2904{_}"Here ye have a knight with so lean a purse as scarce to buy him a crust of bread to munch, yet he keeps a band of retainers and puts rich trappings upon his horse's hide, while his own back goeth bare.
10148{_}2905{_}Is it not well that such men should be brought low?"
10148{_}2906{_}"But art thou sure," said the little doctor tremulously, "that this knight will do us no harm?
10148{_}2907{_}Such as he are fierce when crossed, and he hath a band of naughty men at his heels.
10148{_}2908{_}Mayhap thou hadst better give an extension of his debt."
10148{_}2909{_}Thus he spake, for he was afraid Sir Richard might do him a harm.
10148{_}2910{_}"Thou needst not fear," said the Prior, looking down at the little man beside him.
10148{_}2911{_}"This knight is gentle and would as soon think of harming an old woman as thee."
10148{_}2912{_}As the Prior finished, a door at the lower end of the refectory swung open, and in came Sir Richard, with folded hands and head bowed upon his breast.
10148{_}2913{_}Thus humbly he walked slowly up the hall, while his men-at-arms stood about the door.
10148{_}2914{_}When he had come to where the Prior sat, he knelt upon one knee.
10148{_}2915{_}"Save and keep thee, Sir Prior," said he, "I am come to keep my day."
10148{_}2916{_}Then the first word that the Prior said to him was "Hast thou brought my money?"
10148{_}2917{_}"Alas!
10148{_}2918{_}I have not so much as one penny upon my body," said the Knight; whereat the Prior's eyes sparkled.
10148{_}2919{_}"Now, thou art a shrewd debtor, I wot," said he.
10148{_}2920{_}Then, "Sir Sheriff, I drink to thee."
10148{_}2921{_}But still the Knight kneeled upon the hard stones, so the Prior turned to him again.
10148{_}2922{_}"What wouldst thou have?"
10148{_}2923{_}quoth he sharply.
10148{_}2924{_}At these words, a slow red mounted into the Knight's cheeks; but still he knelt.
10148{_}2925{_}"I would crave thy mercy," said he.
10148{_}2926{_}"As thou hopest for Heaven's mercy, show mercy to me.
10148{_}2927{_}Strip me not of my lands and so reduce a true knight to poverty."
10148{_}2928{_}"Thy day is broken and thy lands forfeit," said the man of law, plucking up his spirits at the Knight's humble speech.
10148{_}2929{_}Quoth Sir Richard, "Thou man of law, wilt thou not befriend me in mine hour of need?"
10148{_}2930{_}"Nay," said the other, "I hold with this holy Prior, who hath paid me my fees in hard gold, so that I am bounder to him."
10148{_}2931{_}"Wilt thou not be my friend, Sir Sheriff?"
10148{_}2932{_}said Sir Richard.
10148{_}2933{_}"Nay, 'fore Heaven," quoth the Sheriff of Nottingham, "this is no business of mine, yet I will do what I may," and he nudged the Prior beneath the cloth with his knee.
10148{_}2934{_}"Wilt thou not ease him of some of his debts, Sir Prior?"
10148{_}2935{_}At this the Prior smiled grimly.
10148{_}2936{_}"Pay me three hundred pounds, Sir Richard," said he, "and I will give thee quittance of thy debt."
10148{_}2937{_}"Thou knowest, Sir Prior, that it is as easy for me to pay four hundred pounds as three hundred," said Sir Richard.
10148{_}2938{_}"But wilt thou not give me another twelvemonth to pay my debt?"
10148{_}2939{_}"Not another day," said the Prior sternly.
10148{_}2940{_}"And is this all thou wilt do for me?"
10148{_}2941{_}asked the Knight.
10148{_}2942{_}"Now, out upon thee, false knight!"
10148{_}2943{_}cried the Prior, bursting forth in anger.
10148{_}2944{_}"Either pay thy debt as I have said, or release thy land and get thee gone from out my hall."
10148{_}2945{_}Then Sir Richard arose to his feet.
10148{_}2946{_}"Thou false, lying priest!"
10148{_}2947{_}said he in so stern a voice that the man of law shrunk affrighted, "I am no false knight, as thou knowest full well, but have even held my place in the press and the tourney.
10148{_}2948{_}Hast thou so little courtesy that thou wouldst see a true knight kneel for all this time, or see him come into thy hall and never offer him meat or drink?"
10148{_}2949{_}Then quoth the man of law in a trembling voice, "This is surely an ill way to talk of matters appertaining to business; let us be mild in speech.
10148{_}2950{_}What wilt thou pay this knight, Sir Prior, to give thee release of his land?"
10148{_}2951{_}"I would have given him two hundred pounds," quoth the Prior, "but since he hath spoken so vilely to my teeth, not one groat over one hundred pounds will he get."
10148{_}2952{_}"Hadst thou offered me a thousand pounds, false prior," said the Knight, "thou wouldst not have got an inch of my land."
10148{_}2953{_}Then turning to where his men-at-arms stood near the door, he called, "Come hither," and beckoned with his finger; whereupon the tallest of them all came forward and handed him a long leathern bag.
10148{_}2954{_}Sir Richard took the bag and shot from it upon the table a glittering stream of golden money.
10148{_}2955{_}"Bear in mind, Sir Prior," said he, "that thou hast promised me quittance for three hundred pounds.
10148{_}2956{_}Not one farthing above that shalt thou get."
10148{_}2957{_}So saying, he counted out three hundred pounds and pushed it toward the Prior.
10148{_}2958{_}But now the Prior's hands dropped at his sides and the Prior's head hung upon his shoulder, for not only had he lost all hopes of the land, but he had forgiven the Knight one hundred pounds of his debt and had needlessly paid the man of law fourscore angels.
10148{_}2959{_}To him he turned, and quoth he, "Give me back my money that thou hast."
10148{_}2960{_}"Nay," cried the other shrilly, "it is but my fee that thou didst pay me, and thou gettest it not back again."
10148{_}2961{_}And he hugged his gown about him.
10148{_}2962{_}"Now, Sir Prior," quoth Sir Richard, "I have held my day and paid all the dues demanded of me; so, as there is no more betwixt us, I leave this vile place straightway."
10148{_}2963{_}So saying, he turned upon his heel and strode away.
10148{_}2964{_}All this time the Sheriff had been staring with wide-open eyes and mouth agape at the tall man-at-arms, who stood as though carved out of stone.
10148{_}2965{_}At last he gasped out, "Reynold Greenleaf!"
10148{_}2966{_}At this, the tall man-at-arms, who was no other than Little John, turned, grinning, to the Sheriff.
10148{_}2967{_}"I give thee good den, fair gossip," quoth he.
10148{_}2968{_}"I would say, sweet Sheriff, that I have heard all thy pretty talk this day, and it shall be duly told unto Robin Hood.
10148{_}2969{_}So, farewell for the nonce, till we meet again in Sherwood Forest."
10148{_}2970{_}Then he, also, turned and followed Sir Richard down the hall, leaving the Sheriff, all pale and amazed, shrunk together upon his chair.
10148{_}2971{_}A merry feast it was to which Sir Richard came, but a sorry lot he left behind him, and little hunger had they for the princely food spread before them.
10148{_}2972{_}Only the learned doctor was happy, for he had his fee.
10148{_}2973{_}Now a twelvemonth and a day passed since Prior Vincent of Emmet sat at feast, and once more the mellow fall of another year had come.
10148{_}2974{_}But the year had brought great change, I wot, to the lands of Sir Richard of the Lea; for, where before shaggy wild grasses grew upon the meadow lands, now all stretch away in golden stubble, betokening that a rich and plentiful crop had been gathered therefrom.
10148{_}2975{_}A year had made a great change in the castle, also, for, where were empty moats and the crumbling of neglect, all was now orderly and well kept.
10148{_}2976{_}Bright shone the sun on battlement and tower, and in the blue air overhead a Hock of clattering jackdaws flew around the gilded weather vane and spire.
10148{_}2977{_}Then, in the brightness of the morning, the drawbridge fell across the moat with a rattle and clank of chains, the gate of the castle swung slowly open, and a goodly array of steel-clad men-at-arms, with a knight all clothed in chain mail, as white as frost on brier and thorn of a winter morning, came flashing out from the castle courtyard.
10148{_}2978{_}In his hand the Knight held a great spear, from the point of which fluttered a blood-red pennant as broad as the palm of one's hand.
10148{_}2979{_}So this troop came forth from the castle, and in the midst of them walked three pack horses laden with parcels of divers shapes and kinds.
10148{_}2980{_}Thus rode forth good Sir Richard of the Lea to pay his debt to Robin Hood this bright and merry morn.
10148{_}2981{_}Along the highway they wended their way, with measured tramp of feet and rattle and jingle of sword and harness.
10148{_}2982{_}Onward they marched till they came nigh to Denby, where, from the top of a hill, they saw, over beyond the town, many gay flags and streamers floating in the bright air.
10148{_}2983{_}Then Sir Richard turned to the man-at-arms nearest to him.
10148{_}2984{_}"What is toward yonder at Denby today?"
10148{_}2985{_}quoth he.
10148{_}2986{_}"Please Your Worship," answered the man-at-arms, "a merry fair is held there today, and a great wrestling match, to which many folk have come, for a prize hath been offered of a pipe of red wine, a fair golden ring, and a pair of gloves, all of which go to the best wrestler."
10148{_}2987{_}"Now, by my faith," quoth Sir Richard, who loved good manly sports right well, "this will be a goodly thing to see.
10148{_}2988{_}Methinks we have to stay a little while on our journey, and see this merry sport."
10148{_}2989{_}So he turned his horse's head aside toward Denby and the fair, and thither he and his men made their way.
10148{_}2990{_}There they found a great hubbub of merriment.
10148{_}2991{_}Flags and streamers were floating, tumblers were tumbling on the green, bagpipes were playing, and lads and lasses were dancing to the music.
10148{_}2992{_}But the crowd were gathered most of all around a ring where the wrestling was going forward, and thither Sir Richard and his men turned their steps.
10148{_}2993{_}Now when the judges of the wrestling saw Sir Richard coming and knew who he was, the chief of them came down from the bench where he and the others sat, and went to the Knight and took him by the hand, beseeching him to come and sit with them and judge the sport.
10148{_}2994{_}So Sir Richard got down from his horse and went with the others to the bench raised beside the ring.
10148{_}2995{_}Now there had been great doings that morning, for a certain yeoman named Egbert, who came from Stoke over in Staffordshire, had thrown with ease all those that came against him; but a man of Denby, well known through all the countryside as William of the Scar, had been biding his time with the Stoke man; so, when Egbert had thrown everyone else, stout William leaped into the ring.
10148{_}2996{_}Then a tough bout followed, and at last he threw Egbert heavily, whereat there was a great shouting and shaking of hands, for all the Denby men were proud of their wrestler.
10148{_}2997{_}When Sir Richard came, he found stout William, puffed up by the shouts of his friends, walking up and down the ring, daring anyone to come and try a throw with him.
10148{_}2998{_}"Come one, come all!"
10148{_}2999{_}quoth he.
10148{_}3000{_}"Here stand I, William of the Scar, against any man.
10148{_}3001{_}If there is none in Derbyshire to come against me, come all who will, from Nottingham, Stafford, or York, and if I do not make them one and all root the ground with their noses like swine in the forests, call me no more brave William the wrestler."
10148{_}3002{_}At this all laughed; but above all the laughter a loud voice was heard to cry out, "Sin' thou talkest so big, here cometh one from Nottinghamshire to try a fall with thee, fellow"; and straightway a tall youth with a tough quarterstaff in his hand came pushing his way through the crowd and at last leaped lightly over the rope into the ring.
10148{_}3003{_}He was not as heavy as stout William, but he was taller and broader in the shoulders, and all his joints were well knit.
10148{_}3004{_}Sir Richard looked upon him keenly, then, turning to one of the judges, he said, "Knowest thou who this youth is?
10148{_}3005{_}Methinks I have seen him before."
10148{_}3006{_}"Nay," said the judge, "he is a stranger to me."
10148{_}3007{_}Meantime, without a word, the young man, laying aside his quarterstaff, began to take off his jerkin and body clothing until he presently stood with naked arms and body; and a comely sight he was when so bared to the view, for his muscles were cut round and smooth and sharp like swift- running water.
10148{_}3008{_}And now each man spat upon his hands and, clapping them upon his knees, squatted down, watching the other keenly, so as to take the vantage of him in the grip.
10148{_}3009{_}Then like a flash they leaped together, and a great shout went up, for William had gotten the better hold of the two.
10148{_}3010{_}For a short time they strained and struggled and writhed, and then stout William gave his most cunning trip and throw, but the stranger met it with greater skill than his, and so the trip came to nought.
10148{_}3011{_}Then, of a sudden, with a twist and a wrench, the stranger loosed himself, and he of the scar found himself locked in a pair of arms that fairly made his ribs crack.
10148{_}3012{_}So, with heavy, hot breathing, they stood for a while straining, their bodies all glistening with sweat, and great drops of sweat trickling down their faces.
10148{_}3013{_}But the stranger's hug was so close that at last stout William's muscles softened under his grip, and he gave a sob.
10148{_}3014{_}Then the youth put forth all his strength and gave a sudden trip with his heel and a cast over his right hip, and down stout William went, with a sickening thud, and lay as though he would never move hand nor foot again.
10148{_}3015{_}But now no shout went up for the stranger, but an angry murmur was heard among the crowd, so easily had he won the match.
10148{_}3016{_}Then one of the judges, a kinsman to William of the Scar, rose with trembling lip and baleful look.
10148{_}3017{_}Quoth he, "If thou hath slain that man it will go ill with thee, let me tell thee, fellow."
10148{_}3018{_}But the stranger answered boldly, "He took his chance with me as I took mine with him.
10148{_}3019{_}No law can touch me to harm me, even if I slew him, so that it was fairly done in the wrestling ring."
10148{_}3020{_}"That we shall see," said the judge, scowling upon the youth, while once more an angry murmur ran around the crowd; for, as I have said, the men of Denby were proud of stout William of the Scar.
10148{_}3021{_}Then up spoke Sir Richard gently.
10148{_}3022{_}"Nay," said he, "the youth is right; if the other dieth, he dieth in the wrestling ring, where he took his chance, and was cast fairly enow."
10148{_}3023{_}But in the meantime three men had come forward and lifted stout William from the ground and found that he was not dead, though badly shaken by his heavy fall.
10148{_}3024{_}Then the chief judge rose and said, "Young man, the prize is duly thine.
10148{_}3025{_}Here is the red-gold ring, and here the gloves, and yonder stands the pipe of wine to do with whatsoever thou dost list."
10148{_}3026{_}At this, the youth, who had donned his clothes and taken up his staff again, bowed without a word, then, taking the gloves and the ring, and thrusting the one into his girdle and slipping the other upon his thumb, he turned and, leaping lightly over the ropes again, made his way through the crowd, and was gone.
10148{_}3027{_}"Now, I wonder who yon youth may be," said the judge, turning to Sir Richard, "he seemeth like a stout Saxon from his red cheeks and fair hair.
10148{_}3028{_}This William of ours is a stout man, too, and never have I seen him cast in the ring before, albeit he hath not yet striven with such great wrestlers as Thomas of Cornwall, Diccon of York, and young David of Doncaster.
10148{_}3029{_}Hath he not a firm foot in the ring, thinkest thou, Sir Richard?"
10148{_}3030{_}"Ay, truly, and yet this youth threw him fairly, and with wondrous ease.
10148{_}3031{_}I much wonder who he can be."
10148{_}3032{_}Thus said Sir Richard in a thoughtful voice.
10148{_}3033{_}For a time the Knight stood talking to those about him, but at last he arose and made ready to depart, so he called his men about him and, tightening the girths of his saddle, he mounted his horse once more.
10148{_}3034{_}Meanwhile the young stranger had made his way through the crowd, but, as he passed, he heard all around him such words muttered as "Look at the cockerel!"
10148{_}3035{_}"Behold how he plumeth himself!"
10148{_}3036{_}"I dare swear he cast good William unfairly!"
10148{_}3037{_}"Yea, truly, saw ye not birdlime upon his hands?"
10148{_}3038{_}"It would be well to cut his cock's comb!"
10148{_}3039{_}To all this the stranger paid no heed, but strode proudly about as though he heard it not.
10148{_}3040{_}So he walked slowly across the green to where the booth stood wherein was dancing, and standing at the door he looked in on the sport.
10148{_}3041{_}As he stood thus, a stone struck his arm of a sudden with a sharp jar, and, turning, he saw that an angry crowd of men had followed him from the wrestling ring.
10148{_}3042{_}Then, when they saw him turn so, a great hooting and yelling arose from all, so that the folk came running out from the dancing booth to see what was to do.
10148{_}3043{_}At last a tall, broad-shouldered, burly blacksmith strode forward from the crowd swinging a mighty blackthorn club in his hand.
10148{_}3044{_}"Wouldst thou come here to our fair town of Denby, thou Jack in the Box, to overcome a good honest lad with vile, juggling tricks?"
10148{_}3045{_}growled he in a deep voice like the bellow of an angry bull.
10148{_}3046{_}"Take that, then!"
10148{_}3047{_}And of a sudden he struck a blow at the youth that might have felled an ox.
10148{_}3048{_}But the other turned the blow deftly aside, and gave back another so terrible that the Denby man went down with a groan, as though he had been smitten by lightning.
10148{_}3049{_}When they saw their leader fall, the crowd gave another angry shout; but the stranger placed his back against the tent near which he stood, swinging his terrible staff, and so fell had been the blow that he struck the stout smith that none dared to come within the measure of his cudgel, so the press crowded back, like a pack of dogs from a bear at bay.
10148{_}3050{_}But now some coward hand from behind threw a sharp jagged stone that smote the stranger on the crown, so that he staggered back, and the red blood gushed from the cut and ran down his face and over his jerkin.
10148{_}3051{_}Then, seeing him dazed with this vile blow, the crowd rushed upon him, so that they overbore him and he fell beneath their feet.
10148{_}3052{_}Now it might have gone ill with the youth, even to the losing of his young life, had not Sir Richard come to this fair; for of a sudden, shouts were heard, and steel flashed in the air, and blows were given with the flat of swords, while through the midst of the crowd Sir Richard of the Lea came spurring on his white horse.
10148{_}3053{_}Then the crowd, seeing the steel-clad knight and the armed men, melted away like snow on the warm hearth, leaving the young man all bloody and dusty upon the ground.
10148{_}3054{_}Finding himself free, the youth arose and, wiping the blood from his face, looked up.
10148{_}3055{_}Quoth he, "Sir Richard of the Lea, mayhap thou hast saved my life this day."
10148{_}3056{_}"Who art thou that knowest Sir Richard of the Lea so well?"
10148{_}3057{_}quoth the Knight.
10148{_}3058{_}"Methinks I have seen thy face before, young man."
10148{_}3059{_}"Yea, thou hast," said the youth, "for men call me David of Doncaster."
10148{_}3060{_}"Ha!"
10148{_}3061{_}said Sir Richard, "I wonder that I knew thee not, David; but thy beard hath grown longer, and thou thyself art more set in manhood since this day twelvemonth.
10148{_}3062{_}Come hither into the tent, David, and wash the blood from thy face.
10148{_}3063{_}And thou, Ralph, bring him straightway a clean jerkin.
10148{_}3064{_}Now I am sorry for thee, yet I am right glad that I have had a chance to pay a part of my debt of kindness to thy good master Robin Hood, for it might have gone ill with thee had I not come, young man."
10148{_}3065{_}So saying, the Knight led David into the tent, and there the youth washed the blood from his face and put on the clean jerkin.
10148{_}3066{_}In the meantime a whisper had gone around from those that stood nearest that this was none other than the great David of Doncaster, the best wrestler in all the mid-country, who only last spring had cast stout Adam o' Lincoln in the ring at Selby, in Yorkshire, and now held the mid-country champion belt, Thus it happened that when young David came forth from the tent along with Sir Richard, the blood all washed from his face, and his soiled jerkin changed for a clean one, no sounds of anger were heard, but all pressed forward to see the young man, feeling proud that one of the great wrestlers of England should have entered the ring at Denby fair.
10148{_}3067{_}For thus fickle is a mass of men.
10148{_}3068{_}Then Sir Richard called aloud, "Friends, this is David of Doncaster; so think it no shame that your Denby man was cast by such a wrestler.
10148{_}3069{_}He beareth you no ill will for what hath passed, but let it be a warning to you how ye treat strangers henceforth.
10148{_}3070{_}Had ye slain him it would have been an ill day for you, for Robin Hood would have harried your town as the kestrel harries the dovecote.
10148{_}3071{_}I have bought the pipe of wine from him, and now I give it freely to you to drink as ye list.
10148{_}3072{_}But never hereafterward fall upon a man for being a stout yeoman."
10148{_}3073{_}At this all shouted amain; but in truth they thought more of the wine than of the Knight's words.
10148{_}3074{_}Then Sir Richard, with David beside him and his men-at-arms around, turned about and left the fair.
10148{_}3075{_}But in after days, when the men that saw that wrestling bout were bent with age, they would shake their heads when they heard of any stalwart game, and say, "Ay, ay; but thou shouldst have seen the great David of Doncaster cast stout William of the Scar at Denby fair."
10148{_}3076{_}Robin Hood stood in the merry greenwood with Little John and most of his stout yeomen around him, awaiting Sir Richard's coming.
10148{_}3077{_}At last a glint of steel was seen through the brown forest leaves, and forth from the covert into the open rode Sir Richard at the head of his men.
10148{_}3078{_}He came straight forward to Robin Hood and leaping from off his horse, clasped the yeoman in his arms.
10148{_}3079{_}"Why, how now," said Robin, after a time, holding Sir Richard off and looking at him from top to toe, "methinks thou art a gayer bird than when I saw thee last."
10148{_}3080{_}"Yes, thanks to thee, Robin," said the Knight, laying his hand upon the yeoman's shoulder.
10148{_}3081{_}"But for thee I would have been wandering in misery in a far country by this time.
10148{_}3082{_}But I have kept my word, Robin, and have brought back the money that thou didst lend me, and which I have doubled four times over again, and so become rich once more.
10148{_}3083{_}Along with this money I have brought a little gift to thee and thy brave men from my dear lady and myself."
10148{_}3084{_}Then, turning to his men, he called aloud, "Bring forth the pack horses."
10148{_}3085{_}But Robin stopped him.
10148{_}3086{_}"Nay, Sir Richard," said he, "think it not bold of me to cross thy bidding, but we of Sherwood do no business till after we have eaten and drunk."
10148{_}3087{_}Whereupon, taking Sir Richard by the hand, he led him to the seat beneath the greenwood tree, while others of the chief men of the band came and seated themselves around.
10148{_}3088{_}Then quoth Robin, "How cometh it that I saw young David of Doncaster with thee and thy men, Sir Knight?"
10148{_}3089{_}Then straightway the Knight told all about his stay at Denby and of the happening at the fair, and how it was like to go hard with young David; so he told his tale, and quoth he, "It was this, good Robin, that kept me so late on the way, otherwise I would have been here an hour agone."
10148{_}3090{_}Then, when he had done speaking, Robin stretched out his hand and grasped the Knight's palm.
10148{_}3091{_}Quoth he in a trembling voice, "I owe thee a debt I can never hope to repay, Sir Richard, for let me tell thee, I would rather lose my right hand than have such ill befall young David of Doncaster as seemed like to come upon him at Denby."
10148{_}3092{_}So they talked until after a while one came forward to say that the feast was spread; whereupon all arose and went thereto.
10148{_}3093{_}When at last it was done, the Knight called upon his men to bring the pack horses forward, which they did according to his bidding.
10148{_}3094{_}Then one of the men brought the Knight a strongbox, which he opened and took from it a bag and counted out five hundred pounds, the sum he had gotten from Robin.
10148{_}3095{_}"Sir Richard," quoth Robin, "thou wilt pleasure us all if thou wilt keep that money as a gift from us of Sherwood.
10148{_}3096{_}Is it not so, my lads?"
10148{_}3097{_}Then all shouted "Ay" with a mighty voice.
10148{_}3098{_}"I thank you all deeply," said the Knight earnestly, "but think it not ill of me if I cannot take it.
10148{_}3099{_}Gladly have I borrowed it from you, but it may not be that I can take it as a gift."
10148{_}3100{_}Then Robin Hood said no more but gave the money to Little John to put away in the treasury, for he had shrewdness enough to know that nought breeds ill will and heart bitterness like gifts forced upon one that cannot choose but take them.
10148{_}3101{_}Then Sir Richard had the packs laid upon the ground and opened, whereupon a great shout went up that made the forest ring again, for lo, there were tenscore bows of finest Spanish yew, all burnished till they shone again, and each bow inlaid with fanciful figures in silver, yet not inlaid so as to mar their strength.
10148{_}3102{_}Beside these were tenscore quivers of leather embroidered with golden thread, and in each quiver were a score of shafts with burnished heads that shone like silver; each shaft was feathered with peacock's plumes, innocked with silver.
10148{_}3103{_}Sir Richard gave to each yeoman a bow and a quiver of arrows, but to Robin he gave a stout bow inlaid with the cunningest workmanship in gold, while each arrow in his quiver was innocked with gold.
10148{_}3104{_}Then all shouted again for joy of the fair gift, and all swore among themselves that they would die if need be for Sir Richard and his lady.
10148{_}3105{_}At last the time came when Sir Richard must go, whereupon Robin Hood called his band around him, and each man of the yeomen took a torch in his hand to light the way through the woodlands.
10148{_}3106{_}So they came to the edge of Sherwood, and there the Knight kissed Robin upon the cheeks and left him and was gone.
10148{_}3107{_}Thus Robin Hood helped a noble knight out of his dire misfortunes, that else would have smothered the happiness from his life.
10148{_}3108{_}Little John Turns Barefoot Friar  COLD WINTER had passed and spring had come.
10148{_}3109{_}No leafy thickness had yet clad the woodlands, but the budding leaves hung like a tender mist about the trees.
10148{_}3110{_}In the open country the meadow lands lay a sheeny green, the cornfields a dark velvety color, for they were thick and soft with the growing blades.
10148{_}3111{_}The plowboy shouted in the sun, and in the purple new- turned furrows flocks of birds hunted for fat worms.
10148{_}3112{_}All the broad moist earth smiled in the warm light, and each little green hill clapped its hand for joy.
10148{_}3113{_}On a deer's hide, stretched on the ground in the open in front of the greenwood tree, sat Robin Hood basking in the sun like an old dog fox.
10148{_}3114{_}Leaning back with his hands clasped about his knees, he lazily watched Little John rolling a stout bowstring from long strands of hempen thread, wetting the palms of his hands ever and anon, and rolling the cord upon his thigh.
10148{_}3115{_}Near by sat Allan a Dale fitting a new string to his harp.
