10148{_}3116{_}Quoth Robin at last, "Methinks I would rather roam this forest in the gentle springtime than be King of all merry England.
10148{_}3117{_}What palace in the broad world is as fair as this sweet woodland just now, and what king in all the world hath such appetite for plover's eggs and lampreys as I for juicy venison and sparkling ale?
10148{_}3118{_}Gaffer Swanthold speaks truly when he saith, 'Better a crust with content than honey with a sour heart.'"
10148{_}3119{_}"Yea," quoth Little John, as he rubbed his new-made bowstring with yellow beeswax, "the life we lead is the life for me.
10148{_}3120{_}Thou speakest of the springtime, but methinks even the winter hath its own joys.
10148{_}3121{_}Thou and I, good master, have had more than one merry day, this winter past, at the Blue Boar.
10148{_}3122{_}Dost thou not remember that night thou and Will Stutely and Friar Tuck and I passed at that same hostelry with the two beggars and the strolling friar?"
10148{_}3123{_}"Yea," quoth merry Robin, laughing, "that was the night that Will Stutely must needs snatch a kiss from the stout hostess, and got a canakin of ale emptied over his head for his pains."
10148{_}3124{_}"Truly, it was the same," said Little John, laughing also.
10148{_}3125{_}"Methinks that was a goodly song that the strolling friar sang.
10148{_}3126{_}Friar Tuck, thou hast a quick ear for a tune, dost thou not remember it?"
10148{_}3127{_}"I did have the catch of it one time," said Tuck.
10148{_}3128{_}"Let me see," and he touched his forefinger to his forehead in thought, humming to himself, and stopping ever and anon to fit what he had got to what he searched for in his mind.
10148{_}3129{_}At last he found it all and clearing his throat, sang merrily:   "_In the blossoming hedge the robin cock sings,      For the sun it is merry and bright,  And he joyfully hops and he flutters his wings,      For his heart is all full of delight.
10148{_}3130{_}For the May bloometh fair,           And there's little of care,  And plenty to eat in the Maytime rare.
10148{_}3131{_}When the flowers all die,           Then off he will fly,           To keep himself warm           In some jolly old barn  Where the snow and the wind neither chill him nor harm.
10148{_}3132{_}"And such is the life of the strolling friar,      With aplenty to eat and to drink;  For the goodwife will keep him a seat by the fire,      And the pretty girls smile at his wink.
10148{_}3133{_}Then he lustily trolls           As he onward strolls,  A rollicking song for the saving of souls.
10148{_}3134{_}When the wind doth blow,           With the coming of snow,           There's a place by the fire           For the fatherly friar,  And a crab in the bowl for his heart's desire_."
10148{_}3135{_}Thus Friar Tuck sang in a rich and mellow voice, rolling his head from side to side in time with the music, and when he had done, all clapped their hands and shouted with laughter, for the song fitted him well.
10148{_}3136{_}"In very sooth," quoth Little John, "it is a goodly song, and, were I not a yeoman of Sherwood Forest, I had rather be a strolling friar than aught else in the world."
10148{_}3137{_}"Yea, it is a goodly song," said Robin Hood, "but methought those two burly beggars told the merrier tales and led the merrier life.
10148{_}3138{_}Dost thou not remember what that great black-bearded fellow told of his begging at the fair in York?"
10148{_}3139{_}"Yea," said Little John, "but what told the friar of the harvest home in Kentshire?
10148{_}3140{_}I hold that he led a merrier life than the other two."
10148{_}3141{_}"Truly, for the honor of the cloth," quoth Friar Tuck, "I hold with my good gossip, Little John."
10148{_}3142{_}"Now," quoth Robin, "I hold to mine own mind.
10148{_}3143{_}But what sayst thou, Little John, to a merry adventure this fair day?
10148{_}3144{_}Take thou a friar's gown from our chest of strange garments, and don the same, and I will stop the first beggar I meet and change clothes with him.
10148{_}3145{_}Then let us wander the country about, this sweet day, and see what befalls each of us."
10148{_}3146{_}"That fitteth my mind," quoth Little John, "so let us forth, say I."
10148{_}3147{_}Thereupon Little John and Friar Tuck went to the storehouse of the band, and there chose for the yeoman the robe of a Gray Friar.
10148{_}3148{_}Then they came forth again, and a mighty roar of laughter went up, for not only had the band never seen Little John in such guise before, but the robe was too short for him by a good palm's-breadth.
10148{_}3149{_}But Little John's hands were folded in his loose sleeves, and Little John's eyes were cast upon the ground, and at his girdle hung a great, long string of beads.
10148{_}3150{_}And now Little John took up his stout staff, at the end of which hung a chubby little leathern pottle, such as palmers carry at the tips of their staves; but in it was something, I wot, more like good Malmsey than cold spring water, such as godly pilgrims carry.
10148{_}3151{_}Then up rose Robin and took his stout staff in his hand, likewise, and slipped ten golden angels into his pouch; for no beggar's garb was among the stores of the band, so he was fain to run his chance of meeting a beggar and buying his clothes of him.
10148{_}3152{_}So, all being made ready, the two yeomen set forth on their way, striding lustily along all in the misty morning.
10148{_}3153{_}Thus they walked down the forest path until they came to the highway, and then along the highway till it split in twain, leading on one hand to Blyth and on the other to Gainsborough.
10148{_}3154{_}Here the yeomen stopped.
10148{_}3155{_}Quoth jolly Robin, "Take thou the road to Gainsborough, and I will take that to Blyth.
10148{_}3156{_}So, fare thee well, holy father, and mayst thou not ha' cause to count thy beads in earnest ere we meet again."
10148{_}3157{_}"Good den, good beggar that is to be," quoth Little John, "and mayst thou have no cause to beg for mercy ere I see thee next."
10148{_}3158{_}So each stepped sturdily upon his way until a green hill rose between them, and the one was hid from the sight of the other.
10148{_}3159{_}Little John walked along, whistling, for no one was nigh upon all the road.
10148{_}3160{_}In the budding hedges the little birds twittered merrily, and on either hand the green hills swept up to the sky, the great white clouds of springtime sailing slowly over their crowns in lazy flight.
10148{_}3161{_}Up hill and down dale walked Little John, the fresh wind blowing in his face and his robes fluttering behind him, and so at last he came to a crossroad that led to Tuxford.
10148{_}3162{_}Here he met three pretty lasses, each bearing a basket of eggs to market.
10148{_}3163{_}Quoth he, "Whither away, fair maids?"
10148{_}3164{_}And he stood in their path, holding his staff in front of them, to stop them.
10148{_}3165{_}Then they huddled together and nudged one another, and one presently spake up and said, "We are going to the Tuxford market, holy friar, to sell our eggs."
10148{_}3166{_}"Now out upon it!"
10148{_}3167{_}quoth Little John, looking upon them with his head on one side.
10148{_}3168{_}"Surely, it is a pity that such fair lasses should be forced to carry eggs to market.
10148{_}3169{_}Let me tell you, an I had the shaping of things in this world, ye should all three have been clothed in the finest silks, and ride upon milk-white horses, with pages at your side, and feed upon nothing but whipped cream and strawberries; for such a life would surely befit your looks."
10148{_}3170{_}At this speech all three of the pretty maids looked down, blushing and simpering.
10148{_}3171{_}One said, "La!"
10148{_}3172{_}another, "Marry, a' maketh sport of us!"
10148{_}3173{_}and the third, "Listen, now, to the holy man!"
10148{_}3174{_}But at the same time they looked at Little John from out the corners of their eyes.
10148{_}3175{_}"Now, look you," said Little John, "I cannot see such dainty damsels as ye are carrying baskets along a highroad.
10148{_}3176{_}Let me take them mine own self, and one of you, if ye will, may carry my staff for me."
10148{_}3177{_}"Nay," said one of the lasses, "but thou canst not carry three baskets all at one time."
10148{_}3178{_}"Yea, but I can," said Little John, "and that I will show you presently.
10148{_}3179{_}I thank the good Saint Wilfred that he hath given me a pretty wit.
10148{_}3180{_}Look ye, now.
10148{_}3181{_}Here I take this great basket, so; here I tie my rosary around the handle, thus; and here I slip the rosary over my head and sling the basket upon my back, in this wise."
10148{_}3182{_}And Little John did according to his words, the basket hanging down behind him like a peddler's pack; then, giving his staff to one of the maids, and taking a basket upon either arm, he turned his face toward Tuxford Town and stepped forth merrily, a laughing maid on either side, and one walking ahead, carrying the staff.
10148{_}3183{_}In this wise they journeyed along, and everyone they met stopped and looked after them, laughing, for never had anybody seen such a merry sight as this tall, strapping Gray Friar, with robes all too short for him, laden with eggs, and tramping the road with three pretty lasses.
10148{_}3184{_}For this Little John cared not a whit, but when such folks gave jesting words to him he answered back as merrily, speech for speech.
10148{_}3185{_}So they stepped along toward Tuxford, chatting and laughing, until they came nigh to the town.
10148{_}3186{_}Here Little John stopped and set down the baskets, for he did not care to go into the town lest he should, perchance, meet some of the Sheriff's men.
10148{_}3187{_}"Alas!
10148{_}3188{_}sweet chucks," quoth he, "here I must leave you.
10148{_}3189{_}I had not thought to come this way, but I am glad that I did so.
10148{_}3190{_}Now, ere we part, we must drink sweet friendship."
10148{_}3191{_}So saying, he unslung the leathern pottle from the end of his staff, and, drawing the stopper therefrom, he handed it to the lass who had carried his staff, first wiping the mouth of the pottle upon his sleeve.
10148{_}3192{_}Then each lass took a fair drink of what was within, and when it had passed all around, Little John finished what was left, so that not another drop could be squeezed from it.
10148{_}3193{_}Then, kissing each lass sweetly, he wished them all good den, and left them.
10148{_}3194{_}But the maids stood looking after him as he walked away whistling.
10148{_}3195{_}"What a pity," quoth one, "that such a stout, lusty lad should be in holy orders."
10148{_}3196{_}"Marry," quoth Little John to himself, as he strode along, "yon was no such ill happening; Saint Dunstan send me more of the like."
10148{_}3197{_}After he had trudged along for a time he began to wax thirsty again in the warmth of the day.
10148{_}3198{_}He shook his leathern pottle beside his ear, but not a sound came therefrom.
10148{_}3199{_}Then he placed it to his lips and tilted it high aloft, but not a drop was there.
10148{_}3200{_}"Little John!
10148{_}3201{_}Little John!"
10148{_}3202{_}said he sadly to himself, shaking his head the while, "woman will be thy ruin yet, if thou dost not take better care of thyself."
10148{_}3203{_}But at last he reached the crest of a certain hill, and saw below a sweet little thatched inn lying snugly in the dale beneath him, toward which the road dipped sharply.
10148{_}3204{_}At the sight of this, a voice within him cried aloud, "I give thee joy, good friend, for yonder is thy heart's delight, to wit, a sweet rest and a cup of brown beer."
10148{_}3205{_}So he quickened his pace down the hill and so came to the little inn, from which hung a sign with a stag's head painted upon it.
10148{_}3206{_}In front of the door a clucking hen was scratching in the dust with a brood of chickens about her heels, the sparrows were chattering of household affairs under the eaves, and all was so sweet and peaceful that Little John's heart laughed within him.
10148{_}3207{_}Beside the door stood two stout cobs with broad soft-padded saddles, well fitted for easy traveling, and speaking of rich guests in the parlor.
10148{_}3208{_}In front of the door three merry fellows, a tinker, a peddler, and a beggar, were seated on a bench in the sun quaffing stout ale.
10148{_}3209{_}"I give you good den, sweet friends," quoth Little John, striding up to where they sat.
10148{_}3210{_}"Give thee good den, holy father," quoth the merry Beggar with a grin.
10148{_}3211{_}"But look thee, thy gown is too short.
10148{_}3212{_}Thou hadst best cut a piece off the top and tack it to the bottom, so that it may be long enough.
10148{_}3213{_}But come, sit beside us here and take a taste of ale, if thy vows forbid thee not."
10148{_}3214{_}"Nay," quoth Little John, also grinning, "the blessed Saint Dunstan hath given me a free dispensation for all indulgence in that line."
10148{_}3215{_}And he thrust his hand into his pouch for money to pay his score.
10148{_}3216{_}"Truly," quoth the Tinker, "without thy looks belie thee, holy friar, the good Saint Dunstan was wise, for without such dispensation his votary is like to ha' many a penance to make.
10148{_}3217{_}Nay, take thy hand from out thy pouch, brother, for thou shalt not pay this shot.
10148{_}3218{_}Ho, landlord, a pot of ale!"
10148{_}3219{_}So the ale was brought and given to Little John.
10148{_}3220{_}Then, blowing the froth a little way to make room for his lips, he tilted the bottom of the pot higher and higher, till it pointed to the sky, and he had to shut his eyes to keep the dazzle of the sunshine out of them.
10148{_}3221{_}Then he took the pot away, for there was nothing in it, and heaved a full deep sigh, looking at the others with moist eyes and shaking his head solemnly.
10148{_}3222{_}"Ho, landlord!"
10148{_}3223{_}cried the Peddler, "bring this good fellow another pot of ale, for truly it is a credit to us all to have one among us who can empty a canakin so lustily."
10148{_}3224{_}So they talked among themselves merrily, until after a while quoth Little John, "Who rideth those two nags yonder?"
10148{_}3225{_}"Two holy men like thee, brother," quoth the Beggar.
10148{_}3226{_}"They are now having a goodly feast within, for I smelled the steam of a boiled pullet just now.
10148{_}3227{_}The landlady sayeth they come from Fountain Abbey, in Yorkshire, and go to Lincoln on matters of business."
10148{_}3228{_}"They are a merry couple," said the Tinker, "for one is as lean as an old wife's spindle, and the other as fat as a suet pudding."
10148{_}3229{_}"Talking of fatness," said the Peddler, "thou thyself lookest none too ill-fed, holy friar."
10148{_}3230{_}"Nay, truly," said Little John, "thou seest in me what the holy Saint Dunstan can do for them that serve him upon a handful of parched peas and a trickle of cold water."
10148{_}3231{_}At this a great shout of laughter went up.
10148{_}3232{_}"Truly, it is a wondrous thing," quoth the Beggar, "I would have made my vow, to see the masterly manner in which thou didst tuck away yon pot of ale, that thou hadst not tasted clear water for a brace of months.
10148{_}3233{_}Has not this same holy Saint Dunstan taught thee a goodly song or two?"
10148{_}3234{_}"Why, as for that," quoth Little John, grinning, "mayhap he hath lent me aid to learn a ditty or so."
10148{_}3235{_}"Then, prythee, let us hear how he hath taught thee," quoth the Tinker.
10148{_}3236{_}At this Little John cleared his throat and, after a word or two about a certain hoarseness that troubled him, sang thus:   "_Ah, pretty, pretty maid, whither dost thou go?
10148{_}3237{_}I prythee, prythee, wait for thy lover also,      And we'll gather the rose      As it sweetly blows,  For the merry, merry winds are blo-o-o-wing_."
10148{_}3238{_}Now it seemed as though Little John's songs were never to get sung, for he had got no farther than this when the door of the inn opened and out came the two brothers of Fountain Abbey, the landlord following them, and, as the saying is, washing his hands with humble soap.
10148{_}3239{_}But when the brothers of Fountain Abbey saw who it was that sang, and how he was clad in the robes of a Gray Friar, they stopped suddenly, the fat little Brother drawing his heavy eyebrows together in a mighty frown, and the thin Brother twisting up his face as though he had sour beer in his mouth.
10148{_}3240{_}Then, as Little John gathered his breath for a new verse, "How, now," roared forth the fat Brother, his voice coming from him like loud thunder from a little cloud, "thou naughty fellow, is this a fit place for one in thy garb to tipple and sing profane songs?"
10148{_}3241{_}"Nay," quoth Little John, "sin' I cannot tipple and sing, like Your Worship's reverence, in such a goodly place as Fountain Abbey, I must e'en tipple and sing where I can."
10148{_}3242{_}"Now, out upon thee," cried the tall lean Brother in a harsh voice, "now, out upon thee, that thou shouldst so disgrace thy cloth by this talk and bearing."
10148{_}3243{_}"Marry, come up!"
10148{_}3244{_}quoth Little John.
10148{_}3245{_}"Disgrace, sayest thou?
10148{_}3246{_}Methinks it is more disgrace for one of our garb to wring hard-earned farthings out of the gripe of poor lean peasants.
10148{_}3247{_}It is not so, brother?"
10148{_}3248{_}At this the Tinker and the Peddler and the Beggar nudged one another, and all grinned, and the friars scowled blackly at Little John; but they could think of nothing further to say, so they turned to their horses.
10148{_}3249{_}Then Little John arose of a sudden from the bench where he sat, and ran to where the brothers of Fountain Abbey were mounting.
10148{_}3250{_}Quoth he, "Let me hold your horses' bridles for you.
10148{_}3251{_}Truly, your words have smitten my sinful heart, so that I will abide no longer in this den of evil, but will go forward with you.
10148{_}3252{_}No vile temptation, I wot, will fall upon me in such holy company."
10148{_}3253{_}"Nay, fellow," said the lean Brother harshly, for he saw that Little John made sport of them, "we want none of thy company, so get thee gone."
10148{_}3254{_}"Alas," quoth Little John, "I am truly sorry that ye like me not nor my company, but as for leaving you, it may not be, for my heart is so moved, that, willy-nilly, I must go with you for the sake of your holy company."
10148{_}3255{_}Now, at this talk all the good fellows on the bench grinned till their teeth glistened, and even the landlord could not forbear to smile.
10148{_}3256{_}As for the friars, they looked at one another with a puzzled look, and knew not what to do in the matter.
10148{_}3257{_}They were so proud that it made them feel sick with shame to think of riding along the highroad with a strolling friar, in robes all too short for him, running beside them, but yet they could not make Little John stay against his will, for they knew he could crack the bones of both of them in a twinkling were he so minded.
10148{_}3258{_}Then up spake the fat Brother more mildly than he had done before.
10148{_}3259{_}"Nay, good brother," said he, "we will ride fast, and thou wilt tire to death at the pace."
10148{_}3260{_}"Truly, I am grateful to thee for the thought of me," quoth Little John, "but have no fear, brother; my limbs are stout, and I could run like a hare from here to Gainsborough."
10148{_}3261{_}At these words a sound of laughing came from the bench, whereat the lean Brother's wrath boiled over, like water into the fire, with great fuss and noise.
10148{_}3262{_}"Now, out upon thee, thou naughty fellow!"
10148{_}3263{_}he cried.
10148{_}3264{_}"Art thou not ashamed to bring disgrace so upon our cloth?
10148{_}3265{_}Bide thee here, thou sot, with these porkers.
10148{_}3266{_}Thou art no fit company for us."
10148{_}3267{_}"La, ye there now!"
10148{_}3268{_}quoth Little John.
10148{_}3269{_}"Thou hearest, landlord; thou art not fit company for these holy men; go back to thine alehouse.
10148{_}3270{_}Nay, if these most holy brothers of mine do but give me the word, I'll beat thy head with this stout staff till it is as soft as whipped eggs."
10148{_}3271{_}At these words a great shout of laughter went up from those on the bench, and the landlord's face grew red as a cherry from smothering his laugh in his stomach; but he kept his merriment down, for he wished not to bring the ill-will of the brothers of Fountain Abbey upon him by unseemly mirth.
10148{_}3272{_}So the two brethren, as they could do nought else, having mounted their nags, turned their noses toward Lincoln and rode away.
10148{_}3273{_}"I cannot stay longer, sweet friends," quoth Little John, as he pushed in betwixt the two cobs, "therefore I wish you good den.
10148{_}3274{_}Off we go, we three."
10148{_}3275{_}So saying, he swung his stout staff over his shoulder and trudged off, measuring his pace with that of the two nags.
10148{_}3276{_}The two brothers glowered at Little John when he so pushed himself betwixt them, then they drew as far away from him as they could, so that the yeoman walked in the middle of the road, while they rode on the footpath on either side of the way.
10148{_}3277{_}As they so went away, the Tinker, the Peddler, and the Beggar ran skipping out into the middle of the highway, each with a pot in his hand, and looked after them laughing.
10148{_}3278{_}While they were in sight of those at the inn, the brothers walked their horses soberly, not caring to make ill matters worse by seeming to run away from Little John, for they could not but think how it would sound in folks' ears when they heard how the brethren of Fountain Abbey scampered away from a strolling friar, like the Ugly One, when the blessed Saint Dunstan loosed his nose from the red-hot tongs where he had held it fast; but when they had crossed the crest of the hill and the inn was lost to sight, quoth the fat Brother to the thin Brother, "Brother Ambrose, had we not better mend our pace?"
10148{_}3279{_}"Why truly, gossip," spoke up Little John, "methinks it would be well to boil our pot a little faster, for the day is passing on.
10148{_}3280{_}So it will not jolt thy fat too much, onward, say I."
10148{_}3281{_}At this the two friars said nothing, but they glared again on Little John with baleful looks; then, without another word, they clucked to their horses, and both broke into a canter.
10148{_}3282{_}So they galloped for a mile and more, and Little John ran betwixt them as lightly as a stag and never turned a hair with the running.
10148{_}3283{_}At last the fat Brother drew his horse's rein with a groan, for he could stand the shaking no longer.
10148{_}3284{_}"Alas," said Little John, with not so much as a catch in his breath, "I did sadly fear that the roughness of this pace would shake thy poor old fat paunch."
10148{_}3285{_}To this the fat Friar said never a word, but he stared straight before him, and he gnawed his nether lip.
10148{_}3286{_}And now they traveled forward more quietly, Little John in the middle of the road whistling merrily to himself, and the two friars in the footpath on either side saying never a word.
10148{_}3287{_}Then presently they met three merry minstrels, all clad in red, who stared amain to see a Gray Friar with such short robes walking in the middle of the road, and two brothers with heads bowed with shame, riding upon richly caparisoned cobs on the footpaths.
10148{_}3288{_}When they had come near to the minstrels, Little John waved his staff like an usher clearing the way.
10148{_}3289{_}"Make way!"
10148{_}3290{_}he cried in a loud voice.
10148{_}3291{_}"Make way!
10148{_}3292{_}make way!
10148{_}3293{_}For here we go, we three!"
10148{_}3294{_}Then how the minstrels stared, and how they laughed!
10148{_}3295{_}But the fat Friar shook as with an ague, and the lean Friar bowed his head over his horse's neck.
10148{_}3296{_}Then next they met two noble knights in rich array, with hawk on wrist, and likewise two fair ladies clad in silks and velvets, all a-riding on noble steeds.
10148{_}3297{_}These all made room, staring, as Little John and the two friars came along the road.
10148{_}3298{_}To them Little John bowed humbly.
10148{_}3299{_}"Give you greetings, lords and ladies," said he.
10148{_}3300{_}"But here we go, we three."
10148{_}3301{_}Then all laughed, and one of the fair ladies cried out, "What three meanest thou, merry friend?"
10148{_}3302{_}Little John looked over his shoulder, for they had now passed each other, and he called back, "Big Jack, lean Jack and fat Jack-pudding."
10148{_}3303{_}At this the fat Friar gave a groan and seemed as if he were like to fall from his saddle for shame; the other brother said nothing, but he looked before him with a grim and stony look.
10148{_}3304{_}Just ahead of them the road took a sudden turn around a high hedge, and some twoscore paces beyond the bend another road crossed the one they were riding upon.
10148{_}3305{_}When they had come to the crossroad and were well away from those they had left, the lean Friar drew rein suddenly.
10148{_}3306{_}"Look ye, fellow," quoth he in a voice quivering with rage, "we have had enough of thy vile company, and care no longer to be made sport of.
10148{_}3307{_}Go thy way, and let us go ours in peace."
10148{_}3308{_}"La there, now!"
10148{_}3309{_}quoth Little John.
10148{_}3310{_}"Methought we were such a merry company, and here thou dost blaze up like fat in the pan.
10148{_}3311{_}But truly, I ha' had enow of you today, though I can ill spare your company.
10148{_}3312{_}I know ye will miss me, but gin ye want me again, whisper to Goodman Wind, and he will bring news thereof to me.
10148{_}3313{_}But ye see I am a poor man and ye are rich.
10148{_}3314{_}I pray you give me a penny or two to buy me bread and cheese at the next inn."
10148{_}3315{_}"We have no money, fellow," said the lean Friar harshly.
10148{_}3316{_}"Come, Brother Thomas, let us forward."
10148{_}3317{_}But Little John caught the horses by the bridle reins, one in either hand.
10148{_}3318{_}"Ha' ye in truth no money about you whatsoever?"
10148{_}3319{_}said he.
10148{_}3320{_}"Now, I pray you, brothers, for charity's sake, give me somewhat to buy a crust of bread, e'en though it be only a penny."
10148{_}3321{_}"I tell thee, fellow, we have no money," thundered the fat little Friar with the great voice.
10148{_}3322{_}"Ha' ye, in holy truth, no money?"
10148{_}3323{_}asked Little John.
10148{_}3324{_}"Not a farthing," said the lean Friar sourly.
10148{_}3325{_}"Not a groat," said the fat Friar loudly.
10148{_}3326{_}"Nay," quoth Little John, "this must not be.
10148{_}3327{_}Far be it from me to see such holy men as ye are depart from me with no money.
10148{_}3328{_}Get both of you down straightway from off your horses, and we will kneel here in the middle of the crossroads and pray the blessed Saint Dunstan to send us some money to carry us on our journey."
10148{_}3329{_}"What sayest thou, thou limb of evil!"
10148{_}3330{_}cried the lean Friar, fairly gnashing his teeth with rage.
10148{_}3331{_}"Doss thou bid me, the high cellarer of Fountain Abbey, to get down from my horse and kneel in the dirty road to pray to some beggarly Saxon saint?"
10148{_}3332{_}"Now," quoth Little John, "I ha' a great part of a mind to crack thy head for thee for speaking thus of the good Saint Dunstan!
10148{_}3333{_}But get down straightway, for my patience will not last much longer, and I may forget that ye are both in holy orders."
10148{_}3334{_}So saying, he twirled his stout staff till it whistled again.
10148{_}3335{_}At this speech both friars grew as pale as dough.
10148{_}3336{_}Down slipped the fat Brother from off his horse on one side, and down slipped the lean Brother on the other.
10148{_}3337{_}"Now, brothers, down on your knees and pray," said Little John; thereupon, putting his heavy hands upon the shoulder of each, he forced them to their knees, he kneeling also.
10148{_}3338{_}Then Little John began to beseech Saint Dunstan for money, which he did in a great loud voice.
10148{_}3339{_}After he had so besought the Saint for a time, he bade the friars feel in their pouches and see if the Saint had sent them anything; so each put his hand slowly in the pouch that hung beside him, but brought nothing thence.
10148{_}3340{_}"Ha!"
10148{_}3341{_}quoth Little John, "have your prayers so little virtue?
10148{_}3342{_}Then let us at it again."
10148{_}3343{_}Then straightway he began calling on Saint Dunstan again, somewhat in this wise:  "O gracious Saint Dunstan!
10148{_}3344{_}Send some money straightway to these poor folk, lest the fat one waste away and grow as lean as the lean one, and the lean one waste away to nothing at all, ere they get to Lincoln Town; but send them only ten shillings apiece, lest they grow puffed up with pride, Any more than that that thou sendest, send to me.
10148{_}3345{_}"Now," quoth he, rising, "let us see what each man hath."
10148{_}3346{_}Then he thrust his hand into his pouch and drew thence four golden angels.
10148{_}3347{_}"What have ye, brothers?"
10148{_}3348{_}said he.
10148{_}3349{_}Then once again each friar slowly thrust his hand into his pouch, and once again brought it out with nothing in it.
10148{_}3350{_}"Have ye nothing?"
10148{_}3351{_}quoth Little John.
10148{_}3352{_}"Nay, I warrant there is somewhat that hath crept into the seams of your pouches, and so ye ha' missed it.
10148{_}3353{_}Let me look."
10148{_}3354{_}So he went first to the lean Friar, and, thrusting his hand into the pouch, he drew forth a leathern bag and counted therefrom one hundred and ten pounds of golden money.
10148{_}3355{_}"I thought," quoth Little John, "that thou hadst missed, in some odd corner of thy pouch, the money that the blessed Saint had sent thee.
10148{_}3356{_}And now let me see whether thou hast not some, also, brother."
10148{_}3357{_}Thereupon he thrust his hand into the pouch of the fat Friar and drew thence a bag like the other and counted out from it threescore and ten pounds.
10148{_}3358{_}"Look ye now," quoth he, "I knew the good Saint had sent thee some pittance that thou, also, hadst missed."
10148{_}3359{_}Then, giving them one pound between them, he slipped the rest of the money into his own pouch, saying, "Ye pledged me your holy word that ye had no money.
10148{_}3360{_}Being holy men, I trust that ye would not belie your word so pledged, therefore I know the good Saint Dunstan hath sent this in answer to my prayers.
10148{_}3361{_}But as I only prayed for ten shillings to be sent to each of you, all over and above that belongeth by rights to me, and so I take it.
10148{_}3362{_}I give you good den, brothers, and may ye have a pleasant journey henceforth."
10148{_}3363{_}So saying, he turned and left them, striding away.
10148{_}3364{_}The friars looked at one another with a woeful look, and slowly and sadly they mounted their horses again and rode away with never a word.
10148{_}3365{_}But Little John turned his footsteps back again to Sherwood Forest, and merrily he whistled as he strode along.
10148{_}3366{_}And now we will see what befell Robin Hood in his venture as beggar.
10148{_}3367{_}Robin Hood Turns Beggar  AFTER JOLLY ROBIN had left Little John at the forking of the roads, he walked merrily onward in the mellow sunshine that shone about him.
10148{_}3368{_}Ever and anon he would skip and leap or sing a snatch of song, for pure joyousness of the day; for, because of the sweetness of the springtide, his heart was as lusty within him as that of a colt newly turned out to grass.
10148{_}3369{_}Sometimes he would walk a long distance, gazing aloft at the great white swelling clouds that moved slowly across the deep blue sky; anon he would stop and drink in the fullness of life of all things, for the hedgerows were budding tenderly and the grass of the meadows was waxing long and green; again he would stand still and listen to the pretty song of the little birds in the thickets or hearken to the clear crow of the cock daring the sky to rain, whereat he would laugh, for it took but little to tickle Robin's heart into merriment.
10148{_}3370{_}So he trudged manfully along, ever willing to stop for this reason or for that, and ever ready to chat with such merry lasses as he met now and then.
10148{_}3371{_}So the morning slipped along, but yet he met no beggar with whom he could change clothes.
10148{_}3372{_}Quoth he, "If I do not change my luck in haste, I am like to have an empty day of it, for it is well nigh half gone already, and, although I have had a merry walk through the countryside, I know nought of a beggar's life."
10148{_}3373{_}Then, after a while, he began to grow hungry, whereupon his mind turned from thoughts of springtime and flowers and birds and dwelled upon boiled capons, Malmsey, white bread, and the like, with great tenderness.
10148{_}3374{_}Quoth he to himself, "I would I had Willie Wynkin's wishing coat; I know right well what I should wish for, and this it should be."
10148{_}3375{_}Here he marked upon the fingers of his left hand with the forefinger of his right hand those things which he wished for.
10148{_}3376{_}"Firstly, I would have a sweet brown pie of tender larks; mark ye, not dry cooked, but with a good sop of gravy to moisten it withal.
10148{_}3377{_}Next, I would have a pretty pullet, fairly boiled, with tender pigeons' eggs, cunningly sliced, garnishing the platter around.
10148{_}3378{_}With these I would have a long, slim loaf of wheaten bread that hath been baked upon the hearth; it should be warm from the fire, with glossy brown crust, the color of the hair of mine own Maid Marian, and this same crust should be as crisp and brittle as the thin white ice that lies across the furrows in the early winter's morning.
10148{_}3379{_}These will do for the more solid things; but with these I must have three potties, fat and round, one full of Malmsey, one of Canary, and one brimming full of mine own dear lusty sack."
10148{_}3380{_}Thus spoke Robin to himself, his mouth growing moist at the corners with the thoughts of the good things he had raised in his own mind.
10148{_}3381{_}So, talking to himself, he came to where the dusty road turned sharply around the hedge, all tender with the green of the coming leaf, and there he saw before him a stout fellow sitting upon a stile, swinging his legs in idleness.
10148{_}3382{_}All about this lusty rogue dangled divers pouches and bags of different sizes and kinds, a dozen or more, with great, wide, gaping mouths, like a brood of hungry daws.
10148{_}3383{_}His coat was gathered in at his waist, and was patched with as many colors as there are stripes upon a Maypole in the springtide.
10148{_}3384{_}On his head he wore a great tall leathern cap, and across his knees rested a stout quarterstaff of blackthorn, full as long and heavy as Robin's.
10148{_}3385{_}As jolly a beggar was he as ever trod the lanes and byways of Nottinghamshire, for his eyes were as gray as slate, and snapped and twinkled and danced with merriment, and his black hair curled close all over his head in little rings of kinkiness.
10148{_}3386{_}"Halloa, good fellow," quoth Robin, when he had come nigh to the other, "what art thou doing here this merry day, when the flowers are peeping and the buds are swelling?"
10148{_}3387{_}Then the other winked one eye and straightway trolled forth in a merry voice:       "_I sit upon the stile,      And I sing a little while  As I wait for my own true dear, O,      For the sun is shining bright,      And the leaves are dancing light,  And the little fowl sings she is near, O_.
10148{_}3388{_}"And so it is with me, bully boy, saving that my doxy cometh not."
10148{_}3389{_}"Now that is a right sweet song," quoth Robin, "and, were I in the right mind to listen to thee, I could bear well to hear more; but I have two things of seriousness to ask of thee; so listen, I prythee."
10148{_}3390{_}At this the jolly Beggar cocked his head on one side, like a rogue of a magpie.
10148{_}3391{_}Quoth he, "I am an ill jug to pour heavy things into, good friend, and, if I mistake not, thou hast few serious words to spare at any time."
10148{_}3392{_}"Nay," quoth jolly Robin, "what I would say first is the most serious of all thoughts to me, to wit, 'Where shall I get somewhat to eat and drink?'"
10148{_}3393{_}"Sayst thou so?"
10148{_}3394{_}quoth the Beggar.
10148{_}3395{_}"Marry, I make no such serious thoughts upon the matter.
10148{_}3396{_}I eat when I can get it, and munch my crust when I can get no crumb; likewise, when there is no ale to be had I wash the dust from out my throat with a trickle of cold water.
10148{_}3397{_}I was sitting here, as thou camest upon me, bethinking myself whether I should break my fast or no.
10148{_}3398{_}I do love to let my hunger grow mightily keen ere I eat, for then a dry crust is as good to me as a venison pasty with suet and raisins is to stout King Harry.
10148{_}3399{_}I have a sharp hunger upon me now, but methinks in a short while it will ripen to a right mellow appetite."
10148{_}3400{_}"Now, in good sooth," quoth merry Robin, laughing, "thou hast a quaint tongue betwixt thy teeth.
10148{_}3401{_}But hast thou truly nought but a dry crust about thee?
10148{_}3402{_}Methinks thy bags and pouches are fat and lusty for such thin fare."
10148{_}3403{_}"Why, mayhap there is some other cold fare therein," said the Beggar slyly.
10148{_}3404{_}"And hast thou nought to drink but cold water?"
10148{_}3405{_}said Robin.
10148{_}3406{_}"Never so much as a drop," quoth the Beggar.
10148{_}3407{_}"Over beyond yon clump of trees is as sweet a little inn as ever thou hast lifted eyelid upon; but I go not thither, for they have a nasty way with me.
10148{_}3408{_}Once, when the good Prior of Emmet was dining there, the landlady set a dear little tart of stewed crabs and barley sugar upon the window sill to cool, and, seeing it there, and fearing it might be lost, I took it with me till that I could find the owner thereof.
10148{_}3409{_}Ever since then they have acted very ill toward me; yet truth bids me say that they have the best ale there that ever rolled over my tongue."
10148{_}3410{_}At this Robin laughed aloud.
10148{_}3411{_}"Marry," quoth he, "they did ill toward thee for thy kindness.
10148{_}3412{_}But tell me truly, what hast thou in thy pouches?"
10148{_}3413{_}"Why," quoth the Beggar, peeping into the mouths of his bags, "I find here a goodly piece of pigeon pie, wrapped in a cabbage leaf to hold the gravy.
10148{_}3414{_}Here I behold a dainty streaked piece of brawn, and here a fair lump of white bread.
10148{_}3415{_}Here I find four oaten cakes and a cold knuckle of ham.
10148{_}3416{_}Ha!
10148{_}3417{_}In sooth, 'tis strange; but here I behold six eggs that must have come by accident from some poultry yard hereabouts.
10148{_}3418{_}They are raw, but roasted upon the coals and spread with a piece of butter that I see--"  "Peace, good friend!"
10148{_}3419{_}cried Robin, holding up his hand.
10148{_}3420{_}"Thou makest my poor stomach quake with joy for what thou tellest me so sweetly.
10148{_}3421{_}If thou wilt give me to eat, I will straightway hie me to that little inn thou didst tell of but now, and will bring a skin of ale for thy drinking and mine."
10148{_}3422{_}"Friend, thou hast said enough," said the Beggar, getting down from the stile.
10148{_}3423{_}"I will feast thee with the best that I have and bless Saint Cedric for thy company.
10148{_}3424{_}But, sweet chuck, I prythee bring three quarts of ale at least, one for thy drinking and two for mine, for my thirst is such that methinks I can drink ale as the sands of the River Dee drink salt water."
10148{_}3425{_}So Robin straightway left the Beggar, who, upon his part, went to a budding lime bush back of the hedge, and there spread his feast upon the grass and roasted his eggs upon a little fagot fire, with a deftness gained by long labor in that line.
10148{_}3426{_}After a while back came Robin bearing a goodly skin of ale upon his shoulder, which he laid upon the grass.
10148{_}3427{_}Then, looking upon the feast spread upon the ground--and a fair sight it was to look upon--he slowly rubbed his hand over his stomach, for to his hungry eyes it seemed the fairest sight that he had beheld in all his life.
10148{_}3428{_}"Friend," said the Beggar, "let me feel the weight of that skin.
10148{_}3429{_}"Yea, truly," quoth Robin, "help thyself, sweet chuck, and meantime let me see whether thy pigeon pie is fresh or no."
10148{_}3430{_}So the one seized upon the ale and the other upon the pigeon pie, and nothing was heard for a while but the munching of food and the gurgle of ale as it left the skin.
10148{_}3431{_}At last, after a long time had passed thus, Robin pushed the food from him and heaved a great sigh of deep content, for he felt as though he had been made all over anew.
10148{_}3432{_}"And now, good friend," quoth he, leaning upon one elbow, "I would have at thee about that other matter of seriousness of which I spoke not long since."
10148{_}3433{_}"How!"
10148{_}3434{_}said the Beggar reproachfully, "thou wouldst surely not talk of things appertaining to serious affairs upon such ale as this!"
10148{_}3435{_}"Nay," quoth Robin, laughing.
10148{_}3436{_}"I would not check thy thirst, sweet friend; drink while I talk to thee.
10148{_}3437{_}Thus it is: I would have thee know that I have taken a liking to thy craft and would fain have a taste of a beggar's life mine own self."
10148{_}3438{_}Said the Beggar, "I marvel not that thou hast taken a liking to my manner of life, good fellow, but 'to like' and 'to do' are two matters of different sorts.
10148{_}3439{_}I tell thee, friend, one must serve a long apprenticeship ere one can learn to be even so much as a clapper- dudgeon, much less a crank or an Abraham-man.
10148{_}3440{_}[3] I tell thee, lad, thou art too old to enter upon that which it may take thee years to catch the hang of."
10148{_}3441{_}[3] Classes of traveling mendicants that infested England as late as the   middle of the seventeenth century.
10148{_}3442{_}VIDE Dakkar's ENGLISH VILLAINIES,   etc.
10148{_}3443{_}"Mayhap that may be so," quoth Robin, "for I bring to mind that Gaffer Swanthold sayeth Jack Shoemaker maketh ill bread; Tom Baker maketh ill shoon.
10148{_}3444{_}Nevertheless, I have a mind to taste a beggar's life, and need but the clothing to be as good as any."
10148{_}3445{_}"I tell thee, fellow," said the Beggar, "if thou wert clad as sweetly as good Saint Wynten, the patron of our craft, thou wouldst never make a beggar.
10148{_}3446{_}Marry, the first jolly traveler that thou wouldst meet would beat thee to a pudding for thrusting thy nose into a craft that belongeth not to thee."
10148{_}3447{_}"Nevertheless," quoth Robin, "I would have a try at it; and methinks I shall change clothes with thee, for thy garb seemeth to be pretty, not to say gay.
10148{_}3448{_}So not only will I change clothes, but I will give thee two golden angels to boot.
10148{_}3449{_}I have brought my stout staff with me, thinking that I might have to rap some one of the brethren of thy cloth over the head by way of argument in this matter, but I love thee so much for the feast thou hast given me that I would not lift even my little finger against thee, so thou needst not have a crumb of fear."
10148{_}3450{_}To this the Beggar listened with his knuckles resting against his hips, and when Robin had ended he cocked his head on one side and thrust his tongue into his cheek.
10148{_}3451{_}"Marry, come up," quoth he at last.
10148{_}3452{_}"Lift thy finger against me, forsooth!
10148{_}3453{_}Art thou out of thy wits, man?
10148{_}3454{_}My name is Riccon Hazel, and I come from Holywell, in Flintshire, over by the River Dee.
10148{_}3455{_}I tell thee, knave, I have cracked the head of many a better man than thou art, and even now I would scald thy crown for thee but for the ale thou hast given me.
10148{_}3456{_}Now thou shalt not have so much as one tag-rag of my coat, even could it save thee from hanging."
10148{_}3457{_}"Now, fellow," said Robin, "it would ill suit me to spoil thy pretty head for thee, but I tell thee plainly, that but for this feast I would do that to thee would stop thy traveling the country for many a day to come.
10148{_}3458{_}Keep thy lips shut, lad, or thy luck will tumble out of thy mouth with thy speech!"
10148{_}3459{_}"Now out, and alas for thee, man, for thou hast bred thyself ill this day!"
10148{_}3460{_}cried the Beggar, rising and taking up his staff.
10148{_}3461{_}"Take up thy club and defend thyself, fellow, for I will not only beat thee but I will take from thee thy money and leave thee not so much as a clipped groat to buy thyself a lump of goose grease to rub thy cracked crown withal.
10148{_}3462{_}So defend thyself, I say."
10148{_}3463{_}Then up leaped merry Robin and snatched up his staff also.
10148{_}3464{_}"Take my money, if thou canst," quoth he.
10148{_}3465{_}"I promise freely to give thee every farthing if thou dost touch me."
10148{_}3466{_}And he twirled his staff in his fingers till it whistled again.
10148{_}3467{_}Then the Beggar swung his staff also, and struck a mighty blow at Robin, which the yeoman turned.
10148{_}3468{_}Three blows the Beggar struck, yet never one touched so much as a hair of Robin's head.
10148{_}3469{_}Then stout Robin saw his chance, and, ere you could count three, Riccon's staff was over the hedge, and Riccon himself lay upon the green grass with no more motion than you could find in an empty pudding bag.
10148{_}3470{_}"How now!"
10148{_}3471{_}quoth merry Robin, laughing.
10148{_}3472{_}"Wilt thou have my hide or my money, sweet chuck?"
10148{_}3473{_}But to this the other answered never a word.
10148{_}3474{_}Then Robin, seeing his plight, and that he was stunned with the blow, ran, still laughing, and brought the skin of ale and poured some of it on the Beggar's head and some down his throat, so that presently he opened his eyes and looked around as though wondering why he lay upon his back.
10148{_}3475{_}Then Robin, seeing that he had somewhat gathered the wits that had just been rapped out of his head, said, "Now, good fellow, wilt thou change clothes with me, or shall I have to tap thee again?
10148{_}3476{_}Here are two golden angels if thou wilt give me freely all thy rags and bags and thy cap and things.
10148{_}3477{_}If thou givest them not freely, I much fear me I shall have to-- " and he looked up and down his staff.
10148{_}3478{_}Then Riccon sat up and rubbed the bump on his crown.
10148{_}3479{_}"Now, out upon it!"
10148{_}3480{_}quoth he.
10148{_}3481{_}"I did think to drub thee sweetly, fellow.
10148{_}3482{_}I know not how it is, but I seem, as it were, to have bought more beer than I can drink.
10148{_}3483{_}If I must give up my clothes, I must, but first promise me, by thy word as a true yeoman, that thou wilt take nought from me but my clothes."
10148{_}3484{_}"I promise on the word of a true yeoman," quoth Robin, thinking that the fellow had a few pennies that he would save.
10148{_}3485{_}Thereupon the Beggar drew a little knife that hung at his side and, ripping up the lining of his coat, drew thence ten bright golden pounds, which he laid upon the ground beside him with a cunning wink at Robin.
10148{_}3486{_}"Now thou mayst have my clothes and welcome," said he, "and thou mightest have had them in exchange for thine without the cost of a single farthing, far less two golden angels."
10148{_}3487{_}"Marry," quoth Robin, laughing, "thou art a sly fellow, and I tell thee truly, had I known thou hadst so much money by thee maybe thou mightst not have carried it away, for I warrant thou didst not come honestly by it."
10148{_}3488{_}Then each stripped off his clothes and put on those of the other, and as lusty a beggar was Robin Hood as e'er you could find of a summer's day.
10148{_}3489{_}But stout Riccon of Holywell skipped and leaped and danced for joy of the fair suit of Lincoln green that he had so gotten.
10148{_}3490{_}Quoth he, "I am a gay-feathered bird now.
10148{_}3491{_}Truly, my dear Moll Peascod would never know me in this dress.
10148{_}3492{_}Thou mayst keep the cold pieces of the feast, friend, for I mean to live well and lustily while my money lasts and my clothes are gay."
10148{_}3493{_}So he turned and left Robin and, crossing the stile, was gone, but Robin heard him singing from beyond the hedge as he strode away:   "_For Polly is smiling and Molly is glad      When the beggar comes in at the door,  And Jack and Dick call him a fine lusty lad,      And the hostess runs up a great score.
10148{_}3494{_}Then hey, Willy Waddykin,      Stay, Billy Waddykin,  And let the brown ale flow free, flow free,      The beggar's the man for me_."
10148{_}3495{_}Robin listened till the song ended in the distance, then he also crossed the stile into the road, but turned his toes away from where the Beggar had gone.
10148{_}3496{_}The road led up a gentle hill and up the hill Robin walked, a half score or more of bags dangling about his legs.
10148{_}3497{_}Onward he strolled for a long time, but other adventure he found not.
10148{_}3498{_}The road was bare of all else but himself, as he went kicking up little clouds of dust at each footstep; for it was noontide, the most peaceful time of all the day, next to twilight.
10148{_}3499{_}All the earth was silent in the restfulness of eating time; the plowhorses stood in the furrow munching, with great bags over their noses holding sweet food, the plowman sat under the hedge and the plowboy also, and they, too, were munching, each one holding a great piece of bread in one fist and a great piece of cheese in the other.
10148{_}3500{_}So Robin, with all the empty road to himself, strode along whistling merrily, his bags and pouches bobbing and dangling at his thighs.
10148{_}3501{_}At last he came to where a little grass-grown path left the road and, passing through a stile and down a hill, led into a little dell and on across a rill in the valley and up the hill on the other side, till it reached a windmill that stood on the cap of the rise where the wind bent the trees in swaying motion.
10148{_}3502{_}Robin looked at the spot and liked it, and, for no reason but that his fancy led him, he took the little path and walked down the grassy sunny slope of the open meadow, and so came to the little dingle and, ere he knew it, upon four lusty fellows that sat with legs outstretched around a goodly feast spread upon the ground.
10148{_}3503{_}Four merry beggars were they, and each had slung about his neck a little board that rested upon his breast.
10148{_}3504{_}One board had written upon it, "I am blind," another, "I am deaf," another, "I am dumb," and the fourth, "Pity the lame one."
10148{_}3505{_}But although all these troubles written upon the boards seemed so grievous, the four stout fellows sat around feasting as merrily as though Cain's wife had never opened the pottle that held misfortunes and let them forth like a cloud of flies to pester us.
10148{_}3506{_}The deaf man was the first to hear Robin, for he said, "Hark, brothers, I hear someone coming."
10148{_}3507{_}And the blind man was the first to see him, for he said, "He is an honest man, brothers, and one of like craft to ourselves."
10148{_}3508{_}Then the dumb man called to him in a great voice and said, "Welcome, brother; come and sit while there is still some of the feast left and a little Malmsey in the pottle."
10148{_}3509{_}At this, the lame man, who had taken off his wooden leg and unstrapped his own leg, and was sitting with it stretched out upon the grass so as to rest it, made room for Robin among them.
10148{_}3510{_}"We are glad to see thee, brother," said he, holding out the flask of Malmsey.
10148{_}3511{_}"Marry," quoth Robin, laughing, and weighing the flask in his hands ere he drank, "methinks it is no more than seemly of you all to be glad to see me, seeing that I bring sight to the blind, speech to the dumb, hearing to the deaf, and such a lusty leg to a lame man.
10148{_}3512{_}I drink to your happiness, brothers, as I may not drink to your health, seeing ye are already hale, wind and limb."
10148{_}3513{_}At this all grinned, and the Blind beggar, who was the chief man among them, and was the broadest shouldered and most lusty rascal of all, smote Robin upon the shoulder, swearing he was a right merry wag.
10148{_}3514{_}"Whence comest thou, lad?"
10148{_}3515{_}asked the Dumb man.
10148{_}3516{_}"Why," quoth Robin, "I came this morning from sleeping overnight in Sherwood."
10148{_}3517{_}"Is it even so?"
10148{_}3518{_}said the Deaf man.
10148{_}3519{_}"I would not for all the money we four are carrying to Lincoln Town sleep one night in Sherwood.
10148{_}3520{_}If Robin Hood caught one of our trade in his woodlands he would, methinks, clip his ears."
10148{_}3521{_}"Methinks he would, too," quoth Robin, laughing.
10148{_}3522{_}"But what money is this that ye speak of?"
10148{_}3523{_}Then up spake the Lame man.
10148{_}3524{_}"Our king, Peter of York," said he, "hath sent us to Lincoln with those moneys that--"  "Stay, brother Hodge," quoth the Blind man, breaking into the talk, "I would not doubt our brother here, but bear in mind we know him not.
10148{_}3525{_}What art thou, brother?
10148{_}3526{_}Upright-man, Jurkman, Clapper-dudgeon, Dommerer, or Abraham-man?"
10148{_}3527{_}At these words Robin looked from one man to the other with mouth agape.
10148{_}3528{_}"Truly," quoth he, "I trust I am an upright man, at least, I strive to be; but I know not what thou meanest by such jargon, brother.
10148{_}3529{_}It were much more seemly, methinks, if yon Dumb man, who hath a sweet voice, would give us a song."
10148{_}3530{_}At these words a silence fell on all, and after a while the Blind man spoke again.
10148{_}3531{_}Quoth he, "Thou dost surely jest when thou sayest that thou dost not understand such words.
10148{_}3532{_}Answer me this: Hast thou ever fibbed a chouse quarrons in the Rome pad for the loure in his bung?
10148{_}3533{_}"[4]    [4] I.E., in old beggar's cant, "beaten a man or gallant upon the   highway for the money in his purse."
10148{_}3534{_}Dakkar's ENGLISH VILLAINIES.
10148{_}3535{_}"Now out upon it," quoth Robin Hood testily, "an ye make sport of me by pattering such gibberish, it will be ill for you all, I tell you.
10148{_}3536{_}I have the best part of a mind to crack the heads of all four of you, and would do so, too, but for the sweet Malmsey ye have given me.
10148{_}3537{_}Brother, pass the pottle lest it grow cold."
10148{_}3538{_}But all the four beggars leaped to their feet when Robin had done speaking, and the Blind man snatched up a heavy knotted cudgel that lay beside him on the grass, as did the others likewise.
10148{_}3539{_}Then Robin, seeing that things were like to go ill with him, albeit he knew not what all the coil was about, leaped to his feet also and, catching up his trusty staff, clapped his back against the tree and stood upon his guard against them.
10148{_}3540{_}"How, now!"
10148{_}3541{_}cried he, twirling his staff betwixt his fingers, "would you four stout fellows set upon one man?
10148{_}3542{_}Stand back, ye rascals, or I will score your pates till they have as many marks upon them as a pothouse door!
10148{_}3543{_}Are ye mad?
10148{_}3544{_}I have done you no harm."
10148{_}3545{_}"Thou liest!"
10148{_}3546{_}quoth the one who pretended to be blind and who, being the lustiest villain, was the leader of the others, "thou liest!
10148{_}3547{_}For thou hast come among us as a vile spy.
10148{_}3548{_}But thine ears have heard too much for thy body's good, and thou goest not forth from this place unless thou goest feet foremost, for this day thou shalt die!
10148{_}3549{_}Come, brothers, all together!
10148{_}3550{_}Down with him!"
10148{_}3551{_}Then, whirling up his cudgel, he rushed upon Robin as an angry bull rushes upon a red rag.
10148{_}3552{_}But Robin was ready for any happening.
10148{_}3553{_}"Crick!
10148{_}3554{_}Crack!"
10148{_}3555{_}he struck two blows as quick as a wink, and down went the Blind man, rolling over and over upon the grass.
10148{_}3556{_}At this the others bore back and stood at a little distance scowling upon Robin.
10148{_}3557{_}"Come on, ye scum!"
10148{_}3558{_}cried he merrily.
10148{_}3559{_}"Here be cakes and ale for all.
10148{_}3560{_}Now, who will be next served?"
10148{_}3561{_}To this speech the beggars answered never a word, but they looked at Robin as great Blunderbore looked upon stout Jack the slayer of giants, as though they would fain eat him, body and bones; nevertheless, they did not care to come nigher to him and his terrible staff.
10148{_}3562{_}Then, seeing them so hesitate, Robin of a sudden leaped upon them, striking even as he leaped.
10148{_}3563{_}Down went the Dumb man, and away flew his cudgel from his hand as he fell.
10148{_}3564{_}At this the others ducked to avoid another blow, then, taking to their heels, scampered, the one one way and the other the other, as though they had the west wind's boots upon their feet.
10148{_}3565{_}Robin looked after them, laughing, and thought that never had he seen so fleet a runner as the Lame man; but neither of the beggars stopped nor turned around, for each felt in his mind the wind of Robin's cudgel about his ears.
10148{_}3566{_}Then Robin turned to the two stout knaves lying upon the ground.
10148{_}3567{_}Quoth he, "These fellows spake somewhat about certain moneys they were taking to Lincoln; methinks I may find it upon this stout blind fellow, who hath as keen sight as e'er a trained woodsman in Nottingham or Yorkshire.
10148{_}3568{_}It were a pity to let sound money stay in the pockets of such thieving knaves."
10148{_}3569{_}So saying, he stooped over the burly rascal and searched among his rags and tatters, till presently his fingers felt a leathern pouch slung around his body beneath his patched and tattered coat.
10148{_}3570{_}This he stripped away and, weighing it in his hands, bethought himself that it was mighty heavy.
10148{_}3571{_}"It were a sweet thing," said he to himself, "if this were filled with gold instead of copper pence."
10148{_}3572{_}Then, sitting down upon the grass, he opened the pocket and looked into it.
10148{_}3573{_}There he found four round rolls wrapped up in dressed sheepskin; one of these rolls he opened; then his mouth gaped and his eyes stared, I wot, as though they would never close again, for what did he see but fifty pounds of bright golden money?
10148{_}3574{_}He opened the other pockets and found in each one the same, fifty bright new-stamped golden pounds.
10148{_}3575{_}Quoth Robin, "I have oft heard that the Beggars' Guild was over-rich, but never did I think that they sent such sums as this to their treasury.
10148{_}3576{_}I shall take it with me, for it will be better used for charity and the good of my merry band than in the enriching of such knaves as these."
10148{_}3577{_}So saying, he rolled up the money in the sheepskin again, and putting it back in the purse, he thrust the pouch into his own bosom.
10148{_}3578{_}Then taking up the flask of Malmsey, he held it toward the two fellows lying on the grass, and quoth he, "Sweet friends, I drink your health and thank you dearly for what ye have so kindly given me this day, and so I wish you good den."
10148{_}3579{_}Then, taking up his staff, he left the spot and went merrily on his way.
10148{_}3580{_}But when the two stout beggars that had been rapped upon the head roused themselves and sat up, and when the others had gotten over their fright and come back, they were as sad and woebegone as four frogs in dry weather, for two of them had cracked crowns, their Malmsey was all gone, and they had not so much as a farthing to cross their palms withal.
10148{_}3581{_}But after Robin left the little dell he strode along merrily, singing as he went; and so blithe was he and such a stout beggar, and, withal, so fresh and clean, that every merry lass he met had a sweet word for him and felt no fear, while the very dogs, that most times hate the sight of a beggar, snuffed at his legs in friendly wise and wagged their tails pleasantly; for dogs know an honest man by his smell, and an honest man Robin was--in his own way.
10148{_}3582{_}Thus he went along till at last he had come to the wayside cross nigh Ollerton, and, being somewhat tired, he sat him down to rest upon the grassy bank in front of it.
10148{_}3583{_}"It groweth nigh time," quoth he to himself, "that I were getting back again to Sherwood; yet it would please me well to have one more merry adventure ere I go back again to my jolly band."
10148{_}3584{_}So he looked up the road and down the road to see who might come, until at last he saw someone drawing near, riding upon a horse.
10148{_}3585{_}When the traveler came nigh enough for him to see him well, Robin laughed, for a strange enough figure he cut.
10148{_}3586{_}He was a thin, wizened man, and, to look upon him, you could not tell whether he was thirty years old or sixty, so dried up was he even to skin and bone.
10148{_}3587{_}As for the nag, it was as thin as the rider, and both looked as though they had been baked in Mother Huddle's Oven, where folk are dried up so that they live forever.
10148{_}3588{_}But although Robin laughed at the droll sight, he knew the wayfarer to be a certain rich corn engrosser of Worksop, who more than once had bought all the grain in the countryside and held it till it reached even famine prices, thus making much money from the needs of poor people, and for this he was hated far and near by everyone that knew aught of him.
10148{_}3589{_}So, after a while, the Corn Engrosser came riding up to where Robin sat; whereupon merry Robin stepped straightway forth, in all his rags and tatters, his bags and pouches dangling about him, and laid his hand upon the horse's bridle rein, calling upon the other to stop.
10148{_}3590{_}"Who art thou, fellow, that doth dare to stop me thus upon the King's highway?"
10148{_}3591{_}said the lean man, in a dry, sour voice.
10148{_}3592{_}"Pity a poor beggar," quoth Robin.
10148{_}3593{_}"Give me but a farthing to buy me a piece of bread."
10148{_}3594{_}"Now, out upon thee!"
10148{_}3595{_}snarled the other.
10148{_}3596{_}"Such sturdy rogues as thou art are better safe in the prisons or dancing upon nothing, with a hempen collar about the neck, than strolling the highways so freely."
10148{_}3597{_}"Tut," quoth Robin, "how thou talkest!
10148{_}3598{_}Thou and I are brothers, man.
10148{_}3599{_}Do we not both take from the poor people that which they can ill spare?
10148{_}3600{_}Do we not make our livings by doing nought of any good?
10148{_}3601{_}Do we not both live without touching palm to honest work?
10148{_}3602{_}Have we either of us ever rubbed thumbs over honestly gained farthings?
10148{_}3603{_}Go to!
10148{_}3604{_}We are brothers, I say; only thou art rich and I am poor; wherefore, I prythee once more, give me a penny."
10148{_}3605{_}"Doss thou prate so to me, sirrah?"
10148{_}3606{_}cried the Corn Engrosser in a rage.
10148{_}3607{_}"Now I will have thee soundly whipped if ever I catch thee in any town where the law can lay hold of thee!
10148{_}3608{_}As for giving thee a penny, I swear to thee that I have not so much as a single groat in my purse.
10148{_}3609{_}Were Robin Hood himself to take me, he might search me from crown to heel without finding the smallest piece of money upon me.
10148{_}3610{_}I trust I am too sly to travel so nigh to Sherwood with money in my pouch, and that thief at large in the woods."
10148{_}3611{_}Then merry Robin looked up and down, as if to see that there was no one nigh, and then, coming close to the Corn Engrosser, he stood on tiptoe and spake in his ear, "Thinkest thou in sooth that I am a beggar, as I seem to be?
10148{_}3612{_}Look upon me.
10148{_}3613{_}There is not a grain of dirt upon my hands or my face or my body.
10148{_}3614{_}Didst thou ever see a beggar so?
10148{_}3615{_}I tell thee I am as honest a man as thou art.
10148{_}3616{_}Look, friend."
10148{_}3617{_}Here he took the purse of money from his breast and showed to the dazzled eyes of the Corn Engrosser the bright golden pieces.
10148{_}3618{_}"Friend, these rags serve but to hide an honest rich man from the eyes of Robin Hood."
10148{_}3619{_}"Put up thy money, lad," cried the other quickly.
10148{_}3620{_}"Art thou a fool, to trust to beggar's rags to shield thee from Robin Hood?
10148{_}3621{_}If he caught thee, he would strip thee to the skin, for he hates a lusty beggar as he doth a fat priest or those of my kind."
10148{_}3622{_}"Is it indeed so?"
10148{_}3623{_}quoth Robin.
10148{_}3624{_}"Had I known this, mayhap I had not come hereabouts in this garb.
10148{_}3625{_}But I must go forward now, as much depends upon my journeying.
10148{_}3626{_}Where goest thou, friend?"
10148{_}3627{_}"I go to Grantham," said the Corn Engrosser, "but I shall lodge tonight at Newark, if I can get so far upon my way."
10148{_}3628{_}"Why, I myself am on the way to Newark," quoth merry Robin, "so that, as two honest men are better than one in roads beset by such a fellow as this Robin Hood, I will jog along with thee, if thou hast no dislike to my company."
10148{_}3629{_}"Why, as thou art an honest fellow and a rich fellow," said the Corn Engrosser, "I mind not thy company; but, in sooth, I have no great fondness for beggars."
10148{_}3630{_}"Then forward," quoth Robin, "for the day wanes and it will be dark ere we reach Newark."
10148{_}3631{_}So off they went, the lean horse hobbling along as before, and Robin running beside, albeit he was so quaking with laughter within him that he could hardly stand; yet he dared not laugh aloud, lest the Corn Engrosser should suspect something.
10148{_}3632{_}So they traveled along till they reached a hill just on the outskirts of Sherwood.
10148{_}3633{_}Here the lean man checked his lean horse into a walk, for the road was steep, and he wished to save his nag's strength, having far to go ere he reached Newark.
10148{_}3634{_}Then he turned in his saddle and spake to Robin again, for the first time since they had left the cross.
10148{_}3635{_}"Here is thy greatest danger, friend," said he, "for here we are nighest to that vile thief Robin Hood, and the place where he dwells.
10148{_}3636{_}Beyond this we come again to the open honest country, and so are more safe in our journeying."
10148{_}3637{_}"Alas!"
10148{_}3638{_}quoth Robin, "I would that I had as little money by me as thou hast, for this day I fear that Robin Hood will get every groat of my wealth."
10148{_}3639{_}Then the other looked at Robin and winked cunningly.
10148{_}3640{_}Quoth he, "I tell thee, friend, that I have nigh as much by me as thou hast, but it is hidden so that never a knave in Sherwood could find it."
10148{_}3641{_}"Thou dost surely jest," quoth Robin.
10148{_}3642{_}"How could one hide so much as two hundred pounds upon his person?"
10148{_}3643{_}"Now, as thou art so honest a fellow, and, withal, so much younger than I am, I will tell thee that which I have told to no man in all the world before, and thus thou mayst learn never again to do such a foolish thing as to trust to beggar's garb to guard thee against Robin Hood.
10148{_}3644{_}Seest thou these clogs upon my feet?"
10148{_}3645{_}"Yea," quoth Robin, laughing, "truly, they are large enough for any man to see, even were his sight as foggy as that of Peter Patter, who never could see when it was time to go to work."
10148{_}3646{_}"Peace, friend," said the Corn Engrosser, "for this is no matter for jesting.
10148{_}3647{_}The soles of these clogs are not what they seem to be, for each one is a sweet little box; and by twisting the second nail from the toe, the upper of the shoe and part of the sole lifts up like a lid, and in the spaces within are fourscore and ten bright golden pounds in each shoe, all wrapped in hair, to keep them from clinking and so telling tales of themselves."
10148{_}3648{_}When the Corn Engrosser had told this, Robin broke into a roar of laughter and, laying his hands upon the bridle rein, stopped the sad- looking nag.
10148{_}3649{_}"Stay, good friend," quoth he, between bursts of merriment, "thou art the slyest old fox that e'er I saw in all my life!
10148{_}3650{_}--In the soles of his shoon, quotha!--If ever I trust a poor-seeming man again, shave my head and paint it blue!
10148{_}3651{_}A corn factor, a horse jockey, an estate agent, and a jackdaw for cunningness, say I!"
10148{_}3652{_}And he laughed again till he shook in his shoes with mirth.
10148{_}3653{_}All this time the Corn Engrosser had been staring at Robin, his mouth agape with wonder.
10148{_}3654{_}"Art thou mad," quoth he, "to talk in this way, so loud and in such a place?
10148{_}3655{_}Let us forward, and save thy mirth till we are safe and sound at Newark."
10148{_}3656{_}"Nay," quoth Robin, the tears of merriment wet on his cheeks, "on second thoughts I go no farther than here, for I have good friends hereabouts.
10148{_}3657{_}Thou mayst go forward if thou dost list, thou sweet pretty fellow, but thou must go forward barefoot, for I am afraid that thy shoon must be left behind.
10148{_}3658{_}Off with them, friend, for I tell thee I have taken a great fancy to them."
10148{_}3659{_}At these words the corn factor grew pale as a linen napkin.
10148{_}3660{_}"Who art thou that talkest so?"
10148{_}3661{_}said he.
10148{_}3662{_}Then merry Robin laughed again, and quoth he, "Men hereabouts call me Robin Hood; so, sweet friend, thou hadst best do my bidding and give me thy shoes, wherefore hasten, I prythee, or else thou wilt not get to fair Newark Town till after dark."
10148{_}3663{_}At the sound of the name of Robin Hood, the corn factor quaked with fear, so that he had to seize his horse by the mane to save himself from falling off its back.
10148{_}3664{_}Then straightway, and without more words, he stripped off his clogs and let them fall upon the road.
10148{_}3665{_}Robin, still holding the bridle rein, stooped and picked them up.
10148{_}3666{_}Then he said, "Sweet friend, I am used to ask those that I have dealings with to come and feast at Sherwood with me.
10148{_}3667{_}I will not ask thee, because of our pleasant journey together; for I tell thee there be those in Sherwood that would not be so gentle with thee as I have been.
10148{_}3668{_}The name of Corn Engrosser leaves a nasty taste upon the tongue of all honest men.
10148{_}3669{_}Take a fool's advice of me and come no more so nigh to Sherwood, or mayhap some day thou mayst of a sudden find a clothyard shaft betwixt thy ribs.
10148{_}3670{_}So, with this, I give thee good den."
10148{_}3671{_}Hereupon he clapped his hand to the horse's flank and off went nag and rider.
10148{_}3672{_}But the man's face was all bedewed with the sweat of fright, and never again, I wot, was he found so close to Sherwood Forest as he had been this day.
10148{_}3673{_}Robin stood and looked after him, and, when he was fairly gone, turned, laughing, and entered the forest carrying the shoes in his hand.
10148{_}3674{_}That night in sweet Sherwood the red fires glowed brightly in wavering light on tree and bush, and all around sat or lay the stout fellows of the band to hear Robin Hood and Little John tell their adventures.
10148{_}3675{_}All listened closely, and again and again the woods rang with shouts of laughter.
10148{_}3676{_}When all was told, Friar Tuck spoke up.
10148{_}3677{_}"Good master," said he, "thou hast had a pretty time, but still I hold to my saying, that the life of the barefoot friar is the merrier of the two."
10148{_}3678{_}"Nay," quoth Will Stutely, "I hold with our master, that he hath had the pleasanter doings of the two, for he hath had two stout bouts at quarterstaff this day."
10148{_}3679{_}So some of the band held with Robin Hood and some with Little John.
10148{_}3680{_}As for me, I think--But I leave it with you to say for yourselves which you hold with.
10148{_}3681{_}Robin Hood Shoots Before Queen Eleanor  THE HIGHROAD stretched white and dusty in the hot summer afternoon sun, and the trees stood motionless along the roadside.
10148{_}3682{_}All across the meadow lands the hot air danced and quivered, and in the limpid waters of the lowland brook, spanned by a little stone bridge, the fish hung motionless above the yellow gravel, and the dragonfly sat quite still, perched upon the sharp tip of a spike of the rushes, with its wings glistening in the sun.
10148{_}3683{_}Along the road a youth came riding upon a fair milk-white barb, and the folk that he passed stopped and turned and looked after him, for never had so lovely a lad or one so gaily clad been seen in Nottingham before.
10148{_}3684{_}He could not have been more than sixteen years of age, and was as fair as any maiden.
10148{_}3685{_}His long yellow hair flowed behind him as he rode along, all clad in silk and velvet, with jewels flashing and dagger jingling against the pommel of the saddle.
10148{_}3686{_}Thus came the Queen's Page, young Richard Partington, from famous London Town down into Nottinghamshire, upon Her Majesty's bidding, to seek Robin Hood in Sherwood Forest.
10148{_}3687{_}The road was hot and dusty and his journey had been long, for that day he had come all the way from Leicester Town, a good twenty miles and more; wherefore young Partington was right glad when he saw before him a sweet little inn, all shady and cool beneath the trees, in front of the door of which a sign hung pendant, bearing the picture of a blue boar.
10148{_}3688{_}Here he drew rein and called loudly for a pottle of Rhenish wine to be brought him, for stout country ale was too coarse a drink for this young gentleman.
10148{_}3689{_}Five lusty fellows sat upon the bench beneath the pleasant shade of the wide-spreading oak in front of the inn door, drinking ale and beer, and all stared amain at this fair and gallant lad.
10148{_}3690{_}Two of the stoutest of them were clothed in Lincoln green, and a great heavy oaken staff leaned against the gnarled oak tree trunk beside each fellow.
10148{_}3691{_}The landlord came and brought a pottle of wine and a long narrow glass upon a salver, which he held up to the Page as he sat upon his horse.
10148{_}3692{_}Young Partington poured forth the bright yellow wine and holding the glass aloft, cried, "Here is to the health and long happiness of my royal mistress, the noble Queen Eleanor; and may my journey and her desirings soon have end, and I find a certain stout yeoman men call Robin Hood."
10148{_}3693{_}At these words all stared, but presently the two stout yeomen in Lincoln green began whispering together.
10148{_}3694{_}Then one of the two, whom Partington thought to be the tallest and stoutest fellow he had ever beheld, spoke up and said, "What seekest thou of Robin Hood, Sir Page?
10148{_}3695{_}And what does our good Queen Eleanor wish of him?
10148{_}3696{_}I ask this of thee, not foolishly, but with reason, for I know somewhat of this stout yeoman."
10148{_}3697{_}"An thou knowest aught of him, good fellow," said young Partington, "thou wilt do great service to him and great pleasure to our royal Queen by aiding me to find him."
10148{_}3698{_}Then up spake the other yeoman, who was a handsome fellow with sunburned face and nut-brown, curling hair, "Thou hast an honest look, Sir Page, and our Queen is kind and true to all stout yeomen.
10148{_}3699{_}Methinks I and my friend here might safely guide thee to Robin Hood, for we know where he may be found.
10148{_}3700{_}Yet I tell thee plainly, we would not for all merry England have aught of harm befall him."
10148{_}3701{_}"Set thy mind at ease; I bring nought of ill with me," quoth Richard Partington.
10148{_}3702{_}"I bring a kind message to him from our Queen, therefore an ye know where he is to be found, I pray you to guide me thither."
10148{_}3703{_}Then the two yeomen looked at one another again, and the tall man said, "Surely it were safe to do this thing, Will"; whereat the other nodded.
10148{_}3704{_}Thereupon both arose, and the tall yeoman said, "We think thou art true, Sir Page, and meanest no harm, therefore we will guide thee to Robin Hood as thou dost wish."
10148{_}3705{_}Then Partington paid his score, and the yeomen coming forward, they all straightway departed upon their way.
10148{_}3706{_}Under the greenwood tree, in the cool shade that spread all around upon the sward, with flickering lights here and there, Robin Hood and many of his band lay upon the soft green grass, while Allan a Dale sang and played upon his sweetly sounding harp.
10148{_}3707{_}All listened in silence, for young Allan's singing was one of the greatest joys in all the world to them; but as they so listened there came of a sudden the sound of a horse's feet, and presently Little John and Will Stutely came forth from the forest path into the open glade, young Richard Partington riding between them upon his milk-white horse.
10148{_}3708{_}The three came toward where Robin Hood sat, all the band staring with might and main, for never had they seen so gay a sight as this young Page, nor one so richly clad in silks and velvets and gold and jewels.
10148{_}3709{_}Then Robin arose and stepped forth to meet him, and Partington leaped from his horse and doffing his cap of crimson velvet, met Robin as he came.
10148{_}3710{_}"Now, welcome!"
10148{_}3711{_}cried Robin.
10148{_}3712{_}"Now, welcome, fair youth, and tell me, I prythee, what bringeth one of so fair a presence and clad in such noble garb to our poor forest of Sherwood?"
10148{_}3713{_}Then young Partington said, "If I err not, thou art the famous Robin Hood, and these thy stout band of outlawed yeomen.
10148{_}3714{_}To thee I bring greetings from our noble Queen Eleanor.
10148{_}3715{_}Oft hath she heard thee spoken of and thy merry doings hereabouts, and fain would she behold thy face; therefore she bids me tell thee that if thou wilt presently come to London Town, she will do all in her power to guard thee against harm, and will send thee back safe to Sherwood Forest again.
10148{_}3716{_}Four days hence, in Finsbury Fields, our good King Henry, of great renown, holdeth a grand shooting match, and all the most famous archers of merry England will be thereat.
10148{_}3717{_}Our Queen would fain see thee strive with these, knowing that if thou wilt come thou wilt, with little doubt, carry off the prize.
10148{_}3718{_}Therefore she hath sent me with this greeting, and furthermore sends thee, as a sign of great good will, this golden ring from off her own fair thumb, which I give herewith into thy hands."
10148{_}3719{_}Then Robin Hood bowed his head and taking the ring, kissed it right loyally, and then slipped it upon his little finger.
10148{_}3720{_}Quoth he, "Sooner would I lose my life than this ring; and ere it departs from me, my hand shall be cold in death or stricken off at the wrist.
10148{_}3721{_}Fair Sir Page, I will do our Queen's bidding, and will presently hie with thee to London; but, ere we go, I will feast thee here in the woodlands with the very best we have."
10148{_}3722{_}"It may not be," said the Page; "we have no time to tarry, therefore get thyself ready straightway; and if there be any of thy band that thou wouldst take with thee, our Queen bids me say that she will make them right welcome likewise."
10148{_}3723{_}"Truly, thou art right," quoth Robin, "and we have but short time to stay; therefore I will get me ready presently.
